## Module 3: Core Java APIs and Libraries

### 3.1 String Handling

- **String Class**
    
    # **String Class**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`String` class** in Java represents a **sequence of characters** and is one of the most frequently used classes in Java.
    
    It belongs to the **`java.lang`** package and is **immutable**, meaning once a string object is created, its content cannot be changed.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Strings are **objects**, not primitive types.
    - They are used to store and manipulate textual data.
    - Java provides many **built-in methods** for string operations such as concatenation, comparison, substring extraction, searching, and formatting.
    - Because strings are immutable, every modification (like concatenation) creates a **new String object** in memory.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Class Declaration**
    
    ```java
    public final class String implements java.io.Serializable, Comparable<String>, CharSequence
    
    ```
    
    **Key points:**
    
    - `final` → cannot be subclassed.
    - Implements `Comparable` for sorting, and `CharSequence` for character-based access.
    - Thread-safe because of immutability.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Immutability of Strings**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Immutability** means **once a `String` object is created, its value cannot be changed**.
    
    Any modification results in the creation of a **new object**, leaving the original unchanged.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Demo {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            String s1 = "Hello";
            s1.concat(" World");
            System.out.println(s1);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `s1.concat(" World")` creates a new string `"Hello World"`.
    - Since `s1` was not reassigned, it still refers to `"Hello"`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Strings Are Immutable**
    
    1. **Security** – Prevents changes to sensitive data like URLs, file paths, or credentials.
    2. **Caching / String Pooling** – Enables reuse through the **String constant pool**.
    3. **Thread Safety** – Immutable objects can be safely shared across threads.
    4. **Hashcode Caching** – Once computed, the hash code is cached for performance in hash-based collections.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Proof of New Object Creation**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = "Java";
    String s2 = s1.concat(" Rocks");
    System.out.println(s1 == s2); // false
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `s1` and `s2` point to different objects because a new string was created.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. String Pool Concept**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **String Pool** (also called the **String Constant Pool** or **SCP**) is a special memory region inside the **Java Heap** where **string literals** are stored.
    
    When a new string literal is created, the JVM first checks if it already exists in the pool:
    
    - If **exists**, the existing reference is reused.
    - If **not**, a new object is created and added to the pool.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = "Hello";
    String s2 = "Hello";
    System.out.println(s1 == s2);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    true
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Both refer to the **same object** in the String Pool.
    - JVM optimizes memory by reusing literals.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Heap vs. String Pool**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Heap Memory (new)** | **String Pool (literals)** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Creation** | Created explicitly using `new` | Created implicitly using literals |
    | **Location** | Regular Heap area | Special area inside Heap |
    | **Duplicate Storage** | Allowed | Not allowed (reused) |
    | **Example** | `new String("Java")` | `"Java"` |
    | **Comparison (`==`)** | Different references | Same reference reused |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = "Java";
    String s2 = new String("Java");
    System.out.println(s1 == s2); // false
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `"Java"` (literal) goes to the pool.
    - `new String("Java")` creates a new object in the **heap**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. String Creation Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **1. Using String Literals**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = "Hello";
    
    ```
    
    - Stored in **String Pool**.
    - Reused if same literal already exists.
    
    ---
    
    ### **2. Using `new` Keyword**
    
    ```java
    String s2 = new String("Hello");
    
    ```
    
    - Always creates a **new object** in heap memory.
    - Literal `"Hello"` also stored in String Pool (if not already present).
    
    ---
    
    ### **3. Using Character Array**
    
    ```java
    char[] ch = {'J', 'a', 'v', 'a'};
    String s3 = new String(ch);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **4. Using `StringBuilder` or `StringBuffer`**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Code");
    String s4 = sb.toString();
    
    ```
    
    - Converts a mutable builder to an immutable string.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Demonstrating All Creation Types**
    
    ```java
    public class StringCreation {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            String s1 = "Java";                     // String Pool
            String s2 = new String("Java");         // Heap
            char[] ch = {'J', 'a', 'v', 'a'};
            String s3 = new String(ch);             // From char array
            StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Java");
            String s4 = sb.toString();              // From StringBuilder
    
            System.out.println(s1 == s2); // false
            System.out.println(s1.equals(s2)); // true
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    false
    true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. String Interning and `intern()` Method**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **String interning** is a process of storing only **one copy** of each distinct string value in the **String Pool** to save memory and enable faster comparisons.
    
    The `intern()` method ensures that a string **exists in the String Pool** and returns a reference from that pool.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Method Declaration**
    
    ```java
    public String intern()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Behavior**
    
    - If the string is **already in the pool**, `intern()` returns the reference to it.
    - If it is **not in the pool**, it adds the string to the pool and returns that pooled reference.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = new String("Hello");
    String s2 = s1.intern();
    String s3 = "Hello";
    
    System.out.println(s1 == s2); // false
    System.out.println(s2 == s3); // true
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `s1` → Heap object.
    - `s1.intern()` → Returns pooled reference `"Hello"`.
    - `"Hello"` literal → Already in pool, so `s2` and `s3` point to same object.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Adding to the Pool**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = new String("World").intern();
    String s2 = "World";
    System.out.println(s1 == s2); // true
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `intern()` ensures `"World"` is in the pool and both refer to the same instance.
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use `intern()`**
    
    - To **optimize memory** when dealing with **repeated strings** (e.g., caching, large text processing).
    - To ensure **reference equality (`==`)** can be used safely.
    - Commonly used in frameworks that deal with large-scale object mapping or caching.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Note**
    
    - `intern()` should be used **judiciously** — excessive use can **increase String Pool memory usage**.
    - Since **String Pool resides in the heap** (since Java 7), it can grow dynamically, but may still affect GC performance.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Concept** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **String Object** | A printed label — once printed, cannot be altered. |
    | **Immutability** | You can’t edit text on the printed label — you must print a new one. |
    | **String Pool** | A filing cabinet storing only one copy of each label. |
    | **Interning** | Checking the cabinet before printing a new label to avoid duplicates. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Memory Behavior**
    
    | **Operation** | **Memory Location** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `"Java"` | String Pool | Reused if exists |
    | `new String("Java")` | Heap | Creates new object |
    | `intern()` | String Pool | Adds if not present, returns pooled reference |
    | `concat()` | Heap | Creates new string (does not modify original) |
    | `StringBuilder` | Heap (mutable) | Used to build new strings efficiently |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Strings are **immutable**; every modification creates a new object.
    - String literals are stored in the **String Pool** for reuse.
    - Use `intern()` to ensure a string reference from the pool.
    - Use `equals()` for value comparison, not `==`.
    - `StringBuilder` and `StringBuffer` are **mutable alternatives** for string manipulation.
    - Immutability provides **security**, **thread-safety**, and **performance optimizations** through pooling.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Feature** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Class Type** | `final`, immutable, in `java.lang` |
    | **Storage** | Heap + String Pool |
    | **Creation Methods** | Literals, `new`, char arrays, builders |
    | **Mutability** | Immutable |
    | **Interning** | Stores/reuses unique strings in pool |
    | **Key Methods** | `concat()`, `equals()`, `length()`, `substring()`, `intern()` |
    | **Comparison** | Use `equals()` for content, `==` for reference |
    | **Best Practice** | Prefer literals and interning for memory efficiency |
    
    ---
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The **`String` class** in Java is an immutable, memory-efficient, and thread-safe representation of text.
    
    Through features like **the String Pool** and **interning**, Java optimizes both performance and memory usage, while **immutability** ensures reliability and security across applications.
    
- **String Methods**
    
    # **String Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The Java **String class** provides a wide range of **built-in methods** for performing common text-processing operations such as **measuring string length**, **accessing characters**, **searching**, **modifying text**, **splitting**, and **joining**.
    
    These methods help handle strings efficiently and are essential in every Java program.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - All methods of the `String` class work on **immutable** string objects.
    - Any operation that *modifies* a string actually creates a **new String object**.
    - String methods provide functionality for:
        - Measuring and comparing text
        - Searching for substrings
        - Extracting parts of strings
        - Transforming cases
        - Cleaning whitespace
        - Splitting and joining content
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Length and Character Access**
    
    ---
    
    ### **length()**
    
    Returns the total number of characters in a string.
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    int length()
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s = "Java";
    System.out.println(s.length());
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    4
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **charAt()**
    
    Returns the character at a specified index (0-based index).
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    char charAt(int index)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s = "Hello";
    System.out.println(s.charAt(1));
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    e
    
    ```
    
    **Note:**
    
    Throws `StringIndexOutOfBoundsException` for invalid indexes.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Comparison Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **equals()**
    
    Checks if two strings have the **same content**.
    
    ```java
    boolean equals(Object obj)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "Java".equals("Java")  // true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **equalsIgnoreCase()**
    
    Compares strings ignoring letter case.
    
    ```java
    boolean equalsIgnoreCase(String anotherString)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "hello".equalsIgnoreCase("HELLO")  // true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **compareTo()**
    
    Performs **lexicographical comparison** (dictionary order).
    
    Returns:
    
    - `0` → equal
    - `<0` → current string is smaller
    - `>0` → current string is larger
    
    ```java
    int compareTo(String anotherString)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "apple".compareTo("banana"); // negative
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Searching Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **indexOf()**
    
    Finds the **first occurrence** of a character or substring. Returns:
    
    - Index if found
    - `1` if not found
    
    ```java
    int indexOf(char c)
    int indexOf(String str)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "programming".indexOf("g");  // 3
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **lastIndexOf()**
    
    Finds the **last occurrence** of a character or substring.
    
    ```java
    int lastIndexOf(char c)
    int lastIndexOf(String str)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "programming".lastIndexOf("g"); // 10
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **contains()**
    
    Checks if a substring exists in the string.
    
    ```java
    boolean contains(CharSequence s)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "Java Programming".contains("Program");  // true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Substring Extraction**
    
    ---
    
    ### **substring()**
    
    Extracts part of a string.
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    String substring(int beginIndex)
    String substring(int beginIndex, int endIndex) // endIndex is exclusive
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s = "HelloWorld";
    System.out.println(s.substring(0, 5));
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Modification Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **replace()**
    
    Replaces characters or substrings.
    
    ```java
    String replace(char old, char new)
    String replace(CharSequence target, CharSequence replacement)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "java".replace('a', 'o'); // "jovo"
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **toLowerCase()** / **toUpperCase()**
    
    Converts characters into lower or upper case.
    
    ```java
    String toLowerCase()
    String toUpperCase()
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "Hello".toUpperCase(); // "HELLO"
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **trim()**
    
    Removes leading and trailing whitespace.
    
    ```java
    String trim()
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    "   Java   ".trim(); // "Java"
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Splitting and Joining Strings**
    
    ---
    
    ### **split()**
    
    Splits a string based on a **regular expression**, returning a `String[]`.
    
    ```java
    String[] split(String regex)
    String[] split(String regex, int limit)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s = "A,B,C,D";
    String[] arr = s.split(",");
    
    ```
    
    Result:
    
    ```
    ["A", "B", "C", "D"]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **join()**
    
    Joins multiple strings using a **delimiter**.
    
    ```java
    static String join(CharSequence delimiter, CharSequence... elements)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    String result = String.join("-", "2025", "11", "24");
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    2025-11-24
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Operation** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `length()` | Counting letters in a word |
    | `charAt()` | Picking a specific letter from a word |
    | `indexOf()` | Searching for a term in a book |
    | `substring()` | Cutting out a section from a sentence |
    | `replace()` | Editing words in a document |
    | `split()` | Breaking a sentence into words |
    | `join()` | Combining words into a sentence |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - All methods create **new String objects** due to immutability.
    - `split()` uses **regex engine**, so special characters must be escaped.
    - `compareTo()` uses Unicode ordering.
    - `trim()` removes only **ASCII whitespace**, not Unicode whitespace (Java 11 introduced `strip()`).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - Always use **equals()** for content comparison, not `==`.
    - Methods like `toLowerCase()` create new objects — use wisely for performance.
    - `split()` can be expensive for large data due to regex processing.
    - Immutable behavior ensures **thread-safety** and consistent results.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    | **Method Category** | **Key Methods** | **Purpose** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Length & Access | `length()`, `charAt()` | Inspect characters |
    | Comparison | `equals()`, `compareTo()`, `equalsIgnoreCase()` | Compare strings |
    | Searching | `indexOf()`, `lastIndexOf()`, `contains()` | Find characters/substrings |
    | Extraction | `substring()` | Extract parts |
    | Modification | `replace()`, `toLowerCase()`, `trim()` | Transform text |
    | Split/Join | `split()`, `join()` | Break or combine strings |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides rich **String methods** that make textual processing efficient and flexible.
    
    By understanding these operations, developers can manipulate and analyze strings cleanly, safely, and effectively while benefiting from the immutability and performance characteristics of the `String` class.
    
- **StringBuilder and StringBuffer**
    
    # **StringBuilder and StringBuffer**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **StringBuilder** and **StringBuffer** are **mutable** string-handling classes in Java, meaning their contents **can be modified** without creating new objects.
    
    They belong to the **java.lang** package and are used for **efficient string manipulation**, especially when performing many modifications such as appending, inserting, or deleting characters.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The `String` class is **immutable**, so operations like concatenation create **new objects**, leading to memory overhead.
    - `StringBuilder` and `StringBuffer` provide **modifiable character sequences**, improving performance.
    - Both maintain an **internal character array** that expands automatically as content grows.
    - Commonly used in scenarios involving loops, dynamic text construction, and heavy string processing.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Class Definitions**
    
    ```java
    public final class StringBuilder extends AbstractStringBuilder
    
    ```
    
    ```java
    public final class StringBuffer extends AbstractStringBuilder
    
    ```
    
    Both extend `AbstractStringBuilder`, which contains most of the logic.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Mutable String Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Mutable Behavior**
    
    Once created, the content of a `StringBuilder` or `StringBuffer` **can be changed** without creating new objects.
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Java");
    sb.append(" Programming");
    System.out.println(sb);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Java Programming
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    No new object is created; the internal buffer is modified.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Capacity and Growth**
    
    - Initial capacity = 16 characters (by default) + length of initial string.
    - When capacity is exceeded, it expands using:
    
    ```
    newCapacity = (oldCapacity * 2) + 2
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    System.out.println(sb.capacity()); // 16
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Difference Between StringBuilder and StringBuffer**
    
    ### **Key Differences**
    
    | **Aspect** | **StringBuilder** | **StringBuffer** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Mutability** | Mutable | Mutable |
    | **Thread-Safety** | ❌ Not synchronized | ✅ Synchronized (thread-safe) |
    | **Performance** | Faster | Slower due to locking |
    | **Usage** | Single-threaded apps | Multi-threaded apps |
    | **Introduced In** | Java 5 | Java 1.0 |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Use StringBuilder Over String?**
    
    - Faster for concatenation inside loops.
    - Recommended for heavy string manipulation.
    
    ### **Why Use StringBuffer?**
    
    - When multiple threads modify the same buffer.
    - E.g., server-side logging, shared message builders.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Important Methods of StringBuilder and StringBuffer**
    
    ---
    
    ### **1. append()**
    
    Adds text at the end of the existing sequence.
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder append(data)
    StringBuffer append(data)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Hello");
    sb.append(" World");
    System.out.println(sb);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello World
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **2. insert()**
    
    Inserts text at a specific index.
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    insert(int index, data)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("HelloWorld");
    sb.insert(5, " ");
    System.out.println(sb);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello World
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **3. delete()**
    
    Deletes characters in a given range (start inclusive, end exclusive).
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    delete(int start, int end)
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Good Morning");
    sb.delete(5, 12);
    System.out.println(sb);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Good g
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **4. reverse()**
    
    Reverses the entire sequence.
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    reverse()
    
    ```
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Java");
    sb.reverse();
    System.out.println(sb);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    avaJ
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Additional Useful Methods**
    
    | **Method** | **Purpose** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `capacity()` | Returns current buffer capacity |
    | `ensureCapacity(int)` | Ensures minimum buffer size |
    | `setLength(int)` | Sets new length (truncate or fill with `\u0000`) |
    | `replace(int, int, String)` | Replaces substring in range |
    | `charAt(int)` | Access specific character |
    | `toString()` | Converts to `String` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Example Program Demonstrating All Methods**
    
    ```java
    public class BuilderDemo {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Hello");
    
            sb.append(" World");
            sb.insert(5, ",");
            sb.delete(6, 12);
            sb.reverse();
    
            System.out.println(sb);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    olleH,
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Concept** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **String** | A printed sheet—you cannot edit it; you must reprint. |
    | **StringBuilder/StringBuffer** | A whiteboard—you can erase, add, or rewrite freely. |
    | **Thread Safety (StringBuffer)** | A shared whiteboard with a lock so only one person writes at a time. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Internal Behavior**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Mutability** | Uses a resizable internal char array |
    | **Appending** | Extends array when needed using growth formula |
    | **Thread-Safety** | StringBuffer synchronizes methods; StringBuilder does not |
    | **Final Output** | Converted to immutable String using `toString()` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use `StringBuilder` for **faster, single-threaded** operations.
    - Use `StringBuffer` for **thread-safe** operations.
    - Both avoid creating multiple intermediate `String` objects.
    - Operations modify the internal buffer rather than creating new objects.
    - Always call `toString()` to convert final result back to `String`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Feature** | **StringBuilder** | **StringBuffer** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Mutability | Yes | Yes |
    | Thread-safe | No | Yes |
    | Performance | Faster | Slower |
    | Best Use | Single-threaded operations | Multi-threaded operations |
    
    **Common Methods:**
    
    `append()`, `insert()`, `delete()`, `reverse()`, `replace()`, `setLength()`, `charAt()`, `capacity()`.
    
    **In summary:**
    
    `StringBuilder` and `StringBuffer` provide powerful, efficient mechanisms for **dynamic string manipulation**, reducing memory overhead and improving performance compared to regular `String` concatenation.
    
- **String Formatting**
    
    # **String Formatting**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **String formatting** is the process of creating structured text output by embedding variables or expressions into a predefined template.
    
    Java provides multiple mechanisms to format strings cleanly and consistently using:
    
    1. **`String.format()`**
    2. **`System.out.printf()`**
    3. **`MessageFormat` class**
    
    These methods support placeholders, number formatting, date formatting, alignment, and localization.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Create readable and consistent output.
    - Format strings, numbers, and dates elegantly.
    - Build dynamic messages without messy concatenation.
    - Support internationalization and localization.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Formatting Style**
    
    Java formatting uses **format specifiers** (`%s`, `%d`, `%f`, etc.) similar to C's printf-style formatting.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. String.format()**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `String.format()` returns a **new formatted string** using a format pattern and replacement values.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    String formatted = String.format(String format, Object... args);
    
    ```
    
    - Returns the formatted string.
    - Does **not print** to console.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String name = "Alice";
    int age = 25;
    
    String result = String.format("Name: %s, Age: %d", name, age);
    System.out.println(result);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Name: Alice, Age: 25
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Format Specifiers**
    
    | **Specifier** | **Meaning** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `%s` | String |
    | `%d` | Integer |
    | `%f` | Floating point number |
    | `%c` | Character |
    | `%b` | Boolean |
    | `%n` | New line |
    | `%t` | Date/time |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Width and Precision**
    
    ```java
    String.format("Value: %.2f", 3.14159)
    
    ```
    
    Output:
    
    ```
    Value: 3.14
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Alignment**
    
    ```java
    String.format("|%-10s|%10s|", "Left", "Right")
    
    ```
    
    Output:
    
    ```
    |Left      |     Right|
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. System.out.printf()**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `printf()` prints formatted text **directly to the console**.
    
    It uses the same format specifiers as `String.format()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    System.out.printf(String format, Object... args);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String course = "Java";
    int duration = 40;
    
    System.out.printf("Course: %s (%d hours)%n", course, duration);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Course: Java (40 hours)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Notes**
    
    - Does not return anything.
    - Often used for console-based applications.
    - `%n` represents a platform-independent newline.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Number Formatting**
    
    ```java
    System.out.printf("Price: %.2f%n", 99.4567);
    
    ```
    
    Output:
    
    ```
    Price: 99.46
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. MessageFormat Class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `MessageFormat` is a formatting class in `java.text` package used for **advanced, locale-sensitive formatting**.
    
    It uses placeholders in the form `{0}`, `{1}`, etc., not `%`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    String result = MessageFormat.format(String pattern, Object... arguments);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Basic Example**
    
    ```java
    String pattern = "Hello {0}, welcome to {1}.";
    String msg = MessageFormat.format(pattern, "Alice", "Java Training");
    System.out.println(msg);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello Alice, welcome to Java Training.
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Number Formatting**
    
    ```java
    String pattern = "Total: {0, number, currency}";
    String msg = MessageFormat.format(pattern, 1500);
    
    ```
    
    Output (example):
    
    ```
    Total: ₹1,500.00
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Date Formatting**
    
    ```java
    String pattern = "Date: {0, date, full}";
    String msg = MessageFormat.format(pattern, new java.util.Date());
    
    ```
    
    Output:
    
    ```
    Date: Monday, November 24, 2025
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Advanced Syntax**
    
    | **Type** | **Usage** | **Example Pattern** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Number** | `{index, number}` | `{0, number, integer}` |
    | **Currency** | `{index, number, currency}` | `{0, number, currency}` |
    | **Percent** | `{index, number, percent}` | `{0, number, percent}` |
    | **Date** | `{index, date}` | `{0, date, short}` |
    | **Time** | `{index, time}` | `{0, time, full}` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Localization Support**
    
    `MessageFormat` adapts formatting to the **default locale** (currency symbol, date formats, number formats).
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Comparison of Formatting Methods**
    
    | **Feature** | **String.format()** | **System.out.printf()** | **MessageFormat** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Returns String** | ✔ Yes | ❌ No | ✔ Yes |
    | **Prints Output** | ❌ No | ✔ Yes | ❌ No |
    | **Placeholder Style** | `%` syntax | `%` syntax | `{0}` syntax |
    | **Localization Support** | Limited | Limited | Extensive |
    | **Best For** | Constructing formatted outputs | Console printing | Internationalized messages |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Operation** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `String.format()` | Writing a formatted letter and returning it |
    | `printf()` | Printing the letter directly |
    | `MessageFormat` | Creating multilingual letters with regional formats |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - `String.format()` internally uses `Formatter` class.
    - `printf()` uses the same `Formatter` but routes output to `System.out`.
    - `MessageFormat` parses patterns using internal tokenizers and applies locale rules.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use `String.format()` when formatting needs to be reused or stored.
    - Use `printf()` for console-based interactive programs.
    - Use `MessageFormat` when working with **dates, currencies**, or **multilingual** messages.
    - Be careful with `%n` vs `\n` — `%n` is platform-independent.
    - Floating-point formatting can control precision using `%.nf`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Method** | **Purpose** | **Usage Example** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **String.format()** | Returns formatted string | `String.format("Hi %s", name)` |
    | **System.out.printf()** | Prints formatted text | `printf("Total: %.2f", amt)` |
    | **MessageFormat** | Localized, structured messages | `MessageFormat.format("Hello {0}", name)` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides powerful formatting tools to generate readable, structured, and locale-sensitive text.
    
    `String.format()` and `printf()` are ideal for general formatting, while `MessageFormat` is best suited for internationalized and complex message templates.
    
- **Modern String Features**
    
    # **Modern String Features**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Modern versions of Java introduced advanced features to improve **string handling**, **performance**, and **readability**. These include:
    
    - **Text Blocks** (Java 15+)
    - **StringJoiner** (Java 8)
    - New methods like `transform()` and `formatted()` (Java 12+)
    - Performance improvements and best practices
    - Better comparison techniques to avoid pitfalls
    
    These features simplify working with complex strings, multi-line text, and functional-style transformations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Modern string features aim to:
    
    - Reduce boilerplate in multi-line strings
    - Improve performance when building large strings
    - Avoid common mistakes like using `==` for comparison
    - Provide readable and flexible formatting/transformations
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Text Blocks (Java 15+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Text Block** is a Java literal for **multi-line strings**, enclosed in triple quotes `"""`.
    
    It:
    
    - Preserves formatting
    - Supports embedded quotes
    - Eliminates need for escape sequences
    - Improves readability of SQL, JSON, HTML, XML, or multi-line messages
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    String text = """
                   line1
                   line2
                   line3
                   """;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: SQL Query**
    
    ```java
    String query = """
                    SELECT id, name, marks
                    FROM student
                    WHERE marks > 80
                    ORDER BY name ASC;
                    """;
    System.out.println(query);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Features**
    
    - Leading indentation is automatically removed.
    - Supports escape sequences if needed.
    - Usable anywhere a normal string is used.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. StringJoiner Class (Java 8)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `StringJoiner` is a utility class used to **construct a single string** from multiple strings with:
    
    - A **delimiter**
    - An optional **prefix**
    - An optional **suffix**
    
    It offers a memory-efficient alternative to repeatedly concatenating strings.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    StringJoiner sj = new StringJoiner(delimiter, prefix, suffix);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    StringJoiner sj = new StringJoiner(", ", "[", "]");
    
    sj.add("Java");
    sj.add("Python");
    sj.add("C++");
    
    System.out.println(sj);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    [Java, Python, C++]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - Efficient joining of dynamic collections or streams.
    - Useful for logging, CSV creation, and formatted lists.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. String Performance Implications in Loops**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Problem**
    
    Using regular string concatenation (`str = str + "value"`) inside loops is inefficient because **String is immutable**.
    
    Each concatenation:
    
    - Creates a **new String object**
    - Copies old and new contents
    - Causes memory overhead and slower performance
    
    ---
    
    ### **Poor Performance Example**
    
    ```java
    String s = "";
    for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
        s = s + i;  // Inefficient
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Optimized Approach Using StringBuilder**
    
    ```java
    StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
    for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
        sb.append(i);
    }
    String result = sb.toString();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **General Rule**
    
    | **Operation** | **Use** |
    | --- | --- |
    | Repeated modifications | **StringBuilder** |
    | Thread-safe modifications | **StringBuffer** |
    | Final result creation | **String** |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. String Comparison Pitfalls (`==` vs `equals()`)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Problem**
    
    Beginners often mistakenly compare strings using `==`, which checks **reference equality**, not content.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Comparison Rules**
    
    | Comparison Method | Checks | Use Case |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `==` | Reference equality | Check if both variables point to same object |
    | `equals()` | Content/value equality | Compare text content |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String s1 = "Java";
    String s2 = new String("Java");
    
    System.out.println(s1 == s2);       // false
    System.out.println(s1.equals(s2));  // true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Because:**
    
    - `s1` → String Pool
    - `s2` → New heap object
    - Same content, **different references**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Correct Practice**
    
    Always use:
    
    ```java
    s1.equals(s2)
    
    ```
    
    or
    
    ```java
    Objects.equals(s1, s2)  // handles null safely
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. String.transform() (Java 12+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `transform()` applies a **function** to a string and returns a new transformed string.
    
    It introduces **functional programming** into string processing.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Method Signature**
    
    ```java
    public <R> R transform(Function<? super String, ? extends R> function)
    
    ```
    
    The return type is **generic** — it can return a transformed string or even another object.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String result = "hello".transform(str -> str.toUpperCase());
    System.out.println(result);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    HELLO
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Complex Example**
    
    ```java
    int len = "Java Programming"
                .transform(String::length);
    
    System.out.println(len);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    16
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    - Cleaner transformation pipelines
    - Applying modular functions
    - Processing input strings functionally
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. String.formatted() (Java 12+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `formatted()` is a **convenient instance method** that works like `String.format()`, but is called directly on a string template.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    String result = "Name: %s, Age: %d".formatted("Alice", 25);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    String result = "Coordinates: (%d, %d)".formatted(10, 20);
    System.out.println(result);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Coordinates: (10, 20)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Difference**
    
    | Method | Type | Example |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `String.format()` | Static | `String.format("%s", name)` |
    | `formatted()` | Instance method | `"Hello %s".formatted(name)` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Cleaner, more readable formatting
    - No need to reference `String.format`
    - Works seamlessly with IDE code completion
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Text Blocks** | Writing multi-line text cleanly on lined paper |
    | **StringJoiner** | Adding items into a shopping cart with separators |
    | **StringBuilder in loops** | Using a reusable container instead of creating a new one each time |
    | **`equals()` vs `==`** | Comparing content of two books vs comparing the bookshelves holding them |
    | **transform()** | Passing text through a “function machine” |
    | **formatted()** | Filling placeholders in a form template |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Text blocks undergo **indentation normalization** at compile time.
    - `StringJoiner` uses an internal `StringBuilder`.
    - `transform()` avoids unnecessary intermediate string copies.
    - `formatted()` internally uses `String.format()`.
    - Using `String` in loops can create **hundreds of temporary objects**, affecting GC.
    - Comparisons using `==` only check **memory address**, handled at JVM reference level.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - Use **text blocks** for SQL, JSON, XML, or multi-line messages.
    - Prefer **StringJoiner**, `String.join()`, or builders for joining operations.
    - Avoid **String concatenation in loops** — use builders.
    - Never compare strings with `==` unless intentionally checking reference equality.
    - Use `transform()` for functional-style transformations.
    - Use `formatted()` for cleaner, embedded formatting.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose | Example |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Text Blocks** | Multi-line easy-to-read strings | `""" ... """` |
    | **StringJoiner** | Efficient joining with prefixes/suffixes | `new StringJoiner(",")` |
    | **Performance in Loops** | Avoids overhead of immutability | Use `StringBuilder` |
    | **Comparison** | Prevents reference-equality errors | `s1.equals(s2)` |
    | **transform()** | Functional transformation | `"hello".transform(...)` |
    | **formatted()** | Inline formatting | `"Name: %s".formatted(name)` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Modern Java provides powerful string-handling enhancements that improve readability, performance, and flexibility.
    
    Features like **Text Blocks**, **StringJoiner**, and functional-style methods like **transform()** and **formatted()** make string manipulation more expressive and efficient in modern Java applications.
    

### 3.2 Arrays and Collections

- **Arrays**
    
    # **Arrays**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **array** in Java is a **fixed-size**, **indexed collection** of elements of the **same data type**, stored in **contiguous memory**.
    
    Arrays allow fast access and efficient iteration using indices.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Arrays are **objects** in Java (even for primitives).
    - The **index starts at 0** and ends at `length - 1`.
    - The size of an array is **fixed at creation** and cannot be changed.
    - Arrays support both **single-dimensional** and **multi-dimensional** structures.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Single-dimensional Arrays**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A single-dimensional array (1D array) stores elements in a **linear* sequence.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Declaration**
    
    ```java
    int[] arr;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    arr = new int[5]; // size = 5
    
    ```
    
    ### **Initialization**
    
    ```java
    int[] ages = {20, 25, 30, 35};
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing Elements**
    
    ```java
    System.out.println(ages[2]); // 30
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int[] nums = new int[3];
    nums[0] = 10;
    nums[1] = 20;
    nums[2] = 30;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Multi-dimensional Arrays**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A multi-dimensional array is an array of arrays.
    
    Most common: **2D array** representing rows and columns.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    int[][] matrix = new int[3][2]; // 3 rows, 2 columns
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Initialization**
    
    ```java
    int[][] matrix = {
        {1, 2},
        {3, 4},
        {5, 6}
    };
    
    ```
    
    ### **Accessing Elements**
    
    ```java
    System.out.println(matrix[1][0]); // 3
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Jagged Arrays (Irregular 2D arrays)**
    
    ```java
    int[][] jagged = new int[3][];
    jagged[0] = new int[2];
    jagged[1] = new int[4];
    jagged[2] = new int[1];
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Array Initialization Types**
    
    ---
    
    ### **1. Default Initialization**
    
    ```java
    int[] a = new int[3];  // {0, 0, 0}
    boolean[] b = new boolean[3];  // {false, false, false}
    String[] s = new String[3];  // {null, null, null}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **2. Explicit Initialization**
    
    ```java
    int[] a = {1, 2, 3};
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **3. Inline Initialization**
    
    ```java
    int[] a = new int[]{10, 20, 30};
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Array Operations: Sorting and Searching**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Sorting Using Arrays.sort()**
    
    ```java
    int[] arr = {5, 2, 8, 1};
    Arrays.sort(arr);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [1, 2, 5, 8]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Binary Search Using Arrays.binarySearch()**
    
    ```java
    int index = Arrays.binarySearch(arr, 5);
    
    ```
    
    **Important:**
    
    Array **must be sorted** before binary search.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Copying Arrays**
    
    ```java
    int[] copy = Arrays.copyOf(arr, arr.length);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Filling Arrays**
    
    ```java
    Arrays.fill(arr, 100);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Arrays Utility Class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Package**
    
    ```java
    java.util.Arrays
    
    ```
    
    The `Arrays` class provides utility methods such as:
    
    - `sort()`
    - `binarySearch()`
    - `copyOf()`
    - `equals()`
    - `fill()`
    - `toString()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    System.out.println(Arrays.toString(arr));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Arrays Deep Methods**
    
    ---
    
    Modern Java provides **deep processing** methods to handle **multi-dimensional arrays**, including nested arrays of objects.
    
    ---
    
    ### **1. deepToString()**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Returns a **deep string representation** of multi-dimensional arrays.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int[][] matrix = {{1, 2}, {3, 4}};
    System.out.println(Arrays.deepToString(matrix));
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [[1, 2], [3, 4]]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **2. deepEquals()**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Compares multi-dimensional arrays **recursively**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int[][] a = {{1,2},{3,4}};
    int[][] b = {{1,2},{3,4}};
    System.out.println(Arrays.deepEquals(a, b)); // true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Difference: equals() vs deepEquals()**
    
    | Method | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | `equals()` | Compares references, not contents of nested arrays |
    | `deepEquals()` | Compares contents of nested arrays |
    
    ---
    
    ### **3. compare()** (Java 9+)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Performs a **lexicographical comparison** of arrays.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int result = Arrays.compare(new int[]{1,2}, new int[]{1,3});
    System.out.println(result); // -1
    
    ```
    
    - Returns:
        - `< 0` → first array is smaller
        - `0` → equal
        - `> 0` → first array is larger
    
    ---
    
    ### **4. mismatch()** (Java 9+)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Finds the **first index where arrays differ**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    int index = Arrays.mismatch(arr1, arr2);
    
    ```
    
    **Returns**
    
    - Index of mismatch
    - `1` if both arrays are identical
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int[] a = {1, 2, 3};
    int[] b = {1, 2, 4};
    
    System.out.println(Arrays.mismatch(a, b));
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    2
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | 1D Array | A row of lockers, each with an index |
    | 2D Array | A spreadsheet with rows and columns |
    | deepEquals() | Checking content of all pages in two books |
    | compare() | Alphabetically comparing two words |
    | mismatch() | Finding first differing character |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Arrays store elements in **contiguous memory**, improving cache performance.
    - Each array stores its **length** in the JVM header.
    - Type checking ensures arrays follow **covariant rules** (e.g., `String[]` is a subtype of `Object[]`).
    - Multi-dimensional arrays are **arrays of arrays**, not true matrices.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - Array size is **fixed**; use `ArrayList` for dynamic sizes.
    - Multi-dimensional arrays may be **jagged**.
    - Use `Arrays` class for efficient operations.
    - Avoid manually sorting or searching — prefer built-in optimized methods.
    - Use **deep methods** for nested arrays to ensure accurate comparisons.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Key Points |
    | --- | --- |
    | 1D Arrays | Linear storage, fixed size |
    | Multi-dimensional Arrays | Arrays of arrays, support jagged structures |
    | Initialization | Default, explicit, inline |
    | Operations | Sorting, searching, copying, filling |
    | Arrays Class | Utility operations for array handling |
    | Deep Methods | `deepToString()`, `deepEquals()`, `compare()`, `mismatch()` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Arrays provide fast, indexed storage for fixed-size collections.
    
    Java enhances array handling with the **Arrays** utility class and **deep methods**, enabling efficient manipulation, comparison, and representation of complex multi-dimensional structures.
    
- **Collection Framework Overview**
    
    # **Collection Framework Overview**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **Java Collection Framework (JCF)** is a unified architecture in the **java.util** package that provides **predefined classes and interfaces** for storing, retrieving, and manipulating groups of objects efficiently.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    The framework offers:
    
    - **Interfaces** (List, Set, Queue, Map) defining abstract data structures
    - **Concrete classes** (ArrayList, HashSet, PriorityQueue, HashMap) implementing the structures
    - **Algorithms and utilities** (sorting, searching, synchronization) through the `Collections` class
    
    The framework standardizes how collections are handled, promoting:
    
    - Code reusability
    - Better performance
    - Type-safety (especially with Generics)
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Collection Hierarchy**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Top-Level Structure**
    
    The Collection Framework has two major roots:
    
    1. **Collection Interface** (for storing individual elements)
    2. **Map Interface** (for key–value pairs)
    
    Below is the simplified hierarchy:
    
    ```
                      Iterable
                          │
                    Collection
            ┌──────────┬──────────┬──────────┐
           List        Set       Queue      Deque
    
    Map (separate hierarchy, not extending Collection)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Hierarchy Diagram (Detailed)**
    
    ```
    Iterable
       └── Collection
             ├── List
             │     ├── ArrayList
             │     ├── LinkedList
             │     └── Vector (Legacy)
             │           └── Stack (Legacy)
             │
             ├── Set
             │     ├── HashSet
             │     ├── LinkedHashSet
             │     └── SortedSet
             │            └── TreeSet
             │
             ├── Queue
             │     ├── PriorityQueue
             │     ├── ArrayDeque
             │     └── LinkedList
             │
             └── Deque
                   ├── ArrayDeque
                   ├── LinkedList
                   └── ConcurrentLinkedDeque
    
    Map (Separate hierarchy)
       ├── HashMap
       ├── LinkedHashMap
       ├── TreeMap
       ├── Hashtable (Legacy)
       └── ConcurrentHashMap
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Iterable Interface**
    
    Root of the Collection hierarchy. Provides:
    
    - `iterator()`
    
    Allows enhanced for-loop:
    
    ```java
    for (String s : list) { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Core Collection Interfaces**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Collection Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **Collection** interface is the root of all collection classes except `Map`.
    
    It represents a group of individual objects, known as **elements**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    - `add()`
    - `remove()`
    - `size()`
    - `isEmpty()`
    - `clear()`
    - `contains()`
    - `iterator()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Subinterfaces**
    
    - **List**
    - **Set**
    - **Queue**
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. List Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **List** is an **ordered** collection that:
    
    - Maintains **insertion order**
    - Allows **duplicate elements**
    - Supports **index-based access**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Implementations**
    
    | Class | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **ArrayList** | Dynamic array; fast random access |
    | **LinkedList** | Doubly-linked list; fast insert/delete |
    | **Vector** | Legacy, synchronized |
    | **Stack** | Legacy LIFO stack |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    - `get(int index)`
    - `add(int index, E element)`
    - `set(int index, E element)`
    - `remove(int index)`
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Set Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Set** is a collection that:
    
    - Does **not allow duplicates**
    - Does **not maintain order** (in general)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Implementations**
    
    | Class | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **HashSet** | Fast, unordered |
    | **LinkedHashSet** | Maintains insertion order |
    | **TreeSet** | Sorted order using **natural order** or **Comparator** |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Storing unique IDs
    - Removing duplicates
    - Efficient `contains()` checks
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Queue Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Queue** represents a **FIFO (First-In-First-Out)** structure.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Implementations**
    
    | Class | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **PriorityQueue** | Elements ordered by natural order or comparator |
    | **ArrayDeque** | Efficient queue/deque |
    | **LinkedList** | Can act as a queue |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Queue Typical Methods**
    
    - `offer()`
    - `poll()`
    - `peek()`
    
    ---
    
    ## **E. Map Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Map** stores **key-value pairs**.
    
    It does **not extend Collection**, but is a part of the Collection Framework.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - **Keys are unique**
    - **Values may be duplicated**
    - Used for fast lookups using keys
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Implementations**
    
    | Class | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **HashMap** | Fast, unordered |
    | **LinkedHashMap** | Maintains insertion order |
    | **TreeMap** | Sorted keys |
    | **Hashtable** | Legacy synchronized version |
    | **ConcurrentHashMap** | Thread-safe, modern alternative |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Typical Map Methods**
    
    - `put(K key, V value)`
    - `get(Object key)`
    - `remove(Object key)`
    - `containsKey(Object key)`
    - `keySet()`
    - `entrySet()`
    - `values()`
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Interface | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **List** | A shopping list where duplicates and order matter |
    | **Set** | A list of unique phone numbers |
    | **Queue** | A ticket queue at a movie theater |
    | **Map** | A dictionary: word → meaning |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Collections use **generic types** to enforce type-safety at compile time.
    - Dynamic collections internally resize or rehash automatically.
    - Many collections rely on **hashing** (HashMap, HashSet) for O(1) average lookup.
    - Iterators use **fail-fast** behavior to detect structural modifications.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Important Points**
    
    - Prefer **Collection interfaces** over concrete classes (e.g., use `List` instead of `ArrayList`).
    - Use **generics** to avoid `ClassCastException`.
    - Understand time complexities (e.g., ArrayList vs LinkedList).
    - Use **Map** for key-value storage (not part of Collection hierarchy).
    - Use **Collections utility class** for sorting, synchronization, immutability wrappers.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Summary**
    
    | Interface | Characteristics | Allows Duplicates? | Maintains Order? |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Collection** | Root of collections | Depends | Depends |
    | **List** | Indexed, ordered | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes |
    | **Set** | Unique elements | ❌ No | Sometimes |
    | **Queue** | FIFO structure | ✔ Usually | ✔ Usually |
    | **Map** | Key-value pairs | Keys: ❌, Values: ✔ | Depends |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The Java Collection Framework provides a rich and flexible set of interfaces and classes for managing data efficiently.
    
    Understanding the hierarchy and core interfaces helps in choosing the right data structure for optimal performance and maintainable code.
    
- **List Implementations**
    
    # **List Implementations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **List** interface represents an **ordered**, **indexed**, and **duplicate-allowing** collection.
    
    Java provides multiple implementations of the List interface, each with different internal structures and performance characteristics:
    
    - **ArrayList** → resizable array
    - **LinkedList** → doubly-linked list
    - **Vector** → synchronized resizable array
    - **Stack** → synchronized LIFO (Last-In-First-Out) stack
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Different List implementations allow choosing the right data structure based on:
    
    - Access speed
    - Insertion/deletion efficiency
    - Thread-safety
    - Memory usage
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. ArrayList**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `ArrayList` is a **resizable array** implementation of the `List` interface.
    
    It stores elements in a **dynamic array** that grows automatically when capacity is exceeded.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Fast **random access** (O(1))
    - Slower insertions/deletions in the **middle** (shifting required)
    - Allows **duplicates** and **null values**
    - Not synchronized (not thread-safe)
    - Backed by an internal **Object[]** array
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    ArrayList<String> list = new ArrayList<>();
    list.add("A");
    list.add("B");
    list.add("C");
    System.out.println(list);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Capacity Growth Rule**
    
    When array overflows:
    
    ```
    newCapacity = oldCapacity + (oldCapacity >> 1)   // 1.5x growth
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Frequent **read operations**
    - Random access needed
    - Mostly sequential insertions
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. LinkedList**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `LinkedList` is a **doubly-linked list** implementation of `List` and `Deque`.
    
    Each node stores:
    
    - Data
    - Reference to **previous**
    - Reference to **next**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Fast **insertions** and **deletions** anywhere (O(1) node unlinking)
    - Slow **random access** (O(n))
    - Can be used as:
        - List
        - Queue
        - Deque
    - Allows duplicates & nulls
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    LinkedList<Integer> list = new LinkedList<>();
    list.add(10);
    list.addFirst(5);
    list.addLast(20);
    System.out.println(list);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Frequent add/remove operations in the middle
    - Implementing queues or deques
    - Large shifting operations avoided
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Vector**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Vector` is a **synchronized**, resizable array-based list.
    
    Introduced in early Java (legacy class), intended for thread-safe operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Thread-safe via **synchronized methods**
    - Slower than ArrayList due to synchronization overhead
    - Allows duplicates and nulls
    - Grows by **100%** (doubles size) by default
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Vector<String> v = new Vector<>();
    v.add("X");
    v.add("Y");
    System.out.println(v);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why It’s Legacy**
    
    - Slower
    - Replaced by **Collections.synchronizedList(new ArrayList<>())**
    - Rarely used now except in legacy systems
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Multi-threaded environments requiring built-in synchronization
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Stack**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Stack` is a **LIFO (Last-In-First-Out)** data structure that extends `Vector`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Uses methods: `push()`, `pop()`, `peek()`
    - Thread-safe (due to Vector)
    - Legacy class — modern replacement is `ArrayDeque`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Stack<Integer> stack = new Stack<>();
    stack.push(10);
    stack.push(20);
    System.out.println(stack.pop()); // 20
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Stack Operations**
    
    | Operation | Method | Description |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Push | `push(E)` | Adds element to top |
    | Pop | `pop()` | Removes and returns top |
    | Peek | `peek()` | Returns top without removing |
    | Empty check | `empty()` | True if empty |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Parsing expressions
    - Undo/Redo operations
    - Tree/graph traversals
    - But prefer **ArrayDeque** for non-synchronized modern stacks
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison Table (ArrayList vs LinkedList vs Vector vs Stack)**
    
    | Feature | ArrayList | LinkedList | Vector | Stack |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Internal Structure** | Dynamic array | Doubly-linked list | Dynamic array | Vector-based |
    | **Thread-safe** | ❌ No | ❌ No | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes |
    | **Allows duplicates** | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes |
    | **Random Access** | ✔ Fast | ❌ Slow | ✔ Fast | ✔ Fast |
    | **Insert/Delete (middle)** | ❌ Slow | ✔ Fast | ❌ Slow | ❌ Slow |
    | **Growth factor** | 1.5x | Node-based | 2x | 2x |
    | **Use Instead** | General lists | Frequent insert/delete | Rare cases | Use ArrayDeque instead |
    | **Legacy?** | No | No | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Structure | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **ArrayList** | A bookshelf — easy to read a book by its index |
    | **LinkedList** | A chain — easy to insert/remove links |
    | **Vector** | A locker with a security guard — synchronized access |
    | **Stack** | A stack of plates — last plate kept is removed first |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - ArrayList and Vector store elements in **contiguous memory**.
    - LinkedList stores each element in a **separate node object**, increasing memory overhead.
    - Modifying a collection during iteration may trigger **fail-fast** behavior.
    - Access to List is via **iterator** or index-based depending on implementation.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Prefer **ArrayList** for general-purpose lists.
    - Use **LinkedList** when many insertions/deletions occur in the middle.
    - Avoid **Vector** unless interacting with legacy systems.
    - Avoid **Stack**; use **ArrayDeque** for stack operations.
    - All implementations allow **null** values (except in concurrent variants).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | List Type | Best For |
    | --- | --- |
    | **ArrayList** | Fast access, general use |
    | **LinkedList** | Many add/remove operations |
    | **Vector** | Legacy synchronized list |
    | **Stack** | LIFO, but replaced by ArrayDeque |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The List interface has multiple implementations optimized for different scenarios.
    
    Understanding how each structure works internally helps choose the right list for performance, memory efficiency, and thread safety in Java applications.
    
- **Set Implementations**
    
    # **Set Implementations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Set** in Java is a collection that **does not allow duplicate elements**.
    
    Java provides multiple implementations of the Set interface, each with different internal structures and ordering rules:
    
    - **HashSet** → Unordered, hash table–based set
    - **LinkedHashSet** → Maintains *insertion order*
    - **TreeSet** → Sorted set (natural order or custom Comparator)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Different Set implementations allow developers to choose between:
    
    - Performance
    - Ordering requirements
    - Memory usage
    - Sorting and navigation capabilities
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. HashSet**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `HashSet` is a Set implementation backed by a **hash table**.
    
    It stores elements based on their **hashCode()**, ensuring **no duplicates** and providing **O(1) average time** for add, search, and remove operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - **No ordering** (elements appear in random order)
    - **Allows one null** value
    - Fast performance due to hashing
    - Uses **hashCode()** and **equals()** to detect duplicates
    - Backed internally by a `HashMap` (keys = elements, values = dummy object)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    HashSet<String> set = new HashSet<>();
    set.add("Apple");
    set.add("Banana");
    set.add("Apple");
    
    System.out.println(set);
    
    ```
    
    **Output Example**
    
    ```
    [Banana, Apple]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **How it prevents duplicates**
    
    1. Computes `hashCode()` of element
    2. Finds bucket in hash table
    3. Uses `equals()` to compare existing elements
    4. Rejects insertion if duplicate found
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Fast lookup
    - Unique IDs
    - Removing duplicates
    - Membership checking (`contains()`)
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. LinkedHashSet**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `LinkedHashSet` is a subclass of `HashSet` that **maintains insertion order** using a **doubly-linked list** attached to hash table entries.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Maintains **insertion order**
    - Slightly slower than HashSet
    - Allows one null
    - Internally uses:
        - Hash table (like HashSet)
        - Linked list to preserve insertion order
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    LinkedHashSet<Integer> set = new LinkedHashSet<>();
    set.add(10);
    set.add(5);
    set.add(20);
    
    System.out.println(set);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [10, 5, 20]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - When order **matters**
    - Creating predictable iteration sequences
    - Caching (because maintains insertion order)
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. TreeSet**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `TreeSet` is a **sorted** Set implementation backed by a **Red-Black Tree** (self-balancing binary search tree).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Elements stored in **sorted (ascending)** order
    - Sorting based on:
        - **Natural ordering** (Comparable)
        - **Custom Comparator**
    - No null allowed
    - Operations: O(log n)
    - Provides **navigational methods**:
        - `higher()`, `lower()`
        - `floor()`, `ceiling()`
        - `first()`, `last()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    TreeSet<String> set = new TreeSet<>();
    set.add("Zebra");
    set.add("Apple");
    set.add("Mango");
    
    System.out.println(set);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [Apple, Mango, Zebra]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example with Comparator**
    
    ```java
    TreeSet<Integer> set = new TreeSet<>(Comparator.reverseOrder());
    set.add(10);
    set.add(3);
    set.add(7);
    
    System.out.println(set);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [10, 7, 3]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Sorted unique elements
    - Range queries
    - Navigable operations
    - Natural ordering or custom sorting
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Comparison Table — HashSet vs LinkedHashSet vs TreeSet**
    
    | Feature | HashSet | LinkedHashSet | TreeSet |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Ordering** | No (random) | Yes (insertion order) | Yes (sorted) |
    | **Underlying Structure** | Hash table | Hash table + linked list | Red-Black Tree |
    | **Time Complexity (add, remove, contains)** | O(1) average | O(1) average | O(log n) |
    | **Allows Null?** | ✔ Yes (one) | ✔ Yes (one) | ❌ No |
    | **Duplicates?** | ❌ No | ❌ No | ❌ No |
    | **Memory Usage** | Low | Medium | High |
    | **Best For** | Fast lookup | Predictable order + fast lookup | Sorted data, range queries |
    | **Thread-safe?** | ❌ No | ❌ No | ❌ No |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Usage Examples**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Remove Duplicates from a List**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> list = Arrays.asList(1,2,2,3,4,4,5);
    Set<Integer> unique = new HashSet<>(list);
    System.out.println(unique);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Maintain insertion order**
    
    ```java
    Set<String> orderSet = new LinkedHashSet<>();
    orderSet.add("A");
    orderSet.add("B");
    orderSet.add("C");
    System.out.println(orderSet);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Automatically sorted**
    
    ```java
    Set<Integer> sorted = new TreeSet<>();
    sorted.add(50);
    sorted.add(10);
    sorted.add(30);
    System.out.println(sorted);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Set Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **HashSet** | A bag where items are thrown randomly, but duplicates aren’t allowed |
    | **LinkedHashSet** | A line of people — order matters, but all must be unique |
    | **TreeSet** | A sorted bookshelf where books must be placed alphabetically |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Internal Behavior**
    
    - **HashSet**
        
        Uses hashing to find bucket index → O(1) average operations.
        
    - **LinkedHashSet**
        
        Uses a linked list to chain elements for predictable iteration.
        
    - **TreeSet**
        
        Each element stored as a node in a Red-Black Tree → O(log n) balancing.
        
    - `equals()` + `hashCode()` are critical for HashSet & LinkedHashSet.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Use **HashSet** for fastest operations when order doesn't matter.
    - Use **LinkedHashSet** when iteration order must match insertion order.
    - Use **TreeSet** when sorted output is required.
    - **TreeSet does not allow null** because it uses comparisons.
    - All Set implementations disallow duplicates.
    - Use custom Comparator with TreeSet for custom sorting logic.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Set Implementation | Key Feature | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **HashSet** | Unordered, fast | High-performance membership checks |
    | **LinkedHashSet** | Maintains insertion order | Predictable ordered iteration |
    | **TreeSet** | Sorted | Range operations, sorted data |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides three major Set implementations — **HashSet**, **LinkedHashSet**, and **TreeSet** — each optimized for different ordering and performance requirements.
    
    Choosing the right Set is essential for writing efficient and predictable Java applications.
    
- **Queue Implementations**
    
    # **Queue Implementations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Queue** in Java represents a **FIFO (First-In-First-Out)** data structure. Elements are added at the **rear** and removed from the **front**.
    
    Java provides several queue implementations in the Collection Framework, each optimized for different use cases:
    
    - **PriorityQueue** → priority-based ordering
    - **LinkedList** → queue + deque functionality
    - **Deque interface + ArrayDeque** → double-ended queue (efficient stack/queue)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Queue implementations are used for:
    
    - Task scheduling
    - Buffering
    - Message processing
    - Order-based processing
    - BFS traversal in graphs
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. PriorityQueue**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `PriorityQueue` is a queue implementation where elements are removed based on **priority** rather than insertion order.
    
    It uses a **binary heap** internally.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Not FIFO → ordered by **natural order** (Comparable) or **custom Comparator**
    - Allows duplicates
    - Does **not allow null**
    - Unbounded but grows dynamically
    - Head element = **lowest priority** (min-heap by default)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>();
    pq.add(30);
    pq.add(10);
    pq.add(20);
    
    System.out.println(pq.poll()); // 10
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Custom Comparator Example**
    
    ```java
    PriorityQueue<Integer> pq = new PriorityQueue<>(Comparator.reverseOrder());
    pq.add(10);
    pq.add(30);
    pq.add(20);
    
    System.out.println(pq.poll()); // 30
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Scheduling tasks
    - Graph algorithms (like Dijkstra’s)
    - Priority-based message handling
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. LinkedList as a Queue**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `LinkedList` implements **Queue** and **Deque** interfaces, making it usable as:
    
    - A FIFO queue
    - A double-ended queue
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Based on **doubly-linked list**
    - Supports fast insertions/removals from both ends
    - Allows null values
    - Slower random access (O(n))
    
    ---
    
    ### **Queue Operations in LinkedList**
    
    ```java
    Queue<String> queue = new LinkedList<>();
    queue.offer("A");
    queue.offer("B");
    queue.offer("C");
    
    System.out.println(queue.poll()); // A
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Deque Operations**
    
    ```java
    Deque<Integer> deque = new LinkedList<>();
    deque.addFirst(10);
    deque.addLast(20);
    
    System.out.println(deque.removeFirst()); // 10
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Implementing FIFO queue
    - BFS traversal
    - Double-ended queue scenarios
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Deque Interface & ArrayDeque**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Deque** (Double-Ended Queue) allows insertion and removal at **both ends**.
    
    `ArrayDeque` is the most efficient general-purpose deque implementation.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.1 Deque Interface**
    
    ### **Methods**
    
    | Operation | Front | Back |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Insert | `addFirst()` | `addLast()` |
    | Remove | `removeFirst()` | `removeLast()` |
    | Peek | `peekFirst()` | `peekLast()` |
    
    Deque is ideal for:
    
    - Queue operations (FIFO)
    - Stack operations (LIFO)
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.2 ArrayDeque**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `ArrayDeque` is a **resizable array-based** implementation of `Deque`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Faster than `LinkedList` for queue and stack operations
    - Does **not allow null**
    - No capacity restrictions (resizes automatically)
    - Not thread-safe
    - Can replace:
        - **Queue**
        - **Stack** (preferred over legacy `Stack` class)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Queue Behavior**
    
    ```java
    Deque<String> deque = new ArrayDeque<>();
    deque.offerLast("A");
    deque.offerLast("B");
    deque.offerLast("C");
    
    System.out.println(deque.pollFirst()); // A
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Stack Behavior**
    
    ```java
    Deque<Integer> stack = new ArrayDeque<>();
    stack.push(10);
    stack.push(20);
    
    System.out.println(stack.pop()); // 20
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Implementing queue or stack
    - Sliding window problems
    - Expression evaluation
    - Efficient LRU cache (with LinkedHashMap)
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Comparison Table**
    
    | Feature | PriorityQueue | LinkedList | ArrayDeque |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Order** | Based on priority | FIFO / LIFO | FIFO + LIFO |
    | **Internal Structure** | Binary heap | Doubly-linked list | Resizable array |
    | **Allows null?** | ❌ No | ✔ Yes | ❌ No |
