    | **Performance** | O(log n) ops | O(1) for insert/remove ends | Fastest for both ends |
    | **Thread-safe?** | ❌ No | ❌ No | ❌ No |
    | **Duplicates?** | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes |
    | **Best For** | Priority scheduling | General-purpose queue | Fast stack/queue |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Structure | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **PriorityQueue** | Hospital emergency room—patients treated by priority |
    | **LinkedList Queue** | People standing in a line (first come, first served) |
    | **ArrayDeque** | Two-way lane allowing vehicles to enter/exit from both ends |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Internal Behavior**
    
    - PriorityQueue uses **binary heap** stored in a dynamic array.
    - LinkedList stores each element in a **node** with previous/next links.
    - ArrayDeque uses a **circular array**, resizing when needed.
    - Null is disallowed in PriorityQueue/ArrayDeque to avoid ambiguity with `null` returns from poll().
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use **PriorityQueue** when order must be based on priority, not insertion.
    - Use **LinkedList** for flexible queue/deque operations but expect slower performance.
    - Prefer **ArrayDeque** for both **queue and stack** implementations.
    - All queues except LinkedList disallow null values.
    - PriorityQueue does not guarantee sorted iteration—only head has highest priority.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Queue Type | Key Feature | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **PriorityQueue** | Priority-based ordering | Task scheduling, algorithms |
    | **LinkedList** | Queue + Deque | General-purpose queue |
    | **ArrayDeque** | Double-ended queue | Fast stack/queue replacement |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The Queue interface has multiple implementations tailored for different use cases.
    
    `PriorityQueue` enables priority-based processing, `LinkedList` provides flexible queue/deque behavior, and `ArrayDeque` offers the most efficient stack and queue operations in modern Java.
    
- **Map Implementations**
    
    # **Map Implementations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Map** in Java stores elements as **key–value pairs**, where:
    
    - **Keys are unique**
    - **Values may be duplicate**
    - Lookups are fast using keys
    
    Java provides several Map implementations, each with different internal data structures and ordering rules:
    
    - **HashMap** → Unordered, hash table
    - **LinkedHashMap** → Insertion-ordered hash table
    - **TreeMap** → Sorted map (Tree-based)
    - **Hashtable** → Legacy synchronized hash table
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Use Maps when you need:
    
    - Fast lookups
    - Unique keys
    - Key-based organization
    - Ordered or sorted mappings (LinkedHashMap, TreeMap)
    - Thread-safe key–value storage (Hashtable, ConcurrentHashMap)
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. HashMap**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `HashMap` is the most commonly used Map.
    
    It stores entries in **hash buckets** and provides **O(1)** average time for `put()`, `get()`, and `remove()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - **Unordered** (no guarantee of iteration order)
    - Allows **one null key** and **multiple null values**
    - Not synchronized (not thread-safe)
    - Backed by **hash table + linked lists** (Java 7)
    - From Java 8: uses **balanced trees (Red-Black trees)** when bucket becomes too large (≥ 8 elements)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Map<Integer, String> map = new HashMap<>();
    map.put(101, "John");
    map.put(102, "Alice");
    map.put(103, "Bob");
    
    System.out.println(map);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - Fastest general-purpose Map
    - Key lookup by `hashCode()`
    - No ordering requirement
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. LinkedHashMap**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `LinkedHashMap` extends HashMap to maintain **insertion order** using a doubly-linked list of entries.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Predictable iteration order
    - Allows one null key
    - Slightly slower than HashMap
    - Supports **access-order** mode for LRU caches
    - Backed by:
        - Hash table
        - Linked list for maintaining order
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, Integer> map = new LinkedHashMap<>();
    map.put("A", 1);
    map.put("B", 2);
    map.put("C", 3);
    
    System.out.println(map);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    {A=1, B=2, C=3}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **LRU Cache Example**
    
    ```java
    LinkedHashMap<Integer, String> lru = new LinkedHashMap<>(16, 0.75f, true);
    
    ```
    
    The `true` enables **access-order**, not insertion-order.
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - When predictable order matters
    - Implementing cache-like structures
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. TreeMap**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `TreeMap` is a **sorted** map implementation backed by a **Red-Black Tree**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Keys stored in **ascending order** (natural or custom)
    - No null key allowed
    - Allows null values
    - Lookup operations: **O(log n)**
    - Provides **NavigableMap methods** like:
        - `higherKey()`, `lowerKey()`
        - `ceilingKey()`, `floorKey()`
        - `firstEntry()`, `lastEntry()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Map<Integer, String> map = new TreeMap<>();
    map.put(30, "A");
    map.put(10, "B");
    map.put(20, "C");
    
    System.out.println(map);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    {10=B, 20=C, 30=A}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Custom Comparator Example**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, Integer> map = new TreeMap<>(Comparator.reverseOrder());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - Need **sorted keys**
    - Performing **range queries**
    - Ordering-sensitive algorithms
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Hashtable**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Hashtable` is a **legacy synchronized hash table** used in early Java.
    
    All methods are **thread-safe** via intrinsic synchronization.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Thread-safe but slow
    - No null keys or null values
    - Replaced by **ConcurrentHashMap** in modern Java
    - Uses hashing but no tree behavior (unlike HashMap after Java 8)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Hashtable<Integer, String> table = new Hashtable<>();
    table.put(1, "A");
    table.put(2, "B");
    
    System.out.println(table);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why It Is Avoided**
    
    - Entire table is locked for each operation → performance bottleneck
    - Outdated design, replaced by modern concurrency utilities
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - Only when working with **very old legacy applications**
    - Better alternative: **ConcurrentHashMap**
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison Table — HashMap vs LinkedHashMap vs TreeMap vs Hashtable**
    
    | Feature | HashMap | LinkedHashMap | TreeMap | Hashtable |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Ordering** | No | Insertion order (or access order) | Sorted | No |
    | **Null Key** | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ❌ No | ❌ No |
    | **Null Values** | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ✔ Yes | ❌ No |
    | **Thread-safe** | ❌ No | ❌ No | ❌ No | ✔ Yes (synchronized) |
    | **Performance** | O(1) avg | O(1) avg | O(log n) | O(1) avg but slower |
    | **Internal Structure** | Hash table + list/tree | Hash table + linked list | Red-Black Tree | Hash table |
    | **Best Use** | Fast lookup | Predictable order | Sorted keys | Legacy synchronized map |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Map Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **HashMap** | A cabinet with fast access but no specific order |
    | **LinkedHashMap** | A notebook where entries appear in the order you wrote them |
    | **TreeMap** | A dictionary organized alphabetically |
    | **Hashtable** | A safe with a lock — slow but synchronized |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Internal Behavior**
    
    - HashMap uses **hashing + tree bins** (after Java 8) to avoid O(n) linked list attacks.
    - LinkedHashMap uses a **doubly-linked list** for maintaining order.
    - TreeMap stores entries using **Red-Black Tree balancing** → predictable O(log n) operations.
    - Hashtable synchronizes every method → heavy locking.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Prefer **HashMap** for general-purpose key–value storage.
    - Use **LinkedHashMap** when order is required or for LRU caching.
    - Use **TreeMap** when sorting or navigation is needed.
    - Avoid **Hashtable** — replaced by **ConcurrentHashMap**.
    - Map keys must implement **equals()** and **hashCode()** correctly (except TreeMap, which uses Compare).
    - Memory usage differs across implementations (TreeMap has higher overhead).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Map Implementation | Key Feature | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **HashMap** | Fast, unordered | General key-value storage |
    | **LinkedHashMap** | Maintains insertion order | Ordered mappings, caches |
    | **TreeMap** | Sorted | Range queries & sorted keys |
    | **Hashtable** | Synchronized | Legacy thread-safe apps |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides versatile Map implementations for various ordering, sorting, and concurrency needs.
    
    Choosing the right Map improves performance, readability, and maintainability of Java applications.
    
- **Collections Utility Class**
    
    # **Collections Utility Class**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`Collections`** class in the `java.util` package is a utility class that provides **static methods** for performing common operations on **Collection**, **List**, **Set**, and **Map** objects.
    
    It offers algorithms, wrappers, and helpers such as sorting, searching, synchronization, and immutability.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Standardized reusable algorithms
    - Thread-safe wrappers
    - Read-only (“unmodifiable”) collection wrappers
    - Utility helpers for common operations
    
    The `Collections` class works on **existing collections**, unlike `Arrays` which works on array data.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Sorting: `sort()`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `Collections.sort()` method sorts a **List** in ascending order either by:
    
    - Natural ordering (`Comparable`)
    - A provided `Comparator`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Collections.sort(List<T> list);
    Collections.sort(List<T> list, Comparator<? super T> c);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example – Natural Order**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> numbers = Arrays.asList(3, 1, 4, 2);
    Collections.sort(numbers);
    System.out.println(numbers);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    [1, 2, 3, 4]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example – Custom Comparator**
    
    ```java
    Collections.sort(numbers, (a, b) -> b - a);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Internal Behavior**
    
    - Uses **Merge Sort (TimSort)** → stable, O(n log n)
    - Does **not** work on Sets or Maps directly (must convert to List)
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Searching: `binarySearch()`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Performs a **binary search** on a **sorted List** and returns the index of the search key.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Collections.binarySearch(List<? extends Comparable> list, T key);
    Collections.binarySearch(List<T> list, T key, Comparator<? super T> c);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Rule**
    
    The list **must be sorted** using the same comparator as binarySearch.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> numbers = Arrays.asList(1, 3, 5, 7, 9);
    int index = Collections.binarySearch(numbers, 5);
    System.out.println(index);
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    2
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **If Element Not Found**
    
    Returns:
    
    `(-(insertion_point) - 1)`
    
    Example: Searching for `6` in `[1,3,5,7,9]` returns `-4`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Performance**
    
    O(log n) search time.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Synchronization Wrappers**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Makes non-thread-safe collections **thread-safe** by returning a **synchronized wrapper** around them.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Collections.synchronizedList(list)
    Collections.synchronizedSet(set)
    Collections.synchronizedMap(map)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> list = new ArrayList<>();
    List<Integer> syncList = Collections.synchronizedList(list);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Usage Note**
    
    For safe iteration:
    
    ```java
    synchronized(syncList) {
        for (int i : syncList) { }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Use?**
    
    - For simple synchronization in multi-threaded environments
    - Replacement to older classes like `Vector` or `Hashtable`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Caution**
    
    These wrappers synchronize **on the whole collection**, which may cause contention.
    
    For highly concurrent environments, prefer:
    
    - `ConcurrentHashMap`
    - `CopyOnWriteArrayList`
    - `ConcurrentLinkedQueue`
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Unmodifiable Wrappers**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Creates a **read-only view** of a collection.
    
    Any modification attempt results in `UnsupportedOperationException`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Collections.unmodifiableList(list)
    Collections.unmodifiableSet(set)
    Collections.unmodifiableMap(map)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = new ArrayList<>();
    list.add("A");
    list.add("B");
    
    List<String> unmodList = Collections.unmodifiableList(list);
    
    System.out.println(unmodList.get(0)); // OK
    unmodList.add("C"); // Throws exception
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Notes**
    
    - Underlying collection is still **modifiable**.
    - Changes to original collection appear in wrapper.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Making API return values safer
    - Preventing clients from modifying internal data
    - Creating read-only configurations
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Additional `Collections` Utility Methods**
    
    | Method | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | `reverse(list)` | Reverses order |
    | `shuffle(list)` | Randomizes list |
    | `fill(list, value)` | Overwrites all elements |
    | `frequency(collection, obj)` | Counts occurrences |
    | `min()` / `max()` | Finds min/max elements |
    | `disjoint(a, b)` | Checks if collections share no common elements |
    | `singleton(value)` | Creates an immutable single-item set |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **sort()** | Organizing books alphabetically |
    | **binarySearch()** | Searching a word in an alphabetized dictionary |
    | **Synchronization wrapper** | Having a guard lock a door before entry |
    | **Unmodifiable wrapper** | Display case items—can view but cannot touch |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - `sort()` uses **TimSort**, optimized for partially sorted data
    - `binarySearch()` requires sorted lists; otherwise, results are undefined
    - Synchronized wrappers use **intrinsic locks** on the wrapper object
    - Unmodifiable wrappers throw exceptions at modification methods only (not read operations)
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Always sort before `binarySearch()` for correct results
    - Synchronized wrappers do not make iterators thread-safe without manual locking
    - Unmodifiable wrappers protect structure but underlying data may still change
    - Prefer modern concurrency classes over synchronization wrappers for performance
    - `Collections` works only on **Collection** types; for arrays, use `Arrays` class
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Function | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **sort()** | Sorts lists | Ordering data |
    | **binarySearch()** | Searches sorted lists | Fast key lookups |
    | **Synchronization wrappers** | Thread-safe collections | Basic concurrency |
    | **Unmodifiable wrappers** | Read-only views | Safe API returns |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The `Collections` utility class provides essential algorithms and wrappers for operating on collections efficiently, safely, and conveniently. It enhances the capabilities of Java’s Collection Framework by offering sorting, searching, synchronization, and immutability options.
    
- **Modern Collection Features** (**NEW**)
    - **Collections Factory Methods** (Java 9+): `List.of()`, `Set.of()`, `Map.of()`, `Map.ofEntries()`
        
        ## 1. Collections Factory Methods (Java 9+)
        
        Java 9 introduced **convenient static factory methods** to create **immutable collections**.
        
        Covered methods:
        
        - `List.of()`
        - `Set.of()`
        - `Map.of()`
        - `Map.ofEntries()`
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.1 `List.of()`
        
        ### Definition and Explanation
        
        `List.of()` is a **static factory method** used to create an **immutable List**.
        
        Once created:
        
        - Elements cannot be added
        - Elements cannot be removed
        - Elements cannot be modified
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        List<E> list = List.of(E... elements);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Rules and Notes
        
        - Does **not allow null elements**
        - Throws `NullPointerException` if any element is null
        - Throws `UnsupportedOperationException` on modification
        - Preserves insertion order
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.List;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                List<String> names = List.of("A","B","C");
                System.out.println(names);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        [A, B, C]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Collection implementation is internal and optimized
        - JVM enforces immutability at runtime
        - No defensive copying required
        
        ---
        
        ### Real-world Analogy
        
        A printed exam question paper:
        
        - Read-only
        - Cannot be altered
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.2 `Set.of()`
        
        ### Definition and Explanation
        
        `Set.of()` creates an **immutable Set** with **unique elements**.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        Set<E> set = Set.of(E... elements);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Rules and Notes
        
        - Duplicate elements cause `IllegalArgumentException`
        - Null values not allowed
        - Order is not guaranteed
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.Set;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                Set<Integer> nums = Set.of(10,20,30);
                System.out.println(nums);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Hash-based internal structure
        - Duplicate detection at creation time
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.3 `Map.of()` and `Map.ofEntries()`
        
        ---
        
        ### `Map.of()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Creates an immutable map with **up to 10 key-value pairs**.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        Map<K, V> map = Map.of(K1, V1, K2, V2, ...);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.Map;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                Map<Integer, String> map = Map.of(
        1,"One",
        2,"Two"
                );
                System.out.println(map);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Rules
        
        - Duplicate keys not allowed
        - Null keys or values not allowed
        - Immutable map
        
        ---
        
        ### `Map.ofEntries()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Used when **more than 10 entries** are required.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        Map<K, V> map = Map.ofEntries(
            Map.entry(K1, V1),
            Map.entry(K2, V2)
        );
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        Map<Integer, String> map = Map.ofEntries(
            Map.entry(1,"A"),
            Map.entry(2,"B"),
            Map.entry(3,"C")
        );
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.4 Factory Methods vs Traditional Collections
        
        | Aspect | Traditional | Factory Methods |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Mutability | Mutable | Immutable |
        | Null allowed | Yes | No |
        | Readability | Verbose | Clean |
        | Safety | Lower | High |
    - **Enhanced Collection Methods** (Java 8+): `removeIf()`, `replaceAll()`, `computeIfAbsent()`, `merge()`, `getOrDefault()`
        
        ## 2. Enhanced Collection Methods (Java 8+)
        
        Java 8 added **default methods** to collection interfaces, enabling **functional-style operations**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.1 `removeIf()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Removes elements that satisfy a given predicate.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        booleanremoveIf(Predicate<? super E> filter)
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.*;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                List<Integer> list =newArrayList<>(List.of(1,2,3,4));
                list.removeIf(n -> n %2 ==0);
                System.out.println(list);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        [1, 3]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Iteration handled internally
        - Safe removal without `ConcurrentModificationException`
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.2 `replaceAll()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Replaces each element using a unary operator.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        voidreplaceAll(UnaryOperator<E> operator)
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        List<Integer> list =newArrayList<>(List.of(1,2,3));
        list.replaceAll(n -> n *10);
        System.out.println(list);
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        [10, 20, 30]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.3 `getOrDefault()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Returns value for key if present, otherwise returns default value.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        VgetOrDefault(Object key, V defaultValue)
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        Map<Integer, String> map =newHashMap<>();
        map.put(1,"Java");
        
        System.out.println(map.getOrDefault(2,"Default"));
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        Default
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.4 `computeIfAbsent()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Computes value only if key is absent.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        VcomputeIfAbsent(K key, Function<? super K, ? extends V> mappingFunction)
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        Map<String, Integer> map =newHashMap<>();
        
        map.computeIfAbsent("A", k ->100);
        System.out.println(map);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Function executed lazily
        - Avoids unnecessary object creation
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.5 `merge()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Merges values for a key using a remapping function.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        Vmerge(K key, V value, BiFunction<V, V, V> remappingFunction)
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        Map<String, Integer> map =newHashMap<>();
        map.put("A",10);
        
        map.merge("A",5, Integer::sum);
        System.out.println(map);
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        {A=15}
        
        ```
        
    - **Stream conversion**: `collection.stream()`, `Stream.toList()` (Java 16+)
        
        ## 3. Stream Conversion and Collection Integration
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.1 `collection.stream()`
        
        ### Definition
        
        Converts a collection into a sequential Stream.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        Stream<E>stream()
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        List<String> list = List.of("a","bb","ccc");
        
        list.stream()
            .filter(s -> s.length() >1)
            .forEach(System.out::println);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Lazy evaluation
        - Operations executed only on terminal operation
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.2 `Stream.toList()` (Java 16+)
        
        ---
        
        ### Definition
        
        Collects stream elements into an **immutable List**.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        List<T>toList()
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        List<Integer> list = List.of(1,2,3,4);
        
        List<Integer> even =
            list.stream()
                .filter(n -> n %2 ==0)
                .toList();
        
        System.out.println(even);
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        [2, 4]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Difference: `Collectors.toList()` vs `toList()`
        
        | Aspect | Collectors.toList() | Stream.toList() |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Mutability | Usually mutable | Immutable |
        | Java version | 8+ | 16+ |
        | Guarantees | None | Unmodifiable |
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Important Points
        
        - Factory methods always return **immutable collections**
        - Enhanced methods reduce boilerplate loops
        - Stream API promotes functional programming
        - `toList()` is preferred for immutable results
        - Nulls are not allowed in factory-created collections
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Summary
        
        Modern Java collections provide **safer, cleaner, and more expressive APIs**.
        
        Factory methods enforce immutability, enhanced methods simplify common operations, and stream conversions enable functional-style data processing—making Java code **more readable, reliable, and enterprise-ready**.
        
- **Specialized Collections** (**NEW**)
    - **EnumSet** and **EnumMap**
        
        ## 1. EnumSet
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        `EnumSet` is a **specialized Set implementation** designed **exclusively for enum types**.
        
        It internally uses **bit vectors**, making it **extremely fast and memory-efficient** compared to other `Set` implementations.
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Store enum constants efficiently
        - Replace flag-based logic
        - Improve performance and readability
        
        ---
        
        ### Simple Understanding
        
        A super-fast set made **only for enums**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.2 Syntax
        
        ```java
        EnumSet<EextendsEnum<E>>
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.3 Rules and Notes
        
        - Only enum types are allowed
        - Null elements are **not permitted**
        - Order is the **natural order of enum declaration**
        - Much faster than `HashSet`
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.4 Example
        
        ```java
        enumDay {
            MON, TUE, WED, THU, FRI, SAT, SUN
        }
        
        import java.util.EnumSet;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                EnumSet<Day> workingDays =
                        EnumSet.of(Day.MON, Day.TUE, Day.WED, Day.THU, Day.FRI);
        
                System.out.println(workingDays);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        [MON, TUE, WED, THU, FRI]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.5 JVM / Internal Behavior
        
        - Internally implemented using **bit masks**
        - No hashing involved
        - Extremely low memory footprint
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.6 Real-world Analogy
        
        Switch panel with predefined switches:
        
        - Each switch is either ON or OFF
        - Very fast to check status
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.7 Important Points
        
        - Preferred over `HashSet<Enum>`
        - Used in configuration flags
        - Highly optimized and type-safe
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. EnumMap
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        `EnumMap` is a **Map implementation** where:
        
        - Keys must be enum constants
        - Internally backed by an **array**
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Fast key-value mapping for enums
        - Replace `HashMap<Enum, V>`
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.2 Syntax
        
        ```java
        EnumMap<KextendsEnum<K>, V>
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.3 Rules and Notes
        
        - Null keys are **not allowed**
        - Null values are allowed
        - Maintains enum declaration order
        - Faster than `HashMap`
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.4 Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.EnumMap;
        
        enumLevel {
            LOW, MEDIUM, HIGH
        }
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                EnumMap<Level, String> map =newEnumMap<>(Level.class);
        
                map.put(Level.LOW,"Low Risk");
                map.put(Level.HIGH,"High Risk");
        
                System.out.println(map);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        {LOW=Low Risk,HIGH=High Risk}
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.5 JVM / Internal Behavior
        
        - Uses ordinal values of enum
        - Array index-based access
        - No hashing or collision handling
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.6 EnumMap vs HashMap
        
        | Aspect | EnumMap | HashMap |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Key type | Enum only | Any object |
        | Performance | Very high | General |
        | Ordering | Enum order | No order |
        | Memory | Low | Higher |
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.7 Important Points
        
        - Ideal for state machines
        - Common in frameworks and configuration maps
    - **WeakHashMap** for cache implementations
        
        ## 3. WeakHashMap (Cache-Oriented Map)
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        `WeakHashMap` stores keys as **weak references**.
        
        If a key is **no longer strongly referenced elsewhere**, it becomes eligible for **garbage collection**, and the entry is automatically removed.
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Memory-sensitive caches
        - Avoid memory leaks
        - Temporary object mapping
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.2 Simple Understanding
        
        Entries disappear **automatically** when keys are no longer used.
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.3 Syntax
        
        ```java
        WeakHashMap<K, V>
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.4 Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.WeakHashMap;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                WeakHashMap<Object, String> map =newWeakHashMap<>();
        
        Objectkey=newObject();
                map.put(key,"Cached Value");
        
                key =null;
                System.gc();
        
                System.out.println(map);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Possible Output
        
        ```
        {}
        
        ```
        
        (Note: GC behavior is JVM-dependent)
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.5 JVM / GC Behavior
        
        - Keys stored as `WeakReference`
        - GC removes entries automatically
        - Cleanup happens during GC cycles
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.6 Real-world Analogy
        
        Temporary notes:
        
        - Disappear when no one refers to them
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.7 WeakHashMap vs HashMap
        
        | Aspect | WeakHashMap | HashMap |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Key reference | Weak | Strong |
        | GC effect | Removes entries | No effect |
        | Use case | Cache | Permanent storage |
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.8 Important Points
        
        - Not suitable for critical data
        - GC timing is unpredictable
        - Used internally in frameworks
    - **IdentityHashMap** for identity-based keys
        
        ## 4. IdentityHashMap
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        `IdentityHashMap` compares keys using:
        
        ```
        ==  (reference equality)
        
        ```
        
        instead of:
        
        ```
        equals()
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Identity-based key comparison
        - Low-level framework and JVM internals
        - Special-case maps
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.2 Simple Understanding
        
        Keys are equal **only if they are the same object in memory**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.3 Syntax
        
        ```java
        IdentityHashMap<K, V>
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.4 Example
        
        ```java
        import java.util.IdentityHashMap;
        
        publicclassTest {
        publicstaticvoidmain(String[] args) {
                IdentityHashMap<String, Integer> map =newIdentityHashMap<>();
        
        Strings1=newString("Java");
        Strings2=newString("Java");
        
                map.put(s1,1);
                map.put(s2,2);
        
                System.out.println(map.size());
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        2
        
        ```
        
        (`HashMap` would output `1`)
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.5 JVM / Internal Behavior
        
        - Uses `==` for comparison
        - Uses `System.identityHashCode()`
        - Completely ignores `equals()` and `hashCode()`
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.6 Real-world Analogy
        
        Two people with same name:
        
        - Treated as different individuals
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.7 IdentityHashMap vs HashMap
        
        | Aspect | IdentityHashMap | HashMap |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Equality check | `==` | `equals()` |
        | Hashing | Identity hash | Object hash |
        | Use case | Framework internals | General |
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Important Points (Interview Focus)
        
        - Use `EnumSet` and `EnumMap` for enum-based logic
        - `WeakHashMap` is GC-driven and non-deterministic
        - `IdentityHashMap` breaks normal Map contracts intentionally
        - These collections solve **specific problems only**
        - Avoid misuse in general-purpose code
        
        ---
        
        ## 6. Summary
        
        `EnumSet` and `EnumMap` provide **high-performance enum handling**,
        
        `WeakHashMap` enables **memory-sensitive caching**,
        
        and `IdentityHashMap` supports **identity-based key semantics**.
        
        These specialized collections are **powerful tools** when used correctly and **dangerous when misused**, making design understanding essential for professional Java development.
        
- **Collection Performance & Pitfalls** (**NEW**)
    - **Concurrent Modification Exception**: causes and prevention
    - **Fail-fast vs Fail-safe iterators**
    - **Collection initial capacity** guidelines
    - **Choosing the right collection** decision matrix

### 3.3 Exception Handling

- **Exception Hierarchy**
    
    # **Exception Hierarchy**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **exception** in Java represents an abnormal event that disrupts the normal flow of a program.
    
    Java provides a structured exception hierarchy rooted at the **Throwable** class, dividing abnormal conditions into:
    
    - **Errors** → Serious issues that applications should not handle
    - **Exceptions** → Conditions that applications can potentially recover from
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide a structured mechanism for error handling
    - Allow programs to continue execution or fail gracefully
    - Enable clean separation of normal logic and exceptional handling
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Throwable Class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Throwable` is the root class of all exceptions and errors in Java.
    
    It defines the common behavior for all throwable objects.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Throwable Hierarchy**
    
    ```
                     Throwable
                    /         \
                Error        Exception
                               /     \
                     Checked Exceptions  RuntimeException (Unchecked)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Methods (from Throwable)**
    
    - `getMessage()` → returns exception message
    - `printStackTrace()` → prints error details
    - `getCause()` → returns cause of exception
    - `toString()` → brief description
    
    ---
    
    ### **Two Major Subclasses**
    
    1. **Error**
    2. **Exception**
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Error vs Exception**
    
    ---
    
    ## **Error**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Errors represent **serious system-level problems** that applications usually cannot handle or recover from.
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    - `OutOfMemoryError`
    - `StackOverflowError`
    - `VirtualMachineError`
    - `NoClassDefFoundError`
    - `ThreadDeath`
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Unchecked (do not need to be declared or caught)
    - Caused by JVM or environment
    - Program should *not* attempt to recover
    - Indicates fundamental problem (memory, JVM crash, hardware issues)
    
    ---
    
    ## **Exception**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Exceptions represent conditions that applications **can handle** or **recover from**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Two types of exceptions:**
    
    1. **Checked Exceptions**
    2. **Unchecked Exceptions (Runtime Exceptions)**
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Checked vs Unchecked Exceptions**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Checked Exceptions**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Checked exceptions are exceptions that must be either:
    
    - **Handled** using `try-catch`, or
    - **Declared** using `throws` in the method signature.
    
    These are checked **at compile time**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    - `IOException`
    - `SQLException`
    - `ClassNotFoundException`
    - `FileNotFoundException`
    - `ParseException`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Compiler enforces mandatory handling
    - Usually external failures (I/O, DB, network)
    - Program can meaningfully recover
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public void readFile() throws IOException {
        FileReader fr = new FileReader("data.txt");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Unchecked Exceptions (Runtime Exceptions)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Unchecked exceptions occur due to **programming errors** and are detected at **runtime**.
    
    They do **not** need to be declared or caught.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    - `NullPointerException`
    - `ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException`
    - `ArithmeticException`
    - `ClassCastException`
    - `IllegalArgumentException`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Not checked by the compiler
    - Typically caused by bugs
    - Should be prevented by proper validation
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int result = 10 / 0; // ArithmeticException
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Full Exception Hierarchy Diagram**
    
    ```
    Throwable
    ├── Error
    │    ├── OutOfMemoryError
    │    ├── StackOverflowError
    │    ├── NoClassDefFoundError
    │    └── AssertionError
    │
    └── Exception
         ├── RuntimeException
         │     ├── NullPointerException
         │     ├── ArithmeticException
         │     ├── IndexOutOfBoundsException
         │     ├── IllegalArgumentException
         │     └── ClassCastException
         │
         ├── IOException
         │     ├── FileNotFoundException
         │     └── EOFException
         │
         ├── SQLException
         ├── ClassNotFoundException
         ├── ParseException
         └── InterruptedException
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Error** | Car engine failure — cannot be fixed during trip |
    | **Checked Exception** | Flat tire — can be repaired or handled |
    | **Unchecked Exception** | Driver mistake — avoidable with careful behavior |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Compiler **enforces** handling of checked exceptions.
    - Unchecked exceptions propagate automatically during runtime.
    - Errors generally terminate the program or thread.
    - All exceptions create a **stack trace object** stored by JVM.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - `Throwable` is the top-most type for all exceptions and errors.
    - Errors should **not** be caught except for logging.
    - Checked exceptions must be **declared or handled**.
    - Unchecked exceptions indicate **bad code**, not external failures.
    - Use custom exceptions by extending `Exception` or `RuntimeException`.
    - Avoid overusing checked exceptions; it can clutter code.
    - Throw `RuntimeException` for programming errors; use checked exceptions for recoverable failures.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Category | Compile-time Checked? | Recoverable? | Examples |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Error** | No | No | OutOfMemoryError |
    | **Checked Exception** | Yes | Yes | IOException, SQLException |
    | **Unchecked Exception** | No | Sometimes | NullPointerException |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java's exception hierarchy provides a structured model for handling abnormal events.
    
    Understanding the difference between **Error**, **Checked Exception**, and **Unchecked Exception** is crucial for writing safe, robust, and maintainable Java programs.
    
- **Exception Handling Mechanisms**
    
    # **Exception Handling Mechanisms**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Exception handling** in Java provides a structured mechanism to detect and manage runtime errors without abruptly terminating the program.
    
    Java offers several constructs such as **try-catch**, **multiple catch blocks**, **multi-catch**, **finally**, and **try-with-resources** to handle exceptions cleanly and safely.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Prevent abnormal program termination
    - Provide alternate logic during error conditions
    - Allow cleanup of resources (files, streams, DB connections)
    - Allow propagation and controlled handling of exceptions
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. try-catch Block**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **try-catch** block encloses code that may throw an exception.
    
    If an exception occurs inside the `try` block, control transfers to the matching `catch` block.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try {
        // risky code
    } catch (ExceptionType e) {
        // exception handling code
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        int a = 10 / 0;
    } catch (ArithmeticException e) {
        System.out.println("Cannot divide by zero");
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Cannot divide by zero
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Behavior**
    
    - `try` block must be followed by either `catch` or `finally` (or both).
    - If exception does not occur → catch block is skipped.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Multiple Catch Blocks**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Multiple catch blocks allow handling **different exception types** separately.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try {
        // risky code
    } catch (IOException e) {
        // handle IO issues
    } catch (SQLException e) {
        // handle DB issues
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Rules**
    
    - Order catch blocks from **most specific to most general**.
    - A superclass catch (like `Exception`) must come last.
    - Compiler error occurs if ordering is invalid.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        int[] arr = new int[2];
        arr[5] = 10; // ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException
    } catch (ArithmeticException e) {
        System.out.println("Math error");
    } catch (ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException e) {
        System.out.println("Invalid array index");
    } catch (Exception e) {
        System.out.println("General exception");
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Invalid array index
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Multi-catch Block (Java 7+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Multi-catch allows catching **multiple unrelated exceptions** in a single catch block using the `|` operator.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try {
        // code
    } catch (IOException | SQLException e) {
        // handle both types
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - Exceptions must be **disjoint** (no parent-child relationship).
    - Catch parameter becomes **effectively final**—cannot be reassigned.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        String s = null;
        s.length();
    } catch (NullPointerException | IllegalArgumentException e) {
        System.out.println("Exception occurred");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. finally Block**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `finally` defines a block that **always executes**, regardless of whether exception occurs or not.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try {
        // risky code
    } catch (Exception e) {
        // handling
    } finally {
        // cleanup code
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Closing database connections
    - Releasing file handles
    - Closing network streams
    - Releasing locks
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        int x = 10 / 5;
    } finally {
        System.out.println("Cleanup completed");
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Cleanup completed
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Behaviors to Note**
    
    - Executes even if `catch` does not run
    - Executes even if exception is not caught
    - Executes even if method returns:
    
    ```java
    try {
        return;
    } finally {
        System.out.println("Executed");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Exception:**
    
    `finally` may not execute when:
    
    - JVM crashes
    - `System.exit()` is called
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. try-with-resources (Java 7+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A try-with-resources block automatically **closes resources** (objects implementing `AutoCloseable` or `Closeable`) after use.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Avoid resource leaks
    - Reduce boilerplate code
    - Automatically invokes `close()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try (ResourceType r = new ResourceType()) {
        // use resource
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (FileReader fr = new FileReader("data.txt");
         BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(fr)) {
    
        System.out.println(br.readLine());
    
    } catch (IOException e) {
        e.printStackTrace();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Advantages**
    
    - No need for manual closing in `finally`
    - Multiple resources can be declared
    - Cleaner, readable code
    
    ---
    
    ### **Behavior (Suppressed Exceptions)**
    
    - If both `try` and `close()` throw exceptions:
        - Primary exception = try block exception
        - Closing exception becomes **suppressed**
    - Retrieve using:
    
    ```java
    e.getSuppressed()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Java 9 Enhancement**
    
    Resources created outside can also be used:
    
    ```java
    FileReader fr = new FileReader("data.txt");
    try (fr) {
        // readable block using fr
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **try-catch** | Try doing a task, handle failure if it occurs |
    | **Multiple catch** | Different reactions to different types of failures |
    | **Multi-catch** | One generic action for several related issues |
    | **finally** | Always clean the workspace after finishing |
    | **try-with-resources** | Automatically closing a tap after use |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Compiler checks catch block order.
    - `try-with-resources` calls `close()` using hidden `finally` logic.
    - Multi-catch prevents redundant code and improves readability.
    - Catch block variable is treated as effectively final.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - A `try` block cannot exist alone; must have catch/finally.
    - Multiple catch blocks allowed; must be ordered carefully.
    - Multi-catch cannot catch superclass/subclass combinations.
    - `finally` always executes unless JVM exits abnormally.
    - try-with-resources is preferred for handling IO, JDBC, files.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Mechanism | Purpose | Key Feature |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **try-catch** | Handle exceptions | Separate normal/exceptional code |
    | **Multiple catch** | Handle specific exceptions | Ordered from specific → general |
    | **Multi-catch** | Combine catches | Uses ` |
    | **finally** | Cleanup operations | Always executes |
    | **try-with-resources** | Auto manage resources | Closes automatically |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides robust exception-handling mechanisms to gracefully handle failures, ensure reliable cleanup, and avoid resource leaks. These constructs help write safe, maintainable, and error-resilient applications.
    
- **Throwing Exceptions**
    
    # **Throwing Exceptions**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java provides mechanisms to **manually throw exceptions** using the `throw` keyword and to **declare** exceptions using the `throws` clause.
    
    Developers can also create **custom exception classes** to represent domain-specific or application-specific error conditions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - To signal and propagate error conditions intentionally
    - To enforce method contracts
    - To create meaningful and descriptive error types
    - To improve debugging and clarity of error flow
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. The `throw` Keyword**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `throw` keyword is used to **explicitly throw an exception object** at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    throw new ExceptionType("message");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Usage Rules**
    
    - Only a single exception can be thrown per `throw` statement.
    - `throw` must be used with an instance of `Throwable` or its subclasses.
    - If throwing a **checked exception**, it must be handled or declared in the method.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Throwing an Unchecked Exception**
    
    ```java
    public void validateAge(int age) {
        if (age < 18) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Age must be 18 or above");
        }
        System.out.println("Valid age");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Throwing a Checked Exception**
    
    ```java
    public void readData() throws IOException {
        throw new IOException("Unable to read file");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. The `throws` Clause**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `throws` clause declares that a method may throw **checked exceptions**, signaling to calling methods that handling is required.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public void methodName() throws Exception1, Exception2 {
        // method body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - To **propagate** checked exceptions to the caller
    - To avoid handling exceptions directly within the method
    - To clearly define the method's exception contract
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Declaring Checked Exceptions**
    
    ```java
    public void connectToDB() throws SQLException {
        throw new SQLException("Connection failed");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Multiple Exception Declaration**
    
    ```java
    public void process() throws IOException, SQLException {
        // risky operations
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Points**
    
    - `throws` does not throw the exception—`throw` does.
    - Used only for checked exceptions (unchecked are optional).
    - Calling method must handle or declare the same exceptions.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Creating Custom Exceptions**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Custom exceptions are user-defined exception classes tailored to represent specific business or application errors.
    
    They are created by extending:
    
    - `Exception` → for checked exceptions
    - `RuntimeException` → for unchecked exceptions
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improve readability with meaningful exception types
    - Capture domain-specific error scenarios
    - Provide descriptive error messages
    
    ---
    
    ### **Steps to Create a Custom Exception**
    
    1. Create a class extending `Exception` or `RuntimeException`
    2. Provide constructors
    3. Optionally override useful methods
    
    ---
    
    ### **4.1 Custom Checked Exception Example**
    
    ```java
    public class InvalidAmountException extends Exception {
    
        public InvalidAmountException(String message) {
            super(message);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Usage**
    
    ```java
    public void withdraw(double amount) throws InvalidAmountException {
        if (amount <= 0) {
            throw new InvalidAmountException("Amount must be positive");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **4.2 Custom Unchecked Exception Example**
    
    ```java
    public class UserNotFoundException extends RuntimeException {
    
        public UserNotFoundException(String message) {
            super(message);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Usage**
    
    ```java
    public User getUser(String id) {
        if (id == null) {
            throw new UserNotFoundException("User ID cannot be null");
        }
        return new User(id);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Difference Between `throw` and `throws`**
    
    | Feature | `throw` | `throws` |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Purpose** | Actually throws an exception | Declares that method may throw exceptions |
    | **Used Inside** | Method body | Method signature |
    | **Throws how many?** | Only one exception at a time | Multiple exceptions can be declared |
    | **Type** | Runtime behavior | Compile-time requirement (for checked exceptions) |
    | **Keyword Role** | Creates/initiates exception | Propagates responsibility |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **throw** | Manually raising a complaint |
    | **throws** | Informing others that you *might* raise a complaint |
    | **Custom Exception** | Creating a specific complaint form for a particular issue |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - When `throw` is executed, JVM skips the remaining code and searches for a matching `catch`.
    - Methods with `throws` in the signature are checked at compile time to ensure callers handle or declare exceptions.
    - Custom exception classes inherit behavior from Throwable hierarchy.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use `throw` to trigger exceptions based on custom logic.
    - Use `throws` to delegate exception handling to callers.
    - Custom exceptions should be meaningful and specific.
    - Checked exceptions should be used for recoverable conditions.
    - Unchecked custom exceptions should represent programming errors.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **throw** | Manually throw an exception |
    | **throws** | Declare exceptions for callers |
    | **Custom Exceptions** | User-defined exception classes for specific conditions |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Throwing exceptions in Java involves using the `throw` keyword for explicit errors, the `throws` clause for checked exception propagation, and custom exception classes for domain-specific error modeling. These mechanisms improve clarity, robustness, and maintainability in error handling.
    
- **Best Practices**
    
    # **Best Practices in Exception Handling**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Exception-handling best practices are guidelines that help write **clean**, **reliable**, and **maintainable** code. They include techniques such as exception translation, proper logging, and correctly choosing between checked and unchecked exceptions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improve debugging through meaningful exceptions
    - Avoid leaking internal implementation details
    - Preserve correct failure behavior
    - Provide clear and actionable error messages
    - Ensure applications remain stable and easy to maintain
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Exception Translation**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Exception Translation** involves catching a low-level exception and throwing a more meaningful, higher-level exception that better matches the context or domain.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Avoid exposing internal details (e.g., SQL exceptions) to upper layers
    - Provide richer, domain-specific error information
    - Convert technical errors to business-readable messages
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        dataAccess.loadUser(id);
    } catch (SQLException e) {
        throw new UserDataAccessException("Failed to load user: " + id, e);
    }
    
    ```
    
    Here:
    
    - SQLException (low-level) is translated into
    - UserDataAccessException (domain-specific)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Clear separation of layers
    - Meaningful errors to upper layers
    - Easier debugging and maintenance
    
    ---
    
    ### **Exception Chaining**
    
    Use constructor that accepts a `cause`:
    
    ```java
    throw new CustomException("message", e);
    
    ```
    
    Preserves original stack trace.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Logging Exceptions**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Logging exceptions means recording error details into logs for debugging, analysis, and monitoring.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Preserve diagnostic information
    - Help identify root causes
    - Monitor failures in production
    - Enable post-mortem debugging
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Practices**
    
    ### **3.1 Log the Exception, Not Just the Message**
    
    ```java
    logger.error("Failed to process request", e);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.2 Avoid Catching and Ignoring Exceptions**
    
    Incorrect:
    
    ```java
    try {
        // risky code
    } catch (Exception e) {
        // ignored
    }
    
    ```
    
    Correct:
    
    ```java
    catch (Exception e) {
        logger.warn("Exception caught", e);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.3 Avoid Double Logging (Log Once Only)**
    
    Do not log and then rethrow repeatedly.
    
    Correct approach:
    
    - Log at *topmost layer* (usually controller/service entry point)
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.4 Include Context**
    
    ```java
    logger.error("Failed to save order: {}", orderId, e);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.5 Choose Appropriate Log Levels**
    
    | Level | Usage |
    | --- | --- |
    | **ERROR** | Serious failures |
    | **WARN** | Recoverable or expected issues |
    | **INFO** | Application flow |
    | **DEBUG** | Detailed debugging information |
    | **TRACE** | Extremely fine-grained events |
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.6 Avoid Printing Stack Trace Directly**
    
    Avoid:
    
    ```java
    e.printStackTrace();
    
    ```
    
    Use logging framework instead.
    
    ---
    
    ### **3.7 Use Logging Frameworks**
    
    - SLF4J
    - Log4j2
    - Logback
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. When to Use Checked vs Unchecked Exceptions**
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.1 Checked Exceptions**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Checked exceptions must be declared or handled.
    
    Use them for **recoverable** conditions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use Checked Exceptions**
    
    - External system failures (file, network, I/O)
    - Operations that *can* recover with alternate action
    - If caller is expected to **handle** the issue
    
    ---
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    - `IOException`
    - `SQLException`
    - `ParseException`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Good Scenario**
    
    ```java
    public void readConfig() throws IOException {
        // caller expected to handle missing file
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.2 Unchecked Exceptions**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Unchecked (runtime) exceptions indicate **programming errors** or conditions that callers cannot handle.
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use Unchecked Exceptions**
    
    - Invalid arguments → `IllegalArgumentException`
    - Null references → `NullPointerException`
    - Incorrect state → `IllegalStateException`
    - Index issues → `IndexOutOfBoundsException`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Unchecked Exceptions When**
    
    - Problem is unavoidable by caller
    - Caller cannot meaningfully recover
    - It indicates **logic error**, not environment failure
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public void setAge(int age) {
        if (age < 0) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Age cannot be negative");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Combined Best Practices**
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.1 Throw Meaningful Exceptions**
    
    Avoid:
    
    ```java
    throw new Exception("Error");
    
    ```
    
    Prefer:
    
    ```java
    throw new InvalidOrderException("Order ID is missing");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.2 Do Not Reveal Sensitive Information**
    
    Avoid exposing:
    
    - File paths
    - SQL queries
    - Internal architecture details
    
    Use safe messages.
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.3 Keep Exception Messages Clear**
    
    Include:
    
    - What failed
    - Why it failed
    - Context (ID, user input)
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.4 Clean Up Resources**
    
    Use:
    
    - try-with-resources
    - finally blocks
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.5 Avoid Overuse of Checked Exceptions**
    
    Too many checked exceptions lead to:
    
    - Verbose code
    - Exception pollution
    
    Use sparingly.
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.6 Never Swallow Exceptions**
    
    Swallowed exceptions hide actual problems.
    
    ---
    
    ### **5.7 Use Custom Exceptions for Domain Logic**
    
    Helps create clean layers:
    
    - Service exceptions
    - Validation exceptions
    - Business rule exceptions
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Exception Translation** | A customer support agent translating a technical issue into customer-friendly language |
    | **Logging Exceptions** | Keeping a record book of all past incidents |
    | **Checked Exceptions** | Issues you *must* fix before continuing (e.g., missing documents) |
    | **Unchecked Exceptions** | Mistakes made due to wrong input or logic |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Compiler forces checked exceptions to be declared or handled.
    - Logging frameworks buffer and format messages efficiently.
    - Exception chaining preserves original stack trace for debugging.
    - Unchecked exceptions propagate automatically through the stack.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Log exceptions with stack trace for debugging.
    - Do not use exceptions for normal control flow.
    - Use checked exceptions sparingly but meaningfully.
    - Translate exceptions at layer boundaries.
    - Create custom exceptions for domain-specific issues.
    - Use try-with-resources to avoid resource leaks.
    - Avoid leaking internal system details in exception messages.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Best Practice | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Exception Translation** | Convert low-level errors to domain-level exceptions |
    | **Logging Exceptions** | Preserve error history for debugging |
    | **Checked Exceptions** | Use for recoverable issues |
    | **Unchecked Exceptions** | Use for programming or validation errors |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Effective exception handling requires meaningful exception types, clean propagation, proper logging, and choosing the correct exception type based on context. These practices help build maintainable and robust Java applications.
    
- **Advanced Exception Handling**
    
    # **Advanced Exception Handling**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Advanced exception handling features provide deeper control over exception diagnostics, stack-trace analysis, exception propagation, and performance management.
    
    Several modern Java capabilities enhance debugging and error visibility, including:
    
    - **Exception Chaining**
    - **Suppressed Exceptions** (from try-with-resources)
    - **Stack Walking API** (Java 9+)
    - Enhanced **@Deprecated** annotation
    - Awareness of **exception performance costs**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide more informative failure analysis
    - Allow better root-cause tracing
    - Improve clarity and maintainability of error handling
    - Optimize exceptions usage in performance-critical applications
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Exception Chaining and `initCause()`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Exception Chaining** links a higher-level exception to its original cause, preserving the full error path.
    
    This enables better debugging and aligns with layered application design.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Constructor-based Chaining**
    
    Most exception classes support chaining via:
    
    ```java
    new Exception("Message", cause);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using `initCause()`**
    
    `initCause()` allows associating a cause **after** creating the exception.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Exception ex = new Exception("Higher-level error");
    ex.initCause(originalException);
    throw ex;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - `initCause()` can be called **only once**
    - Throws `IllegalStateException` if cause already initialized
    - Used rarely, but useful for legacy exception types without cause constructors
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try {
        riskyOperation();
    } catch (SQLException e) {
        IOException io = new IOException("Failed to process DB data");
        io.initCause(e);
        throw io;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose of Chaining**
    
    - Preserve full stack trace
    - Prevent information loss
    - Enable layered exception translation
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Suppressed Exceptions in Try-with-Resources**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    When using **try-with-resources**, if:
    
    - the `try` block throws an exception, **and**
    - the resource’s `close()` method also throws an exception,
    
    Java stores the closing exception as a **suppressed exception** so that the primary exception is not lost.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Retrieving Suppressed Exceptions**
    
    ```java
    Throwable[] suppressed = exception.getSuppressed();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (MyResource r = new MyResource()) {
        throw new RuntimeException("Main failure");
    } catch (Exception e) {
        for (Throwable s : e.getSuppressed()) {
            System.out.println("Suppressed: " + s);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why This Matters**
    
    - Ensures the **primary exception** is prioritized
    - Still retains important cleanup-phase failure information
    - Improves error debugging in resource-heavy operations
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Stack Walking API (Java 9+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **Stack Walking API** provides a programmable way to traverse the call stack with more control than `Throwable.getStackTrace()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Classes**
    
    - `StackWalker`
    - `StackWalker.StackFrame`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Efficient stack inspection
    - On-demand frame access
    - Lazily processed stack frames (better performance)
    - Access to class and method metadata
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating a StackWalker**
    
    ```java
    StackWalker walker = StackWalker.getInstance();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Printing Method Names**
    
    ```java
    StackWalker.getInstance()
               .forEach(frame -> System.out.println(frame.getMethodName()));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Filter Stack Frames**
    
    ```java
    String caller = StackWalker.getInstance()
            .walk(frames -> frames
                .skip(1)
                .findFirst()
                .map(StackWalker.StackFrame::getClassName)
                .orElse("Unknown"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Advantages Over getStackTrace()**
    
    | Feature | Old API (`getStackTrace`) | StackWalker API |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Performance | Slower | Faster (lazy) |
    | Control | Full array only | Stream-like navigation |
    | Metadata access | Limited | Method/class info |
    | Filtering | Manual | Built-in |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. @Deprecated Annotation (Enhanced in Java 9+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **@Deprecated** annotation marks elements that should not be used, typically because they are unsafe or replaced.
    
    Java 9 added two optional parameters:
    
    ```java
    @Deprecated(since = "version", forRemoval = true/false)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Parameters**
    
    | Parameter | Meaning |
    | --- | --- |
    | **since** | Version when element was deprecated |
    | **forRemoval** | Indicates whether element will be removed in future |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @Deprecated(since = "11", forRemoval = true)
    public void oldMethod() { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **IDE & Compiler Benefits**
    
    - Issues warnings
    - Helps developers identify deprecated APIs
    - Signals planned removal
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Practices**
    
    - Always specify **since** for clarity
    - Set `forRemoval=true` when replacing APIs
    - Provide alternative method in Javadoc
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Exception Performance Costs**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Throwing exceptions is **expensive** in Java due to:
    
    - Stack trace creation
    - Object creation
    - Stack unwinding
    - Garbage collection pressure
    
    ---
    
    ### **Performance Cost Breakdown**
    
    1. Creating exception object
    2. Capturing stack trace (heavy operation)
    3. JVM unwinds call stack
    4. Control is transferred to handler
    5. Allocation of exception object triggers garbage collection
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Guidelines**
    
    ### **6.1 Do NOT Use Exceptions for Control Flow**
    
    Incorrect:
    
    ```java
    try {
        findUser();
    } catch (UserNotFoundException e) {
        // normal behavior
    }
    
    ```
    
    Correct:
    
    ```java
    if (!existsUser()) {
        // handle normally
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **6.2 Avoid Throwing Exceptions in Hot Loops**
    
    Poor practice:
    
    ```java
    for (...) {
        if (invalid) throw new RuntimeException();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **6.3 Avoid Creating Stack Trace for Performance-Critical Paths**
    
    Rarely, you may use:
    
    ```java
    new RuntimeException("msg").fillInStackTrace(); // avoid costly stack creation
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **6.4 Use Exceptions Sparingly**
    
    Use only for **unexpected**, **exceptional**, and **error** scenarios.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Exception Chaining** | Reporting a complaint with full background details |
    | **Suppressed Exceptions** | Recording secondary issues without hiding the main one |
    | **Stack Walker** | A CCTV replay that can jump to specific timestamps |
    | **@Deprecated** | “This machine will be removed soon — do not use” sign |
    | **Performance Costs** | Emergency alarm — used only when necessary, not for normal actions |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - StackWalker traverses call stack lazily, improving performance.
    - try-with-resources implements suppressed exceptions using hidden `finally` blocks.
    - Exception chaining preserves original cause in `Throwable.cause`.
    - Deprecated annotation metadata is stored in class file and used by compilers.
    - Creating stack traces involves native JVM calls, making exception creation costly.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Always chain exceptions (`cause`) when translating.
    - In try-with-resources, suppressed exceptions maintain cleanup failure info.
    - Use StackWalker for efficient, controlled stack analysis.
    - Use `@Deprecated(since, forRemoval)` for planned API deprecations.
    - Do not use exceptions for ordinary logic—they are expensive.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Exception Chaining** | Preserve original cause for better debugging |
    | **Suppressed Exceptions** | Store cleanup failures during try-with-resources |
    | **Stack Walking API** | Efficient, modern stack inspection |
    | **Enhanced @Deprecated** | Clear deprecation lifecycle |
    | **Performance Awareness** | Use exceptions only for exceptional events |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Advanced exception handling techniques enhance the robustness and clarity of Java applications.
    
    They help maintain meaningful error behavior, optimize debugging, and ensure performance-conscious exception usage in modern Java development.
    

### 3.4 Input/Output (I/O) Operations

- **Streams Overview**
    
    # **Streams Overview**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **stream** in Java represents a continuous flow of data—similar to a pipeline—used for reading from or writing to various data sources such as files, memory, network connections, or keyboard input.
    
    Streams handle data **sequentially**, one byte or character at a time.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide a unified model for input and output (I/O) operations
    - Support efficient reading/writing from diverse sources
    - Encapsulate low-level device handling
