    - Offer flexible combinations through stream chaining (wrappers)
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Byte Streams vs Character Streams**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Byte Streams**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Byte streams handle **binary data** using 8-bit bytes.
    
    Used for:
    
    - Images
    - Audio files
    - Video files
    - Executable files
    - Raw network data
    
    ---
    
    ### **Base Classes**
    
    - `InputStream`
    - `OutputStream`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Byte Stream Classes**
    
    | Class | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | `FileInputStream` | Read bytes from a file |
    | `FileOutputStream` | Write bytes to a file |
    | `BufferedInputStream` | Buffered reading |
    | `BufferedOutputStream` | Buffered writing |
    | `DataInputStream` | Read primitive data types |
    | `ObjectInputStream` | Deserialize objects |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("data.bin");
    int b = fis.read();
    fis.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Character Streams**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Character streams handle **Unicode characters** (16-bit), making them ideal for text files.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Base Classes**
    
    - `Reader`
    - `Writer`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Character Stream Classes**
    
    | Class | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | `FileReader` | Read characters from a file |
    | `FileWriter` | Write characters to a file |
    | `BufferedReader` | Efficient reading, supports readLine() |
    | `BufferedWriter` | Efficient writing |
    | `PrintWriter` | Formatted printing |
    | `CharArrayReader` | Read from char array |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    FileReader fr = new FileReader("message.txt");
    int ch = fr.read();
    fr.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Difference Table: Byte vs Character Streams**
    
    | Aspect | Byte Streams | Character Streams |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Base Classes | InputStream / OutputStream | Reader / Writer |
    | Data Type | 8-bit bytes | 16-bit Unicode chars |
    | Suitable For | Binary data | Text data |
    | Encoding Handling | Not handled | Built-in character encoding |
    | Examples | FileInputStream | FileReader |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Input Streams and Output Streams**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Input Streams**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **InputStream** reads data from a source into a program.
    
    Represents **data coming into** the application.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common InputStream Methods**
    
    ```java
    int read()
    int read(byte[] b)
    int read(byte[] b, int off, int len)
    long skip(long n)
    int available()
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Reading Bytes**
    
    ```java
    InputStream is = new FileInputStream("data.txt");
    int data = is.read();
    while (data != -1) {
        System.out.print((char) data);
        data = is.read();
    }
    is.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Output Streams**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **OutputStream** writes data from a program to a destination.
    
    Represents **data going out** of the application.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common OutputStream Methods**
    
    ```java
    void write(int b)
    void write(byte[] b)
    void write(byte[] b, int off, int len)
    void flush()
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Writing Bytes**
    
    ```java
    OutputStream os = new FileOutputStream("out.txt");
    os.write("Hello".getBytes());
    os.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Character Stream Examples**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Reading Using BufferedReader**
    
    ```java
    BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("note.txt"));
    String line;
    while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
        System.out.println(line);
    }
    br.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Writing Using BufferedWriter**
    
    ```java
    BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("note.txt"));
    bw.write("Hello Java");
    bw.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Stream Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **InputStream** | Water flowing *into* a tank |
    | **OutputStream** | Water flowing *out* of a tank |
    | **Byte Stream** | Raw numbers or binary files like ZIP, image |
    | **Character Stream** | Human-readable text |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Streams abstract away OS file handling.
    - Character streams internally use byte streams with an encoding layer.
    - Buffered streams reduce I/O calls, enhancing performance.
    - `close()` is critical to free OS-level file descriptors.
    - try-with-resources should be used to ensure automatic closing.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - Use **byte streams** for binary files, **character streams** for text.
    - Always close streams (prefer try-with-resources).
    - Character streams handle encoding automatically.
    - Streams can be chained (e.g., `BufferedInputStream` on `FileInputStream`).
    - Avoid using `FileReader`/`FileWriter` when dealing with non-default encodings—use `InputStreamReader` and `OutputStreamWriter` instead.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Key Idea |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Byte Streams** | Handle 8-bit raw data using InputStream/OutputStream |
    | **Character Streams** | Handle Unicode text using Reader/Writer |
    | **Input Streams** | Read data into the program |
    | **Output Streams** | Write data out of the program |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java’s stream-based I/O system provides a unified, efficient, and flexible mechanism for reading and writing data—whether binary or character-based—using input and output streams.
    
- **File Operations**
    
    # **File Operations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    File operations in Java involve handling filesystem resources such as files and directories using classes from the `java.io` and `java.nio.file` packages.
    
    The core class for basic file manipulation is the **`File`** class, which represents file and directory paths in the filesystem.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Create, delete, rename files and directories
    - Inspect file properties and metadata
    - Perform read/write operations on file content
    - Manage file permissions and attributes
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. `File` Class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `java.io.File` represents an **abstract path** to a file or directory.
    
    It does **not** read or write file contents directly — instead, it provides metadata operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Uses**
    
    - Checking existence
    - Getting file size
    - Creating directories
    - Checking permissions
    - Listing directory contents
    - Determining whether path is a file or directory
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Methods**
    
    ### **Path Information**
    
    ```java
    getName()
    getPath()
    getAbsolutePath()
    getParent()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Existence & Type**
    
    ```java
    exists()
    isFile()
    isDirectory()
    
    ```
    
    ### **File Operations**
    
    ```java
    createNewFile()
    delete()
    renameTo(File dest)
    mkdir()
    mkdirs()
    list()
    listFiles()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Permissions**
    
    ```java
    canRead()
    canWrite()
    canExecute()
    setReadOnly()
    setWritable(boolean)
    setExecutable(boolean)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Basic File Metadata**
    
    ```java
    File file = new File("data.txt");
    
    System.out.println("Exists: " + file.exists());
    System.out.println("Is File: " + file.isFile());
    System.out.println("Path: " + file.getAbsolutePath());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Creating, Reading, Writing Files**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Creating Files**
    
    ### **Using `File` Class**
    
    ```java
    File file = new File("notes.txt");
    if (file.createNewFile()) {
        System.out.println("File created.");
    } else {
        System.out.println("File already exists.");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Writing to Files**
    
    ### **Using `FileWriter`**
    
    ```java
    FileWriter writer = new FileWriter("notes.txt");
    writer.write("Hello Java");
    writer.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using `BufferedWriter`**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("log.txt"))) {
        bw.write("Logging line...");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using `PrintWriter`**
    
    ```java
    PrintWriter pw = new PrintWriter("output.txt");
    pw.println("Line 1");
    pw.println("Line 2");
    pw.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Reading Files**
    
    ### **Using `FileReader`**
    
    ```java
    FileReader fr = new FileReader("notes.txt");
    int ch;
    while ((ch = fr.read()) != -1) {
        System.out.print((char) ch);
    }
    fr.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using `BufferedReader`**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("notes.txt"))) {
        String line;
        while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
            System.out.println(line);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Using `java.nio.file` (Modern API)**
    
    The `Files` class provides easier, faster file handling.
    
    ### **Reading**
    
    ```java
    List<String> lines = Files.readAllLines(Paths.get("notes.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ### **Writing**
    
    ```java
    Files.write(Paths.get("notes.txt"), "Hello".getBytes());
    
    ```
    
    ### **Creating**
    
    ```java
    Files.createFile(Paths.get("new.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. File Permissions and Attributes**
    
    ---
    
    File permissions and attributes allow managing access rights, timestamps, and file metadata.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. File Permissions**
    
    ### **Checking Permissions**
    
    ```java
    file.canRead()
    file.canWrite()
    file.canExecute()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Setting Permissions**
    
    ```java
    file.setReadable(true);
    file.setWritable(false);
    file.setExecutable(true);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Read-only Example**
    
    ```java
    file.setReadOnly();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. File Attributes (Using `java.nio.file.attribute`)**
    
    The modern NIO.2 API exposes detailed file metadata.
    
    ---
    
    ### **BasicFileAttributes**
    
    Retrieve attributes like size, creation time, modified time, type.
    
    ```java
    Path path = Paths.get("notes.txt");
    BasicFileAttributes attrs = Files.readAttributes(path, BasicFileAttributes.class);
    
    System.out.println("Size: " + attrs.size());
    System.out.println("Created: " + attrs.creationTime());
    System.out.println("Modified: " + attrs.lastModifiedTime());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **PosixFileAttributes**
    
    (For Linux/Unix systems)
    
    ```java
    PosixFileAttributes posix = Files.readAttributes(path, PosixFileAttributes.class);
    System.out.println(posix.permissions());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Changing File Permissions (POSIX)**
    
    ```java
    Files.setPosixFilePermissions(path, PosixFilePermissions.fromString("rwxr-x---"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Hidden Files**
    
    ```java
    boolean hidden = Files.isHidden(path);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Directory Operations**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating a Directory**
    
    ```java
    new File("logs").mkdir();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Creating Nested Directories**
    
    ```java
    new File("data/backup/docs").mkdirs();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Listing Directory Contents**
    
    ```java
    File dir = new File("data");
    File[] files = dir.listFiles();
    
    for (File f : files) {
        System.out.println(f.getName());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Operation | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | `File` class | A reference to a locker in a storage room |
    | Creating files | Adding a new notebook |
    | Reading files | Reading pages inside the notebook |
    | Writing files | Writing notes in the notebook |
    | Permissions | Locking the notebook or marking it “read-only” |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - The `File` class stores path metadata but not actual file content.
    - `FileReader` and `FileWriter` rely on default character encoding—may cause portability issues.
    - Closing streams is critical to release OS-level file handles.
    - NIO's `Files` class performs most operations atomically and efficiently.
    - Permissions handling differs between OS types (Windows vs UNIX).
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use `File` class for path and metadata; not for content I/O.
    - Prefer `BufferedReader` and `BufferedWriter` for performance.
    - Use try-with-resources to auto-close streams.
    - Use NIO (`Files`, `Path`) for modern applications.
    - Always check file existence before reading or writing.
    - Avoid using default encoding—explicitly specify charset when possible.
    - Understand OS-level differences for permissions.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **File class** | Represents file paths, metadata, operations |
    | **Creating Files** | Using `File`, `Files`, or Streams |
    | **Reading/Writing Files** | Using Readers/Writers, InputStream/OutputStream |
    | **Permissions** | Managing read/write/execute access |
    | **Attributes** | Metadata like size, timestamps, POSIX permissions |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java provides robust file handling through both traditional `java.io` classes and the modern NIO.2 API. Developers can create, read, write, inspect, and manage file permissions efficiently using these tools.
    
- **Byte Streams**
    
    # **Byte Streams**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Byte Streams** in Java handle **raw binary data** using 8-bit bytes.
    
    They are the foundation of Java’s I/O system for reading/writing:
    
    - Images
    - Audio/video files
    - PDFs
    - Zip files
    - Raw network data
    - Any non-text (binary) content
    
    All byte stream classes derive from:
    
    - **`InputStream`** → reads bytes
    - **`OutputStream`** → writes bytes
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Efficient handling of binary data
    - Device-independent input/output
    - Ability to chain streams (e.g., buffering, filtering)
    - Basis for higher-level streams
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. InputStream and OutputStream**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. InputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `InputStream` is an abstract superclass for all classes that read byte data *into* a Java program.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    int read()
    int read(byte[] b)
    int read(byte[] b, int off, int len)
    long skip(long n)
    int available()
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    InputStream is = new FileInputStream("data.bin");
    int data = is.read();     // reads one byte
    is.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. OutputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `OutputStream` is an abstract superclass for all classes that write byte data *out* of a Java program.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    void write(int b)
    void write(byte[] b)
    void write(byte[] b, int off, int len)
    void flush()
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    OutputStream os = new FileOutputStream("out.bin");
    os.write(65);  // writes one byte
    os.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. FileInputStream and FileOutputStream**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. FileInputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `FileInputStream` reads **raw bytes** from a file.
    
    Used for binary file processing.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("image.jpg");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("data.bin")) {
        int b;
        while ((b = fis.read()) != -1) {
            System.out.print(b + " ");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Reading binary files
    - Streaming file-based input
    - Reading serialized data (with ObjectInputStream)
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. FileOutputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `FileOutputStream` writes **raw bytes** to a file.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    FileOutputStream fos = new FileOutputStream("output.bin");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (FileOutputStream fos = new FileOutputStream("output.bin")) {
        fos.write(100);
        fos.write(new byte[]{1, 2, 3});
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Writing binary data
    - Saving files
    - Writing images/audio in raw byte form
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. BufferedInputStream and BufferedOutputStream**
    
    ---
    
    ## **Purpose of Buffering**
    
    Buffering improves performance by reducing **disk I/O operations**.
    
    Instead of reading/writing byte-by-byte, a buffered stream reads/writes in **large chunks**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. BufferedInputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A wrapper around any InputStream that improves **read performance** by maintaining a buffer (default: 8 KB).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    BufferedInputStream bis = new BufferedInputStream(new FileInputStream("data.bin"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedInputStream bis = new BufferedInputStream(new FileInputStream("large.bin"))) {
        int data;
        while ((data = bis.read()) != -1) {
            // faster reading
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Efficient reading of large files
    - Ideal for network or file streams
    - Reduces system calls
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. BufferedOutputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A wrapper around any OutputStream that buffers output to reduce write operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    BufferedOutputStream bos = new BufferedOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("out.bin"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedOutputStream bos = new BufferedOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("out.bin"))) {
        bos.write("Hello".getBytes());
        bos.flush(); // ensure data is written
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Improved performance for large writes
    - Fewer disk or network write calls
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Stream Chaining (Decorator Pattern)**
    
    Buffered streams are typically chained with file streams:
    
    ```java
    InputStream is = new BufferedInputStream(
                        new FileInputStream("data.bin"));
    
    ```
    
    ```java
    OutputStream os = new BufferedOutputStream(
                        new FileOutputStream("out.bin"));
    
    ```
    
    This design uses the **Decorator Pattern**, allowing multiple stream features to be combined.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Stream | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **InputStream** | Water entering a pipe |
    | **OutputStream** | Water leaving a pipe |
    | **BufferedInputStream** | Water tank that stores water and provides at stable speed |
    | **BufferedOutputStream** | A tank that gathers water before flushing it out |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Streams are **blocking I/O**: read/write waits until data is available.
    - Buffered streams reduce overhead from frequent system-level I/O calls.
    - `close()` automatically closes wrapped streams.
    - Streams must be handled with try-with-resources to prevent leaks.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use **byte streams** for binary data; **character streams** for text.
    - Always prefer buffered streams for performance.
    - `FileInputStream`/`FileOutputStream` deal with raw binary files.
    - `flush()` is important to force buffered data to be written.
    - Use try-with-resources to ensure safe closure.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Stream Type | Purpose | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **InputStream** | Read bytes | All binary inputs |
    | **OutputStream** | Write bytes | All binary outputs |
    | **FileInputStream** | Read from files | Images, PDFs, binary files |
    | **FileOutputStream** | Write to files | Saving binary data |
    | **BufferedInputStream** | Fast reading | Large files, network data |
    | **BufferedOutputStream** | Fast writing | Bulk write operations |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Byte streams form the foundation of Java's binary I/O system.
    
    File streams provide file-based byte access, and buffered streams significantly improve performance by reducing the cost of frequent read/write operations.
    
- **Character Streams**
    
    # **Character Streams**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Character Streams** in Java handle **text data** using 16-bit Unicode characters.
    
    They are designed for reading and writing **human-readable text**, unlike byte streams that process raw binary data.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Handle text in a Unicode-safe manner
    - Automatically manage character encoding (via Readers/Writers)
    - Provide efficient text I/O through buffering
    - Serve as the base for high-level text-processing utilities
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Reader and Writer**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Reader**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Reader` is the abstract superclass for all **character-input** classes.
    
    It reads **char** data (16-bit) instead of raw bytes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    int read()
    int read(char[] cbuf)
    int read(char[] cbuf, int off, int len)
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Reader reader = new FileReader("text.txt");
    int ch = reader.read();
    reader.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Writer**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Writer` is the abstract superclass for all **character-output** classes.
    
    It writes characters, arrays, and strings.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    void write(int c)
    void write(char[] cbuf)
    void write(String str)
    void flush()
    void close()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Writer writer = new FileWriter("output.txt");
    writer.write("Hello");
    writer.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. FileReader and FileWriter**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. FileReader**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `FileReader` is a character stream used to read **text files**.
    
    It is a convenience class built on top of `InputStreamReader`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Internally**
    
    - Uses the **default character encoding** of the platform
    - Suitable for simple text files
    - Not recommended when encoding must be controlled (use `InputStreamReader` instead)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    FileReader fr = new FileReader("data.txt");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (FileReader fr = new FileReader("data.txt")) {
        int ch;
        while ((ch = fr.read()) != -1) {
            System.out.print((char) ch);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Reading text files
    - Reading small configuration files
    - Reading character-based data from disk
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. FileWriter**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `FileWriter` writes **character data** to text files.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    FileWriter fw = new FileWriter("output.txt");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (FileWriter fw = new FileWriter("output.txt")) {
        fw.write("Java Character Streams");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Writing text files
    - Creating logs or reports
    - Generating simple text-based content
    
    ---
    
    ### **Append Mode**
    
    ```java
    FileWriter fw = new FileWriter("output.txt", true);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. BufferedReader and BufferedWriter**
    
    ---
    
    ## **Purpose of Buffering**
    
    Buffered streams enhance performance by reducing the number of low-level disk I/O operations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. BufferedReader**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `BufferedReader` wraps a `Reader` to provide:
    
    - Faster reading
    - Line-based reading (`readLine()`)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("text.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Methods**
    
    ```java
    String readLine()
    int read()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("text.txt"))) {
        String line;
        while ((line = br.readLine()) != null) {
            System.out.println(line);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Reading text line-by-line
    - Processing logs or configuration files
    - Efficient reading of large text files
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. BufferedWriter**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `BufferedWriter` wraps a `Writer` to provide:
    
    - Faster writing
    - Support for newLine()
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("output.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Methods**
    
    ```java
    void write(String str)
    void newLine()
    void flush()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedWriter bw = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("output.txt"))) {
        bw.write("First Line");
        bw.newLine();
        bw.write("Second Line");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Writing large text files
    - Logging
    - Generating reports
    - Writing formatted content efficiently
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Difference Table: Character vs Byte Streams**
    
    | Aspect | Character Streams | Byte Streams |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Base Classes | Reader / Writer | InputStream / OutputStream |
    | Data Type | 16-bit Unicode chars | 8-bit bytes |
    | Use | Text | Binary |
    | Encoding | Automatically handled | Not handled |
    | Examples | FileReader, BufferedReader | FileInputStream, BufferedInputStream |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Stream Chaining (Decorator Pattern)**
    
    Character streams can be wrapped to add functionality:
    
    ```java
    Reader r = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("notes.txt"));
    Writer w = new BufferedWriter(new FileWriter("output.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Stream Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Reader** | Reading letters from a document |
    | **Writer** | Writing sentences into a notebook |
    | **BufferedReader** | Reading with eye scanning multiple words at once |
    | **BufferedWriter** | Writing quickly using a buffer before committing |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Character streams internally use byte streams through decoders/encoders.
    - Character buffering significantly improves performance.
    - `readLine()` does not include newline characters in result.
    - Default encoding varies across systems; avoid relying on it.
    - try-with-resources ensures safe closure of streams.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Use character streams **only** for text data.
    - Prefer buffered versions for performance.
    - Avoid default system encoding → use `InputStreamReader` with charset when necessary.
    - Always close streams to avoid resource leaks.
    - FileReader/FileWriter are simple but limited (no encoding control).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Stream | Purpose | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Reader** | Read characters | Reading text files |
    | **Writer** | Write characters | Writing text files |
    | **FileReader** | Simple file reading | Small/medium text files |
    | **FileWriter** | Simple writing | Logs, notes, configuration |
    | **BufferedReader** | Fast reading + readLine | Large text files |
    | **BufferedWriter** | Fast writing + newLine | Reports, logs, data export |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Character streams provide efficient and Unicode-safe mechanisms for reading and writing text in Java. `Reader`, `Writer`, and their buffered/file-based implementations form the foundation of text-based I/O operations.
    
- **Serialization**
    
    # **Serialization**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Serialization** in Java is the process of converting an object into a **byte stream** so it can be stored (file, database) or transmitted (network).
    
    **Deserialization** is the reverse process—reconstructing an object from its byte-stream form.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Persist object state to storage
    - Transfer objects across networks (RMI, sockets)
    - Enable deep copying
    - Store session data
    
    Serialization works through the **`Serializable`** interface and stream classes such as **`ObjectOutputStream`** and **`ObjectInputStream`**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Serializable Interface**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Serializable` is a **marker interface** (has no methods) that tells the JVM a class is eligible for serialization.
    
    ```java
    public class Employee implements Serializable {
        private int id;
        private String name;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - Fields must be **serializable** themselves
    - Static fields are **not serialized** (belong to class, not object)
    - Constructors are **not invoked** during deserialization
    - If a superclass is not serializable, its fields are handled through default constructors
    
    ---
    
    ### **serialVersionUID**
    
    Used to verify the sender and receiver of a serialized object have compatible classes.
    
    ```java
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    
    ```
    
    If not explicitly declared, JVM generates one automatically.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. ObjectOutputStream and ObjectInputStream**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. ObjectOutputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Used to serialize Java objects into a byte stream.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("data.obj"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Writing an Object**
    
    ```java
    try (ObjectOutputStream oos = new ObjectOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("emp.ser"))) {
        oos.writeObject(emp);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. ObjectInputStream**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Used to deserialize Java objects from a byte stream.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(new FileInputStream("data.obj"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Reading an Object**
    
    ```java
    try (ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(new FileInputStream("emp.ser"))) {
        Employee emp = (Employee) ois.readObject();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Behavior**
    
    - Returns **Object**, so casting is required
    - Throws `ClassNotFoundException` if class definition not found
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. transient Keyword**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `transient` marks a field that should **NOT** be serialized.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Protect sensitive data (passwords)
    - Avoid serialization of derived fields
    - Skip unserializable objects
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class User implements Serializable {
        private String username;
        private transient String password;
    }
    
    ```
    
    Upon deserialization:
    
    - `username` → restored
    - `password` → set to default (`null`)
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - Cache fields
    - Security-sensitive data
    - Objects that cannot be serialized (Threads, Sockets, etc.)
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Custom Serialization**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Custom serialization allows developers to control what and how object fields are serialized or deserialized, using special methods.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Using `writeObject()` and `readObject()`**
    
    ### **Custom write**
    
    ```java
    private void writeObject(ObjectOutputStream oos) throws IOException {
        oos.defaultWriteObject(); // serializes non-transient fields
        oos.writeUTF(encrypt(password)); // custom logic
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Custom read**
    
    ```java
    private void readObject(ObjectInputStream ois) throws IOException, ClassNotFoundException {
        ois.defaultReadObject();
        this.password = decrypt(ois.readUTF());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Fully Custom Serialization with Externalizable**
    
    ### **Externalizable Interface**
    
    ```java
    public class Student implements Externalizable {
        public void writeExternal(ObjectOutput out) throws IOException { }
        public void readExternal(ObjectInput in) throws IOException, ClassNotFoundException { }
    }
    
    ```
    
    - Complete control over serialization
    - Must explicitly read/write all fields
    - Requires public no-arg constructor
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Serialization Process Internals**
    
    - JVM assigns serialization stream handles
    - Reference tracking prevents duplicate object serialization
    - Circular references are handled automatically
    - serialVersionUID ensures version compatibility
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Serialization** | Compressing a suitcase to ship it |
    | **Deserialization** | Opening the suitcase to restore items |
    | **transient fields** | Items you choose *not* to pack |
    | **Custom serialization** | Packing items in a special way |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Constructors do **not** run during deserialization
    - transient fields are replaced with default values
    - serialVersionUID mismatch throws `InvalidClassException`
    - Object graphs are serialized recursively
    - Static fields are skipped entirely
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Always define **serialVersionUID** for version safety
    - Make fields transient if they are sensitive or non-serializable
    - Prefer custom serialization when transformation is required
    - Keep serialized form stable for long-term storage
    - Serialization is slow—avoid using it in performance-critical systems
    - Use modern alternatives when possible (JSON, Protocol Buffers, XML)
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Serializable** | Marks object as serializable |
    | **ObjectOutputStream / ObjectInputStream** | Write/read objects to/from streams |
    | **transient** | Exclude fields from serialization |
    | **Custom serialization** | Fine-grained control over serialized form |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Serialization allows Java objects to be converted to and from byte streams. The Serializable interface, object streams, and transient keyword form the basis of this mechanism, while custom serialization provides deeper control over how object data is handled.
    
- **Modern I/O: NIO.2 API (Java 7+)**
    
    # **Modern I/O: NIO.2 API (Java 7+)**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    NIO.2 (New I/O version 2), introduced in **Java 7**, is a modern file I/O API providing:
    
    - More powerful **file paths** (`Path`)
    - Flexible **file operations** (`Files`)
    - Efficient **directory traversal** (`walk`, `list`, `find`)
    - Unified **file attribute and metadata access**
    - Better error handling and symbolic link support
    
    It is designed to replace many limitations of the old `java.io.File` API.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide a consistent, scalable, and flexible file I/O mechanism
    - Offer improved performance and greater file system support
    - Enable safe and powerful operations on paths and metadata
    - Support symbolic links, asynchronous I/O, and file visitors
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Path, Files, and Paths Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Path Interface**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Path` represents a platform-independent file or directory path.
    
    It replaces `File` for modern applications.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating a Path**
    
    ```java
    Path p = Paths.get("data/file.txt");
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Methods**
    
    ```java
    p.getFileName()
    p.getParent()
    p.getRoot()
    p.toAbsolutePath()
    p.normalize()
    p.resolve("subdir")
    p.relativize(otherPath)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Immutable and thread-safe
    - Supports symbolic links
    - Easier path manipulation
    - Cross-platform
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Paths Class**
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Utility class to create `Path` instances.
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    ```java
    Path p1 = Paths.get("notes.txt");
    Path p2 = Paths.get("folder", "sub", "file.txt");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Files Class**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Files` provides static utility methods for reading, writing, copying, moving, deleting, and checking file attributes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    Files.exists(path)
    Files.createFile(path)
    Files.createDirectory(path)
    Files.readAllLines(path)
    Files.write(path, bytes)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Directory Operations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Files.list()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Lists only the **entries inside a directory** (one level deep).
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try (Stream<Path> stream = Files.list(path)) {
        stream.forEach(System.out::println);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Features**
    
    - Does not traverse subdirectories
    - Returns a **Stream**
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Files.walk()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Traverses directory tree recursively.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    try (Stream<Path> stream = Files.walk(path)) {
        stream.forEach(System.out::println);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Features**
    
    - Depth-first traversal
    - Return all files/subdirectories
    - Depth control with `Files.walk(path, depth)`
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Files.find()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Searches directory trees with a **filter predicate**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Files.find(
        path,
        Integer.MAX_VALUE,
        (p, attrs) -> attrs.isRegularFile()
    );
    
    ```
    
    ### **Features**
    
    - Depth-limited searching
    - Access to file attributes while searching
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Find All `.txt` Files**
    
    ```java
    try (Stream<Path> stream = Files.find(
            start,
            5,
            (p, a) -> p.toString().endsWith(".txt"))) {
        stream.forEach(System.out::println);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. File Attributes and Metadata Access**
    
    ---
    
    NIO.2 provides rich APIs for reading file metadata.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. BasicFileAttributes**
    
    ```java
    BasicFileAttributes attrs = Files.readAttributes(path, BasicFileAttributes.class);
    
    attrs.size();
    attrs.creationTime();
    attrs.lastModifiedTime();
    attrs.isDirectory();
    attrs.isRegularFile();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. PosixFileAttributes** (Linux/Unix)
    
    ```java
    PosixFileAttributes posix = Files.readAttributes(path, PosixFileAttributes.class);
    
    posix.permissions();
    posix.owner();
    posix.group();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. File Ownership**
    
    ```java
    UserPrincipal owner = Files.getOwner(path);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Setting Attributes**
    
    ```java
    Files.setAttribute(path, "basic:lastModifiedTime", FileTime.fromMillis(0));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Supported Attribute Views**
    
    | Attribute View | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | basic | Size, timestamps, type |
    | posix | Permissions, group, owner |
    | dos | Hidden, read-only |
    | acl | Access Control Lists |
    | fileOwner | File ownership |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. File Operations: copy(), move(), delete()**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Files.copy()**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Files.copy(source, target, StandardCopyOption.REPLACE_EXISTING);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Options**
    
    - `REPLACE_EXISTING`
    - `COPY_ATTRIBUTES`
    - `NOFOLLOW_LINKS`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Files.copy(Paths.get("a.txt"), Paths.get("b.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Files.move()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Moves or renames files or directories.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Files.move(source, target, StandardCopyOption.REPLACE_EXISTING);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Files.move(Paths.get("old.txt"), Paths.get("new.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Files.delete()**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    Files.delete(path);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Safe Delete**
    
    ```java
    Files.deleteIfExists(path);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Creating Files and Directories**
    
    ### **Create File**
    
    ```java
    Files.createFile(Paths.get("data.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ### **Create Directory**
    
    ```java
    Files.createDirectory(Paths.get("logs"));
    
    ```
    
    ### **Create Nested Directories**
    
    ```java
    Files.createDirectories(Paths.get("dir/sub/inner"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Reading & Writing Files (NIO.2)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Reading Entire File**
    
    ```java
    List<String> lines = Files.readAllLines(path);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Writing File**
    
    ```java
    Files.write(path, "Hello NIO.2".getBytes());
    
    ```
    
    ### **Efficient Streaming Read**
    
    ```java
    Files.lines(path).forEach(System.out::println);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Path** | A detailed map describing how to reach a file |
    | **Files class** | A toolkit for interacting with files |
    | **Files.walk()** | Exploring every room in a building |
    | **Files.copy()** | Photocopying a document |
    | **Files.move()** | Renaming or relocating a file |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - NIO.2 uses **channels** and **buffers** internally for high performance.
    - Symbolic link support is built in.
    - Streams created by `list()`, `walk()`, and `find()` must be closed.
    - Exceptions provide better detail compared to `File` class.
    - Cross-platform handling of path separators is standardized.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Always use `Path` instead of `File` for new code.
    - Use `Files.walk()` for recursive traversal.
    - `Files.copy()` and `move()` provide atomic operations.
    - Use attribute views for OS-specific metadata.
    - Always close stream-based directory operations.
    - Avoid relying on system default character encoding—use explicit charset.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Path** | Modern path representation |
    | **Files** | Rich utility for I/O operations |
    | **Directory Traversal** | list(), walk(), find() |
    | **Attributes** | Detailed metadata handling |
    | **Operations** | copy(), move(), delete(), read, write |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    NIO.2 provides a robust, modern, and powerful alternative to the older File API, making file handling safer, faster, and far more flexible in Java.
    
- **Additional I/O Classes**
    
    # **Additional I/O Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Beyond streams and NIO.2, Java provides additional high-level I/O classes that simplify console interactions, user input parsing, and character encoding management.
    
    Key classes include:
    
    - **Console** → secure console-based I/O (especially passwords)
    - **Scanner** → convenient parsing of primitive data and text
    - **Charset / encoding utilities** → explicit control over text encoding (UTF-8, ISO-8859-1, etc.)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improve user interactivity through console
    - Parse formatted input easily
    - Ensure proper handling of character encoding
    - Support secure password entry without echoing characters
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Console Class (Secure Password Input)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `java.io.Console` provides **secure and system-level console input/output**, especially for reading **passwords without echoing** them on screen.
    
    ---
    
    ### **How to Access Console**
    
    ```java
    Console console = System.console();
    
    ```
    
    **Note:**
    
    `System.console()` returns `null` in some environments (IDEs, GUI apps).
    
    Works reliably in real terminal/command prompt.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Methods**
    
    ```java
    String readLine(String fmt, Object... args)
    char[] readPassword(String fmt, Object... args)
    void printf(String fmt, Object... args)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Read User Credentials Securely**
    
    ```java
    Console c = System.console();
    if (c != null) {
        String user = c.readLine("Enter username: ");
        char[] pass = c.readPassword("Enter password: ");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Security Advantage**
    
    - Password is read into a `char[]` instead of String
    - Programmers can explicitly clear the array to avoid storing passwords in memory
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Authentication workflows
    - Secure command-line utilities
    - Tools requiring sensitive input
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Scanner Class (Parsing Input)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `java.util.Scanner` parses primitive types and strings using **regular expressions**.
    
    Highly convenient for reading input from:
    
    - Keyboard (`System.in`)
    - Files
    - Strings
    - Input streams
    
    ---
    
    ### **Constructor Examples**
    
    ```java
    Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
    Scanner sc = new Scanner(new File("data.txt"));
    Scanner sc = new Scanner("123 456 789");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ### **Reading Tokens**
    
    ```java
    next()
    nextLine()
    nextInt()
    nextDouble()
    nextBoolean()
    nextLong()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Checking Tokens**
    
    ```java
    hasNext()
    hasNextInt()
    hasNextDouble()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Command-line Input Parsing**
    
    ```java
    Scanner sc = new Scanner(System.in);
    
    System.out.print("Enter age: ");
    int age = sc.nextInt();
    
    System.out.print("Enter name: ");
    String name = sc.next();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Reading File Using Scanner**
    
    ```java
    Scanner sc = new Scanner(new File("input.txt"));
    while (sc.hasNextLine()) {
        System.out.println(sc.nextLine());
    }
    sc.close();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Characteristics**
    
    - Splits input using default **whitespace delimiter**
    - Can use custom delimiters:
    
    ```java
    sc.useDelimiter(",");
    
    ```
    
    - Slower than buffered streams for heavy I/O
    - Always close the Scanner (closes underlying stream)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Use Cases**
    
    - Parsing structured user input
    - Simple file reading
    - Quick prototyping
    - Lightweight text parsing
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Character Encoding Handling (UTF-8, ISO-8859-1)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Character encoding determines how characters are translated to bytes and vice-versa.
    
    Common encodings:
    
    - **UTF-8** (recommended, universal)
    - **ISO-8859-1** (Western European languages)
    - **UTF-16** (wide Unicode representation)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Encoding Matters**
    
    - Ensures text consistency across systems
    - Avoids corrupt characters when writing/reading text
    - Critical in web apps, network communication, and file I/O
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Using Encodings with Reader/Writer**
    
    ### **InputStreamReader**
    
    ```java
    Reader r = new InputStreamReader(
                    new FileInputStream("data.txt"),
                    StandardCharsets.UTF_8);
    
    ```
    
    ### **OutputStreamWriter**
    
    ```java
    Writer w = new OutputStreamWriter(
                    new FileOutputStream("out.txt"),
                    StandardCharsets.ISO_8859_1);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Using Encodings with Files Class (NIO.2)**
    
    ### **Reading**
    
    ```java
    List<String> lines = Files.readAllLines(path, StandardCharsets.UTF_8);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Writing**
    
    ```java
    Files.write(path, text.getBytes(StandardCharsets.UTF_8));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Converting Between Encodings**
    
    ```java
    byte[] utf8 = str.getBytes(StandardCharsets.UTF_8);
    String iso = new String(utf8, StandardCharsets.ISO_8859_1);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Difference Table**
    
    | Feature | Console | Scanner | Encoding Handling |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | Purpose | Secure console I/O | Parsing structured input | Managing character sets |
    | Best Use | Passwords, secure input | User input, file parsing | Internationalization |
    | Output/Input Type | Strings, char[] | Tokens, primitives | Characters ↔ bytes |
    | Security | High | Low | Depends on encoding |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Console** | Whispering a password safely |
    | **Scanner** | A clerk reading and parsing forms |
    | **Encoding** | Language translation (letter ↔ byte mapping) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - `console.readPassword()` does not echo input to avoid exposure.
    - `Scanner` uses regex engine internally, which adds overhead.
    - Encodings affect how bytes are interpreted; wrong encoding leads to corrupted text.
    - JVM uses **default charset** unless explicitly specified; avoid relying on it.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Prefer `Console` for sensitive inputs like passwords.
    - Scanner is convenient but not optimal for performance-heavy I/O.
    - Always specify character encoding explicitly (UTF-8 recommended).
    - Be cautious with `Scanner.nextLine()` after numeric reads — it can consume leftover newline characters.
    - Close `Scanner` and Writer/Reader objects properly.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Component | Description | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Console** | Secure input/output | Passwords, secure CLI apps |
    | **Scanner** | Simple text parsing | User input, quick file parsing |
    | **Encoding Handling** | Controls character storage/transmission | Internationalized apps, consistent text processing |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The Console class, Scanner class, and encoding utilities extend Java’s I/O capabilities by offering secure input, easy parsing, and precise character-set management. They support modern, robust, and internationalized applications.
    
- **Advanced I/O Features**
    
    # **Advanced I/O Features**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java’s NIO.2 API (Java 7+) introduces several **advanced I/O capabilities** designed for high-performance applications, real-time file monitoring, asynchronous operations, and efficient handling of large data.
    
    These features include:
    
    - **WatchService** → real-time directory event monitoring
    - **Asynchronous I/O** → non-blocking file operations
    - **Memory-mapped files** → map files directly into memory
    - **Zip File System provider** → treat ZIP/JAR files as file systems
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Enable scalable and reactive I/O
    - Improve performance for large files
    - Monitor filesystem changes efficiently
    - Treat ZIP archives like virtual file systems
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. WatchService (Directory Monitoring)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `WatchService` monitors directories for file system changes such as:
    
    - File creation
    - File modification
    - File deletion
    
    It is part of `java.nio.file` package.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Classes**
    
    - `WatchService`
    - `WatchKey`
    - `WatchEvent`
    - `StandardWatchEventKinds`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Typical Usage Steps**
    
    1. Obtain a WatchService
    2. Register a directory
    3. Continuously poll for events
    4. Handle events
    5. Reset the WatchKey
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Path dir = Paths.get("data");
    WatchService watcher = FileSystems.getDefault().newWatchService();
    
    dir.register(watcher,
                 StandardWatchEventKinds.ENTRY_CREATE,
                 StandardWatchEventKinds.ENTRY_DELETE,
                 StandardWatchEventKinds.ENTRY_MODIFY);
    
    while (true) {
        WatchKey key = watcher.take();
        for (WatchEvent<?> event : key.pollEvents()) {
            System.out.println("Event: " + event.kind() + " - " + event.context());
        }
        key.reset();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Important Notes**
    
    - Works at **directory level**, not file level
    - Events are aggregated and may be delivered with slight delay
    - Some platforms have OS-specific limitations
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Real-time log monitoring
    - File synchronization tools
    - Auto-update triggers
    - File-based messaging systems
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Asynchronous I/O (NIO.2)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Asynchronous I/O allows file operations to run **non-blocking**, enabling high-performance or reactive applications.
    
    Uses:
    
    - `AsynchronousFileChannel`
    - Completion handlers
    - Futures
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Perform file I/O without blocking the main thread
    - Improve scalability in server applications
    - Handle multiple concurrent I/O operations
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Classes**
    
    - `AsynchronousFileChannel`
    - `CompletionHandler`
    - `Future<Integer>`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Asynchronous Read**
    
    ```java
    AsynchronousFileChannel channel =
            AsynchronousFileChannel.open(Paths.get("data.txt"), StandardOpenOption.READ);
    
    ByteBuffer buffer = ByteBuffer.allocate(1024);
    
    channel.read(buffer, 0, buffer, new CompletionHandler<Integer, ByteBuffer>() {
        @Override
        public void completed(Integer result, ByteBuffer attachment) {
            attachment.flip();
            System.out.println("Bytes read: " + result);
        }
    
        @Override
        public void failed(Throwable exc, ByteBuffer attachment) {
            exc.printStackTrace();
        }
    });
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Does not block
    - Uses OS-level asynchronous capabilities where available
    - Suitable for high-concurrency applications
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - High-performance servers
    - Networked file systems
    - Real-time data streaming
    - Concurrent large-file processing
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Memory-Mapped Files**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Memory-mapped files map portions of a file directly into **virtual memory**.
    
    This allows extremely fast read/write access without traditional stream overhead.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Core Class**
    
    `MappedByteBuffer` (from `java.nio`)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating a Memory-Mapped File**
    
    ```java
    RandomAccessFile raf = new RandomAccessFile("large.bin", "rw");
    FileChannel channel = raf.getChannel();
    
    MappedByteBuffer buffer = channel.map(
            FileChannel.MapMode.READ_WRITE,
            0,
            channel.size());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Very fast random access
    - Efficient large-file processing (1GB+)
    - OS handles paging, reducing overhead
    - Ideal for high-performance systems
    
    ---
    
    ### **Usage Example**
    
    ```java
    buffer.put(0, (byte) 65); // Write 'A' at position 0
    byte b = buffer.get(0);   // Read from memory directly
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Large log analysis
    - Databases and caching engines
    - Real-time data processing
    - Large binary file editing
    
    ---
    
    ### **Precautions**
    
    - Entire file region must fit in virtual memory
    - Unmapping buffer requires JVM hacks (or rely on GC)
    - OS may lock file until mapping is released
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. ZIP File System Provider**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java allows treating ZIP or JAR files as **virtual file systems**, enabling:
    
    - Reading/writing files inside ZIP
    - Walking directories inside ZIP
    - Copying/moving files in/out of ZIP archives
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing ZIP as a File System**
    
    Use `FileSystems.newFileSystem()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Open ZIP File System**
    
    ```java
    Path zipPath = Paths.get("archive.zip");
    
    try (FileSystem zipfs = FileSystems.newFileSystem(zipPath, null)) {
        Path fileInsideZip = zipfs.getPath("/data.txt");
        List<String> lines = Files.readAllLines(fileInsideZip);
        lines.forEach(System.out::println);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Writing to ZIP**
    
    ```java
    try (FileSystem zipfs = FileSystems.newFileSystem(zipPath, null)) {
        Path target = zipfs.getPath("/newFile.txt");
        Files.write(target, "Hello ZIP".getBytes());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Treat ZIP just like normal folder
    - No need to manually extract
    - Works with `Files.copy()`, `Files.walk()`, etc.
    - Useful for JAR manipulation
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Auto-updating ZIP/JAR files
    - Packaging/unpacking archives
    - Editing resources inside ZIP files
    - Reading config files from JAR
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison Table**
    
    | Feature | Purpose | Key Class | Use Case |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **WatchService** | Directory monitoring | WatchService | Real-time file updates |
    | **Asynchronous I/O** | Non-blocking operations | AsynchronousFileChannel | High-performance servers |
    | **Memory-mapped files** | Direct memory access | MappedByteBuffer | Large-file processing |
    | **ZIP File System** | Treat ZIP as file system | FileSystem (ZIP provider) | Editing ZIP/JAR contents |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **WatchService** | Security camera watching a doorway |
    | **Asynchronous I/O** | Delegating tasks to assistants while continuing work |
    | **Memory-Mapped Files** | Holding a book open on a desk for fast access |
    | **ZIP File System** | Opening an archive folder without extracting it |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - WatchService depends heavily on OS capabilities (e.g., inotify on Linux).
    - AsynchronousFileChannel may revert to thread-based async where OS async is not available.
    - Memory-mapped files rely on OS paging; unmapping is dependent on GC.
    - ZIP file system uses Java’s built-in ZIP provider; operations behave like normal file I/O.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Use WatchService for reactive applications that monitor folders.
    - Asynchronous I/O avoids blocking, improving scalability.
    - Memory-mapped files are ideal for ultra-large datasets but must be used carefully.
    - ZIP file system allows ZIP editing without extraction.
    - Always close channels and file system resources (prefer try-with-resources).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **WatchService** | Monitors directory changes |
    | **Asynchronous I/O** | Non-blocking file operations |
    | **Memory-Mapped Files** | Map files to memory for fast access |
    | **ZIP File System Provider** | Treat ZIP/JAR as virtual file systems |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Advanced Java I/O features enable efficient, scalable, and powerful file processing capabilities suitable for modern applications such as monitoring tools, high-performance servers, and large data processing systems.
    
- **I/O Best Practices & Security**
    
    # **I/O Best Practices & Security**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java I/O best practices and security measures help ensure safe, efficient, and robust handling of files, streams, and resources.
    
    They protect applications from vulnerabilities such as:
    
    - **Path Traversal Attacks**
    - **Resource Exhaustion (Denial of Service)**
    - **Improper buffer usage**
    - **Stream leaks or improper closure**
    
    These practices apply across traditional I/O (`java.io`), NIO.2 (`java.nio.file`), and advanced I/O features.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Protect the filesystem from malicious inputs
    - Ensure predictable performance
    - Prevent accidental data exposure
    - Improve application reliability and resource usage
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Path Traversal Prevention**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Path Traversal Attacks** allow attackers to access files outside the intended directory using patterns like:
    
    ```
    ../../etc/passwd
    
    ```
    
    This can compromise sensitive data or manipulate system files.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Prevention Strategies**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Normalize and Validate Paths**
    
    Use `normalize()` to eliminate `.` and `..` from paths.
    
    ```java
    Path base = Paths.get("/app/data");
    Path userPath = base.resolve(inputPath).normalize();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Verify that Resolved Path Stays Within Allowed Directory**
    
    ```java
    if (!userPath.startsWith(base)) {
        throw new SecurityException("Invalid path: Outside permitted directory");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Avoid Directly Concatenating User Input to File Paths**
    
    Incorrect (vulnerable):
    
    ```java
    new File("/app/data/" + input);
    
    ```
    
    Correct:
    
    ```java
    Path safePath = base.resolve(input).normalize();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Restrict Upload/Download Directories**
    
    Never expose entire filesystem to user-controlled paths.
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Use Safelist (Allow-list) Validation**
    
    Accept only allowed filenames, extensions, or patterns.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Resource Exhaustion Attacks Prevention**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Attackers may intentionally consume excessive:
    
    - File descriptors
    - Memory
    - Disk writes
    - Read operations
    - Buffers
    
    This can lead to Denial of Service (DoS).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Practices**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Limit File Size When Reading Input**
    
    ```java
    long size = Files.size(path);
    if (size > MAX_ALLOWED_SIZE) {
        throw new IOException("File too large");
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Limit Input Lines or Tokens (for Scanner, BufferedReader)**
    
    ```java
    if (lineCount > MAX_LINES) break;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Use Bounded Buffers**
    
    Avoid allocating huge byte/char arrays from untrusted input.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Validate ZIP Bombs (Compressed File Attacks)**
    
    Check compression ratio and entry sizes:
    
    ```java
    if (entry.getSize() > MAX_UNCOMPRESSED_SIZE)
        throw new IOException("Potential ZIP bomb");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Close Streams Immediately (or use try-with-resources)**
    
    Prevents descriptor exhaustion.
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Avoid Reading Untrusted Files into Memory**
    
    Prefer streaming/chunk-based reads.
    
    ---
    
    ### **G. Limit Thread Count for Asynchronous I/O**
    
    Use bounded thread pools.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. I/O Buffering Strategies**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Buffering is the technique of reading/writing data in **chunks** rather than small pieces to improve performance.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Guidelines**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Always Use Buffered Streams for Large Data**
    
    ```java
    BufferedInputStream bis = new BufferedInputStream(new FileInputStream("big.bin"));
    BufferedOutputStream bos = new BufferedOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("out.bin"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Choose Appropriate Buffer Sizes**
    
    Default buffer (8 KB) is suitable for general use.
    
    Increase buffer for:
    
    - Large file copy operations
    - Network communication
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    BufferedInputStream bis = new BufferedInputStream(fis, 64 * 1024); // 64 KB
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Avoid Excessively Large Buffers**
    
    Huge buffer sizes waste memory without meaningful gains.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. For Character I/O, Prefer BufferedReader/BufferedWriter**
    
    Especially when reading line-by-line.
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Use NIO Channels and ByteBuffers for High-Performance I/O**
    
    Useful for:
    
    - Large binary data
    - High-throughput systems
    - Network servers
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Stream Closing Patterns**
    
    ---
    
    Proper stream closure prevents:
    
    - Memory leaks
    - File handle leaks
    - Locking issues
    - Corrupt data
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Use try-with-resources (Preferred)**
    
    ```java
    try (BufferedReader br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("data.txt"))) {
        // reading
    }
    
    ```
    
    Automatically closes all resources implementing `AutoCloseable`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Nesting Multiple Resources**
    
    ```java
    try (
        FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("in.txt");
        BufferedInputStream bis = new BufferedInputStream(fis);
    ) {
        // operations
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Manual Closing (Legacy)**
    
    ```java
    BufferedReader br = null;
    try {
        br = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("file.txt"));
    } finally {
        if (br != null) br.close();
    }
    
    ```
    
    Use only when required for backward compatibility.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Close Streams in Reverse Order of Opening**
    
    Useful with legacy code:
    
    - Close the outermost wrapper first
    - It closes underlying streams automatically
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Always Flush Before Close**
    
    ```java
    bw.flush();
    bw.close();
    
    ```
    
    `close()` implicitly calls `flush()`, but explicit flushing avoids partial writes in complex workflows.
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Beware of Double Closing**
    
    Double-closing can hide errors or corrupt data in some wrappers.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison Table**
    
    | Concern | Prevention Method | Tools/API |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Path Traversal | Normalize + validate path | Path, normalize(), startsWith() |
    | Resource Exhaustion | Limit sizes/timeouts | Files.size(), bounded buffers |
    | Buffering Strategy | Optimal buffer usage | BufferedReader, ByteBuffer |
    | Stream Closing | Use auto-close | try-with-resources |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Path Traversal | Preventing a guest from entering restricted rooms |
    | Resource Exhaustion | Preventing someone from using all water/tanks |
    | Buffering | Carrying books in stacks instead of one-by-one |
    | Stream Closing | Locking storage cabinet after use |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - try-with-resources compiles into a bytecode sequence that handles suppressed exceptions safely.
    - Buffered streams reduce system calls significantly.
    - Path normalization resolves symbolic link and relative path complexities.
    - Excessive open streams exhaust OS-level file descriptor limits (per-process limit).
    - NIO.2 reduces errors by offering atomic operations like `Files.move()`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Never trust user-supplied file paths—always sanitize.
    - Use safe-directory validation (start-with check).
    - Limit file size, buffer size, and input tokens.
    - Always use buffered streams for performance.
    - Prefer try-with-resources for automatic cleanup.
    - Avoid using default encoding—always specify UTF-8 for consistency.
    - Avoid loading untrusted files directly into memory.
    - Monitor open stream count in long-running applications.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Key Idea |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Path Traversal Prevention** | Normalize + validate paths to avoid unauthorized access |
    | **Resource Exhaustion Prevention** | Limit file size, input length, buffer usage |
    | **Buffering Strategies** | Use buffered streams, proper buffer sizes |
    | **Stream Closing Patterns** | Prefer try-with-resources for safe cleanup |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    I/O security and best practices help ensure applications remain safe, fast, and reliable. Proper path validation, resource limitation, buffering, and correct resource management form the foundation of secure and efficient Java I/O design.
    

### 3.5 Java 8+ Features

- **Lambda Expressions**
    
    # **Lambda Expressions**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **lambda expression** in Java (introduced in Java 8) is a concise way to represent a **block of code as a function**, without creating an anonymous inner class.
    
    Lambdas make code more readable, especially when working with **functional interfaces**, Streams API, and functional-style programming.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide cleaner and more compact code
    - Enable functional programming constructs
    - Improve readability and maintainability
    - Reduce boilerplate (especially compared to anonymous classes)
    
    A lambda is essentially a **function without a name**, treated as an **object**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax and Structure**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Lambda General Syntax**
    
    ```java
    (parameters) -> expression
    (parameters) -> { statements }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Forms of Lambda Expressions**
    
    | Form | Example | Description |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | No parameters | `() -> System.out.println("Hi")` | Simple runnable-style lambda |
    | One parameter | `x -> x * x` | Parentheses optional |
    | Multiple parameters | `(a, b) -> a + b` | Require parentheses |
    | Multi-statement body | `(x) -> { int y = x + 1; return y; }` | Requires `{}` and `return` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    ### **Simple Lambda**
    
    ```java
    () -> System.out.println("Hello Lambda");
    
    ```
    
    ### **Lambda with Parameter**
    
    ```java
    name -> System.out.println("Hello " + name);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Lambda Returning Value**
    
    ```java
    (a, b) -> a + b;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Multi-line Lambda**
    
    ```java
    (x, y) -> {
        int sum = x + y;
        return sum;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Lambda Type is Inferred**
    
    Lambdas **do not define their own type**.
    
    They are assigned to a **functional interface reference**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Functional Interfaces with Lambdas**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **functional interface** is an interface containing exactly **one abstract method** (SAM ― Single Abstract Method).
    
    Examples:
    
    - `Runnable`
    - `Callable`
    - `Comparator<T>`
    - `Consumer<T>`
    - `Supplier<T>`
    - `Function<T, R>`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using Lambda with Functional Interface**
    
    ```java
    Runnable r = () -> System.out.println("Running...");
    
    ```
    
    Behind the scenes, the lambda is converted to an instance of the functional interface.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Custom Functional Interface**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Calculator {
        int compute(int a, int b);
    }
    
    Calculator add = (x, y) -> x + y;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Avoid verbose anonymous inner classes
    - Cleaner functional-style code
    - Works seamlessly with Streams API
    
    ---
    
    ### **Comparison: Lambda vs Anonymous Class**
    
    | Aspect | Lambda | Anonymous Class |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Syntax | Short, concise | Verbose |
    | Type | Functional interface | Direct class implementation |
    | this keyword | Refers to enclosing object | Refers to anonymous class instance |
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Variable Capture in Lambdas**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Variable capture means a lambda can access **variables from its enclosing scope**, but only if they are:
    
    - **final**, or
    - **effectively final**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Effectively Final**
    
    A variable is effectively final if it is **assigned once and never changed**, even if not explicitly declared final.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Valid Capture**
    
    ```java
    int base = 10;  // effectively final
    
    Function<Integer, Integer> f = x -> x + base;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Invalid Capture (Compilation Error)**
    
    ```java
    int count = 0;
    
    Function<Integer, Integer> f = x -> {
        count++;    // Error: not effectively final
        return x + count;
    };
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why This Restriction?**
    
    - Lambdas may be executed after the method returns
    - Prevents inconsistent shared state
    - Ensures memory safety and predictability
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Using this and super in Lambdas**
    
    - `this` refers to the **enclosing class instance**, not to a new object (unlike anonymous classes)
    - Lambdas **cannot** declare shadowing variables (same name as outer variables)
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Lambda | A quick note containing a small instruction |
    | Functional interface | A template with one required action |
    | Variable capture | Borrowing a value from outside but not being allowed to modify it |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Lambdas compile to **invokedynamic** bytecode instruction
    - They are implemented as **lightweight function objects** (lambda metafactory)
    - No extra class files are generated (unlike anonymous classes pre-Java 8)
    - Captured variables are treated like variables in closures
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Lambda expressions require functional interfaces.
    - They cannot modify local variables unless they are effectively final.
    - Lambdas support concise, readable code patterns.
    - `this` refers to enclosing class instance.
    - Useful in Streams, Collections, callbacks, events, concurrency, etc.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Key Idea |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Syntax** | `(params) -> { body }` |
    | **Functional Interfaces** | Lambdas implement SAM interfaces |
    | **Variable Capture** | Only final/effectively final variables allowed |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Lambda expressions bring functional programming style to Java. They simplify code, reduce boilerplate, and work seamlessly with functional interfaces and the Streams API. Variable capture rules ensure safety and avoid accidental state modifications.
    
- **Stream API**
    
    # **Stream API**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **Stream API** (introduced in Java 8) provides a **functional, declarative, and pipeline-based approach** to process collections and data sequences.
    
    A **Stream** is a sequence of elements that supports **sequential** and **parallel** aggregate operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Perform bulk operations on data
    - Write clean, declarative code
    - Avoid external iteration (loops) in favor of internal iteration
    - Support lazy evaluation and efficient pipelines
    - Enable parallel processing easily
    
    Streams do **not store data**; they operate on data from a source (collections, arrays, files, generators, etc.).
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Stream Creation**
