    ---
    
    Streams can be created from various sources.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. From Collections**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> list = Arrays.asList(1, 2, 3);
    Stream<Integer> stream = list.stream();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. From Arrays**
    
    ```java
    Stream<String> stream = Arrays.stream(new String[]{"A", "B", "C"});
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Using Stream.of()**
    
    ```java
    Stream<String> s = Stream.of("A", "B", "C");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Infinite Streams**
    
    ### **iterate()**
    
    ```java
    Stream<Integer> s = Stream.iterate(1, n -> n + 1);
    
    ```
    
    ### **generate()**
    
    ```java
    Stream<Double> s = Stream.generate(Math::random);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. From Files (NIO.2)**
    
    ```java
    Stream<String> lines = Files.lines(Paths.get("data.txt"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Primitive Streams**
    
    - `IntStream`
    - `LongStream`
    - `DoubleStream`
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    IntStream.range(1, 10);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Intermediate Operations**
    
    Intermediate operations return **new streams** and are **lazy**, meaning they execute only when a terminal operation is invoked.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. filter()**
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Selects elements that match a condition.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    list.stream()
        .filter(n -> n % 2 == 0)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. map()**
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Transforms each element into another form.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    list.stream()
        .map(n -> n * n)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. sorted()**
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Sorts elements in natural or custom order.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    list.stream()
        .sorted()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Custom Comparator**
    
    ```java
    list.stream()
        .sorted((a, b) -> b - a)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Other Common Intermediate Operations**
    
    - `distinct()`
    - `limit()`
    - `skip()`
    - `flatMap()`
    
    All are **lazy** and build the processing pipeline.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Terminal Operations**
    
    Terminal operations produce a **result** (value, collection, or side effect).
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. forEach()**
    
    ### **Description**
    
    Performs an action for each element.
    
    ```java
    list.stream()
        .forEach(System.out::println);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. collect()**
    
    ### **Description**
    
    Collects stream elements into a container.
    
    ### **To List**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> result = list.stream()
        .filter(n -> n > 5)
        .collect(Collectors.toList());
    
    ```
    
    ### **To Set**
    
    ```java
    Set<Integer> result = list.stream()
        .collect(Collectors.toSet());
    
    ```
    
    ### **To Map**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, Integer> map = list.stream()
        .collect(Collectors.toMap(s -> s, String::length));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. reduce()**
    
    ### **Description**
    
    Aggregates elements into one final result.
    
    ### **Sum**
    
    ```java
    int sum = list.stream()
        .reduce(0, (a, b) -> a + b);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Max**
    
    ```java
    int max = list.stream()
        .reduce(Integer.MIN_VALUE, Integer::max);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Other Terminal Operations**
    
    - `count()`
    - `min()`, `max()`
    - `allMatch()`, `anyMatch()`, `noneMatch()`
    - `findFirst()`, `findAny()`
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Parallel Streams**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Parallel streams divide the data into multiple chunks and process them concurrently using the **ForkJoinPool**.
    
    ```java
    list.parallelStream()
        .filter(n -> n % 2 == 0)
        .forEach(System.out::println);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Faster processing on large datasets
    - Automatic parallelization
    - Ideal for CPU-intensive tasks
    
    ---
    
    ### **Cautions**
    
    Parallel streams **must be used carefully**:
    
    - Not suitable for small datasets
    - Ordering may not be preserved
    - Heavy overhead for synchronization
    - Avoid shared mutable state
    - Performance depends on CPU cores
    
    ---
    
    ### **Custom Parallelism Level**
    
    ```java
    System.setProperty("java.util.concurrent.ForkJoinPool.common.parallelism", "8");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Stream Pipeline Example**
    
    ```java
    List<String> names = Arrays.asList("John", "Alice", "Bob", "Alex");
    
    List<String> result = names.stream()
        .filter(s -> s.startsWith("A"))
        .map(String::toUpperCase)
        .sorted()
        .collect(Collectors.toList());
    
    System.out.println(result);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    [ALEX, ALICE]
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Stream Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Stream** | Conveyor belt with items flowing |
    | **Intermediate ops** | Filters or transformers on the belt |
    | **Terminal ops** | Final collecting or counting station |
    | **Parallel stream** | Multiple workers handling conveyor parts |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Streams use **lazy evaluation** to optimize performance.
    - Behind the scenes, streams use **Spliterator** to traverse data.
    - Parallel streams use the **ForkJoinPool (common pool)**.
    - Reduction operations may require combining partial results.
    - Intermediate operations do not mutate the data source.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Streams are **not** data structures; they do not modify the source.
    - Intermediate operations are **lazy**; terminal operations trigger execution.
    - Avoid modifying external state inside stream operations.
    - Prefer sequential streams unless parallel processing is actually beneficial.
    - Avoid using parallel streams with I/O operations.
    - Streams are **single-use**—once consumed, they cannot be reused.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Stream Creation** | Build streams from collections, arrays, files, generators |
    | **Intermediate Ops** | Transform and filter data (lazy) |
    | **Terminal Ops** | Produce final result (collect, reduce, forEach) |
    | **Parallel Streams** | Multi-threaded stream operations |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The Stream API introduces a powerful, concise, and declarative approach for processing data in Java. With features like lazy evaluation, functional operations, and parallel execution, it enhances both readability and performance when used correctly.
    
- **Optional Class**
    
    # **Optional Class**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `Optional<T>` (introduced in Java 8) is a **container object** that may or may not hold a non-null value.
    
    It is designed to help developers:
    
    - Avoid **NullPointerException**
    - Make null handling **explicit**
    - Write cleaner, more expressive code
    
    `Optional` is **not** a replacement for all nulls—it is mainly used for **return types** where a value may be absent.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Represent optional or missing values safely
    - Avoid defensive null checks
    - Encourage functional-style programming with map/filter
    - Make intention clear: "This value may be empty"
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Avoiding NullPointerException**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Without Optional (Traditional Approach)**
    
    ```java
    String name = user.getName();
    if (name != null) {
        System.out.println(name.toUpperCase());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **With Optional**
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> name = user.getName();
    name.ifPresent(n -> System.out.println(n.toUpperCase()));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **How Optional Prevents NullPointerException**
    
    - Optional **never** holds a null internally
    - Methods enforce explicit handling
    - Encourages safer programming patterns
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Misuse**
    
    Do **not** use Optional for:
    
    - Fields in entities
    - Method parameters
    - Serialization
    - High-performance code (slight overhead)
    
    It is meant for **method return types**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Creating Optional Objects**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Optional.of(value)**
    
    Creates an Optional with a **non-null** value.
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> opt = Optional.of("Hello");
    
    ```
    
    If value is `null`, it throws `NullPointerException`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Optional.ofNullable(value)**
    
    Creates an Optional that may hold `null`.
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> opt = Optional.ofNullable(maybeNull);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Optional.empty()**
    
    Creates an Optional with **no value**.
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> opt = Optional.empty();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases Summary**
    
    | Method | Holds null? | Use case |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `of()` | ❌ No | When value is guaranteed non-null |
    | `ofNullable()` | ✔ Yes | When value may be null |
    | `empty()` | No value | Default empty Optional |
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Common Optional Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. ifPresent()**
    
    Executes a lambda if value exists.
    
    ```java
    opt.ifPresent(v -> System.out.println("Value: " + v));
    
    ```
    
    This is a replacement for:
    
    ```java
    if (value != null) { ... }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. orElse()**
    
    Returns default value if Optional is empty.
    
    ```java
    String name = opt.orElse("Unknown");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. orElseGet()**
    
    Lazily executes supplier **only if empty**.
    
    ```java
    String name = opt.orElseGet(() -> computeDefaultName());
    
    ```
    
    Faster than `orElse()` if default value creation is expensive.
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. orElseThrow()**
    
    Throws exception when value is missing.
    
    ```java
    String name = opt.orElseThrow(() -> new IllegalArgumentException("Missing name"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **E. map()**
    
    Transforms the contained value.
    
    ```java
    Optional<Integer> length =
        Optional.of("Test").map(String::length);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **F. flatMap()**
    
    Used when mapping returns another Optional.
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> city = userOpt.flatMap(User::getCityOptional);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **G. filter()**
    
    Keeps value only if it matches condition.
    
    ```java
    Optional<Integer> opt = Optional.of(10)
        .filter(n -> n > 5);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Example Demonstrating All Concepts**
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> nameOpt = Optional.ofNullable(getName());
    
    // Execute if present
    nameOpt.ifPresent(n -> System.out.println(n.toUpperCase()));
    
    // Provide default
    String name = nameOpt.orElse("Guest");
    
    // Transform value
    int length = nameOpt
                    .map(String::length)
                    .orElse(0);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Optional** | A sealed envelope that may be empty |
    | **ifPresent()** | Open envelope only if something is inside |
    | **orElse()** | Use fallback item if envelope is empty |
    | **map()** | Apply a transformation to the item inside the envelope |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Optional is **not serializable** by design.
    - It is implemented as a simple wrapper around a reference value.
    - Designed for **functional pipelines** and readability, not raw performance.
    - Avoid using Optional in fields to reduce memory overhead.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Use Optional as **return type**, not for fields or parameters.
    - Avoid `Optional.get()` — use safe methods like `orElse`, `orElseThrow`.
    - Prefer `map()` and `flatMap()` for functional transformations.
    - Does not solve all null issues—use judiciously.
    - Avoid nested Optionals (bad design).
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Avoid NPE** | Explicitly represent missing values |
    | **Creation** | `of()`, `ofNullable()`, `empty()` |
    | **Core Methods** | `ifPresent()`, `orElse()`, `map()` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    `Optional` provides a safe and expressive way to handle nullable values — reducing NullPointerException risks and enabling functional-style data processing through rich transformation and defaulting methods.
    
- **Date and Time API (java.time)**
    
    # **Date and Time API (java.time)**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **java.time** package (introduced in Java 8) provides a modern, immutable, thread-safe date and time API inspired by the Joda-Time library.
    
    It replaces the outdated `java.util.Date` and `java.util.Calendar` classes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provide clear, consistent date/time manipulation
    - Support ISO-8601 standards
    - Offer immutable classes (safe in multithreading)
    - Provide strong timezone and offset handling
    - Offer fluent APIs for calculations and formatting
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. LocalDate, LocalTime, LocalDateTime**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. LocalDate**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents a **date without time and without timezone** (e.g., 2025-11-24).
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    LocalDate date = LocalDate.now();
    LocalDate specific = LocalDate.of(2025, 11, 24);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    date.getYear()
    date.getMonth()
    date.plusDays(10)
    date.minusMonths(2)
    date.isLeapYear()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. LocalTime**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents a **time without date and without timezone** (e.g., 10:30:45).
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    LocalTime time = LocalTime.now();
    LocalTime t = LocalTime.of(10, 30, 45);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    time.plusHours(2)
    time.minusMinutes(10)
    time.getSecond()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. LocalDateTime**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents **date + time without timezone**.
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    LocalDateTime dt = LocalDateTime.now();
    LocalDateTime specific = LocalDateTime.of(2025, 11, 24, 10, 30);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    ```java
    dt.plusDays(1)
    dt.minusHours(3)
    dt.withMonth(6)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Application logs
    - Scheduled tasks
    - Basic date/time handling
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. ZonedDateTime and Instant**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. ZonedDateTime**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents **date + time + timezone** information.
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    ZonedDateTime zdt = ZonedDateTime.now();
    ZonedDateTime tokyo = ZonedDateTime.now(ZoneId.of("Asia/Tokyo"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Conversion Examples**
    
    Add timezone to LocalDateTime:
    
    ```java
    LocalDateTime now = LocalDateTime.now();
    ZonedDateTime z = now.atZone(ZoneId.of("Europe/London"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Global applications
    - Event scheduling
    - Converting between time zones
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Instant**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents a timestamp as **seconds and nanoseconds since Unix Epoch** (1970-01-01T00:00:00Z).
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    Instant now = Instant.now();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Conversion**
    
    To/from LocalDateTime:
    
    ```java
    LocalDateTime ldt = LocalDateTime.ofInstant(now, ZoneId.systemDefault());
    Instant inst = ldt.atZone(ZoneId.systemDefault()).toInstant();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Logging timestamps
    - Storing database timestamps
    - Time comparisons
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Period and Duration**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Period**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents **date-based** amount of time (years, months, days).
    
    Example: “2 years and 5 days”.
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    Period p = Period.ofDays(10);
    Period between = Period.between(LocalDate.of(2025,1,1),
                                    LocalDate.of(2025,1,20));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Uses**
    
    - Age calculation
    - Due date differences
    - Iterating date ranges
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Duration**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Represents **time-based** amount of time (hours, minutes, seconds, nanos).
    
    ### **Creation**
    
    ```java
    Duration d = Duration.ofHours(5);
    Duration diff = Duration.between(LocalTime.now(), LocalTime.NOON);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Uses**
    
    - Timer functions
    - Measuring execution time
    - Timeouts and delays
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. DateTimeFormatter**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Used to **format** and **parse** date/time objects.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Predefined Formatters**
    
    ```java
    DateTimeFormatter.ISO_DATE
    DateTimeFormatter.ISO_LOCAL_DATE_TIME
    DateTimeFormatter.RFC_1123_DATE_TIME
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Custom Formatter**
    
    ```java
    DateTimeFormatter fmt = DateTimeFormatter.ofPattern("dd-MM-yyyy HH:mm");
    
    String formatted = LocalDateTime.now().format(fmt);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Parsing**
    
    ```java
    LocalDate date = LocalDate.parse("24-11-2025", fmt);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Locale Support**
    
    ```java
    DateTimeFormatter fmt = DateTimeFormatter
            .ofPattern("dd MMM yyyy", Locale.FRANCE);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Example Program**
    
    ```java
    LocalDate date = LocalDate.now();
    LocalTime time = LocalTime.now();
    LocalDateTime dt = LocalDateTime.now();
    
    ZonedDateTime zdt = ZonedDateTime.now(ZoneId.of("Asia/Kolkata"));
    Instant instant = Instant.now();
    
    Period period = Period.ofMonths(3);
    Duration duration = Duration.ofMinutes(90);
    
    DateTimeFormatter fmt = DateTimeFormatter.ofPattern("yyyy/MM/dd HH:mm");
    String formatted = dt.format(fmt);
    
    System.out.println(formatted);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | LocalDate | Calendar date |
    | LocalTime | Clock time |
    | LocalDateTime | Date + time but no timezone awareness |
    | ZonedDateTime | LocalDateTime + “where in the world” |
    | Instant | Global timestamp |
    | Period | “3 months 10 days” like in a calendar |
    | Duration | Stopwatch measuring seconds |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - All java.time classes are **immutable**.
    - Thread-safe by design.
    - Timezone calculations use the **IANA timezone database**.
    - Instant is the closest modern equivalent to `java.util.Date`.
    - Period uses date units; Duration uses seconds+nanos.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Prefer `java.time` over old Date/Calendar classes.
    - Use Instant for timestamps.
    - Use ZonedDateTime for global applications.
    - Use Period for dates; Duration for times.
    - Always use `DateTimeFormatter` for formatting/parsing.
    - Avoid manual timezone calculations—use ZoneId.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Component | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **LocalDate / LocalTime / LocalDateTime** | Date/time without timezone |
    | **ZonedDateTime** | Full date/time with timezone |
    | **Instant** | Machine timestamp |
    | **Period** | Date-based amount |
    | **Duration** | Time-based amount |
    | **DateTimeFormatter** | Formatting and parsing |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The java.time API offers a robust, modern, and intuitive framework for date and time management—immutable, thread-safe, and feature-rich, designed to replace the legacy APIs entirely.
    
- **Other Features**
    
    # **Other Features**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Method References**
    
    ### **Definition and Explanation**
    
    A **method reference** is a shorthand syntax for using existing methods as lambda expressions.
    
    It improves readability when a lambda simply calls an existing method.
    
    Method references work only with **functional interfaces**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Types of Method References**
    
    | Type | Syntax | Example | Meaning |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static method** | `ClassName::methodName` | `Math::max` | Calls a static method |
    | **Instance method (particular object)** | `obj::methodName` | `System.out::println` | Calls instance method on specific instance |
    | **Instance method (arbitrary object of a type)** | `ClassName::methodName` | `String::toUpperCase` | Calls method on each element |
    | **Constructor reference** | `ClassName::new` | `ArrayList::new` | Creates new object |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    ### **Static Method Reference**
    
    ```java
    Function<Integer, Double> f = Math::sqrt;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Instance Method Reference (Specific Object)**
    
    ```java
    PrintStream ps = System.out;
    Consumer<String> printer = ps::println;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Instance Method Reference (Arbitrary Instance)**
    
    ```java
    List<String> names = List.of("a", "b");
    names.stream().map(String::toUpperCase);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Constructor Reference**
    
    ```java
    Supplier<List<String>> listSupplier = ArrayList::new;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use**
    
    - When lambda simply forwards parameters
    - To improve readability
    - When referencing existing behavior
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Default Methods in Interfaces**
    
    ### **Definition and Explanation**
    
    A **default method** is a method inside an interface that has a method body.
    
    Introduced in Java 8 to allow interface evolution without breaking existing implementations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface Vehicle {
        default void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle starting...");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Features**
    
    - Allows adding new functionality to interfaces
    - Classes implementing the interface **inherit** the method automatically
    - Can be overridden in implementing classes
    
    ---
    
    ### **Multiple Interface Conflict Resolution**
    
    If two interfaces provide the same default method, the class must override it:
    
    ```java
    interface A { default void show() { } }
    interface B { default void show() { } }
    
    class C implements A, B {
        @Override
        public void show() { }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Evolve APIs (like the Java Collections API)
    - Provide helper or utility methods inside interfaces
    - Avoid excessive abstract-class hierarchies
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. CompletableFuture (Asynchronous Programming)**
    
    ### **Definition and Explanation**
    
    `CompletableFuture` (Java 8) is a powerful class for **asynchronous**, **non-blocking**, and **callback**-based programming.
    
    It allows tasks to run in the background and provides a fluent API for chaining.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Build asynchronous pipelines
    - Handle complex workflows (dependent tasks, parallel tasks)
    - Replace `Future` limitations (no chaining, no completion listeners)
    
    ---
    
    ## **Creating a CompletableFuture**
    
    ### **Run task asynchronously**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Void> cf = CompletableFuture.runAsync(() -> {
        System.out.println("Running in background");
    });
    
    ```
    
    ### **Supply a result**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> cf = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Chaining Operations**
    
    ### **thenApply()** → transforms result
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> doubled =
        cf.thenApply(n -> n * 2);
    
    ```
    
    ### **thenAccept()** → consumes result
    
    ```java
    cf.thenAccept(n -> System.out.println("Result: " + n));
    
    ```
    
    ### **thenRun()** → runs without using result
    
    ```java
    cf.thenRun(() -> System.out.println("Done"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Combining Multiple Futures**
    
    ### **thenCombine()**
    
    ```java
    var combined = cf1.thenCombine(cf2, (a, b) -> a + b);
    
    ```
    
    ### **allOf()**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture.allOf(cf1, cf2, cf3).join();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Exception Handling**
    
    ### **exceptionally()**
    
    ```java
    cf.exceptionally(ex -> {
        System.out.println(ex.getMessage());
        return -1;
    });
    
    ```
    
    ### **handle()** → catches and recovers
    
    ```java
    cf.handle((result, ex) -> ex == null ? result : 0);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Running in Custom Thread Pools**
    
    ```java
    ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4);
    
    CompletableFuture<Integer> cf =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 42, executor);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **CompletableFuture Workflow Example**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10)
        .thenApply(n -> n * 2)
        .thenAccept(System.out::println)
        .exceptionally(ex -> { ex.printStackTrace(); return null; });
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Method references | Addressing a saved instruction directly |
    | Default methods | Adding new instructions to a template everyone uses |
    | CompletableFuture | Assigning tasks to assistants and reacting when they finish |
    
    ---
    
    ## **JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Method references compile into lightweight lambda metafactory calls.
    - Default methods are stored in interface bytecode — they work like inherited methods.
    - CompletableFuture uses **ForkJoinPool.commonPool** by default unless custom executor provided.
    - Asynchronous stages may execute in different threads.
    
    ---
    
    ## **Important Points**
    
    - Method references improve readability over simple lambdas.
    - Default methods help evolve interfaces without breaking compatibility.
    - CompletableFuture supports chaining, parallelism, and non-blocking programming.
    - Always handle exceptions in asynchronous pipelines.
    - Avoid blocking operations (`join()`, `get()`) unless necessary.
    
    ---
    
    ## **Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Method References** | Reuse existing methods in lambda-friendly syntax |
    | **Default Methods** | Add implementations to interfaces without breaking code |
    | **CompletableFuture** | Build asynchronous workflows with clean, non-blocking APIs |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    These additional Java features enhance functional programming expressiveness, interface extensibility, and asynchronous computation capabilities in modern Java applications.
    
- **Stream API Deep Dive**
    
    # **Stream API Deep Dive**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The Stream API Deep Dive covers advanced operations and mechanisms that enable powerful, expressive, and high-performance data processing in Java.
    
    This includes:
    
    - Advanced collectors
    - Primitive streams
    - Short-circuiting behaviors
    - Custom collector creation
    - Performance considerations
    
    These features help build optimized, functional-style pipelines for large datasets and complex transformations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Collectors Class (Advanced Collectors)**
    
    The **Collectors** utility class provides factory methods for common collection and aggregation patterns used in **terminal operations** like `collect()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. groupingBy()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Groups stream elements based on a classifier function into a `Map<K, List<T>>` or another downstream collector.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, List<Person>> byCity =
        people.stream().collect(Collectors.groupingBy(Person::getCity));
    
    ```
    
    ### **Grouping with downstream collector**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, Long> countByCity =
        people.stream().collect(
            Collectors.groupingBy(Person::getCity, Collectors.counting())
        );
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. partitioningBy()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Splits elements into two groups (true/false) based on a predicate.
    
    Always returns a **Map<Boolean, List>** unless a downstream collector is supplied.
    
    ```java
    Map<Boolean, List<Person>> adults =
        people.stream().collect(Collectors.partitioningBy(p -> p.getAge() >= 18));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. joining()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Concatenates stream of strings into a single string.
    
    ```java
    String names = people.stream()
                         .map(Person::getName)
                         .collect(Collectors.joining(", "));
    
    ```
    
    ### **With prefix, delimiter, suffix**
    
    ```java
    Collectors.joining(", ", "[", "]");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. toMap()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Collects elements into a map using key and value mapping functions.
    
    ```java
    Map<Integer, String> map = people.stream()
        .collect(Collectors.toMap(Person::getId, Person::getName));
    
    ```
    
    ### **Handling duplicate keys**
    
    ```java
    .toMap(Person::getId, Person::getName, (a, b) -> a)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. summarizingInt(), summarizingDouble(), summarizingLong()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Returns statistics like count, min, max, sum, and average.
    
    ```java
    IntSummaryStatistics stats =
        numbers.stream().collect(Collectors.summarizingInt(n -> n));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. teeing() (Java 12+)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Runs **two collectors in parallel** and merges their results using a merger function.
    
    ### **Example — average and sum together**
    
    ```java
    var result = numbers.stream().collect(
        Collectors.teeing(
            Collectors.summingInt(n -> n),
            Collectors.averagingInt(n -> n),
            (sum, avg) -> Map.of("sum", sum, "avg", avg)
        )
    );
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Primitive Streams (IntStream, LongStream, DoubleStream)**
    
    Primitive streams avoid **boxing/unboxing** overhead and offer specialized operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Methods**
    
    | Stream | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **IntStream** | int-based operations |
    | **LongStream** | long-based operations |
    | **DoubleStream** | double-based operations |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating Primitive Streams**
    
    ### **From values**
    
    ```java
    IntStream s = IntStream.of(1, 2, 3);
    
    ```
    
    ### **From ranges**
    
    ```java
    IntStream.range(1, 5);
    IntStream.rangeClosed(1, 5);
    
    ```
    
    ### **From arrays**
    
    ```java
    IntStream.of(array);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Primitive-specific operations**
    
    ```java
    sum(), average(), max(), min()
    boxed() // converts to Stream<Integer>
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    int sum = IntStream.range(1, 10).sum();
    
    ```
    
    Primitive streams improve performance for numerical work.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Short-Circuiting Operations**
    
    Short-circuit operations can terminate the pipeline early based on conditions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. findFirst()**
    
    Returns the first element (if any) and stops further traversal.
    
    ```java
    Optional<String> first = names.stream().findFirst();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. limit()**
    
    Restricts stream to first N elements.
    
    ```java
    numbers.stream().limit(5);
    
    ```
    
    Often used with infinite streams.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. anyMatch(), allMatch(), noneMatch()**
    
    Stop as soon as the result is determined.
    
    ```java
    boolean ok = numbers.stream().anyMatch(n -> n > 100);
    
    ```
    
    Short-circuiting improves performance significantly.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Custom Collector Implementations**
    
    For specialized collection logic, we can create **custom collectors**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Creating Custom Collector Using `Collector.of()`**
    
    ```java
    Collector<Person, StringBuilder, String> namesCollector =
        Collector.of(
            StringBuilder::new,
            (sb, p) -> sb.append(p.getName()).append(","),
            StringBuilder::append,
            StringBuilder::toString
        );
    
    ```
    
    Usage:
    
    ```java
    String result = people.stream().collect(namesCollector);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Characteristics Flags**
    
    Collectors can specify properties:
    
    - `Collector.Characteristics.UNORDERED`
    - `CONCURRENT`
    - `IDENTITY_FINISH`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Collect into Immutable List**
    
    ```java
    Collector<String, List<String>, List<String>> collector =
        Collector.of(
            ArrayList::new,
            List::add,
            (left, right) -> { left.addAll(right); return left; },
            Collections::unmodifiableList
        );
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Write Custom Collectors**
    
    - Complex aggregation
    - Multi-value summarization
    - Custom immutable results
    - Performance-sensitive data reduction
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Stream Performance Characteristics**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Laziness**
    
    - Intermediate operations are lazy
    - Execution occurs ONLY on terminal operation
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Cost of Boxing**
    
    - Object streams cause auto-boxing
    - Prefer primitive streams for numeric calculations
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Use Parallel Streams Carefully**
    
    Parallel streams are beneficial when:
    
    - Large dataset
    - CPU-bound operations
    - No shared mutable state
    
    Avoid when:
    
    - Using I/O
    - Small datasets
    - High synchronization overhead
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Spliterator Characteristics**
    
    Spliterator determines how streams split work for parallel processing.
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Avoid Side Effects**
    
    Side effects hurt performance and correctness.
    
    Bad:
    
    ```java
    list.stream().forEach(n -> result.add(n)); // risk of concurrency issues
    
    ```
    
    Good:
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> result = list.stream().collect(Collectors.toList());
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Cost of Ordered Streams**
    
    Preserving order slows operations like sorted(), distinct(), limit().
    
    ---
    
    ### **G. Reuse of Streams Not Allowed**
    
    Streams can be consumed once.
    
    ---
    
    ### **H. Memory Efficiency**
    
    Streaming pipelines avoid loading all data into memory.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-World Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Collectors | Different boxes for grouping, partitioning, summarizing items |
    | Primitive streams | Fast conveyor belts specialized for certain item sizes |
    | Short-circuiting | Stopping search after first match found |
    | Custom collector | Building your own packaging machine |
    | Stream performance | Optimizing factory assembly line speed |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - Stream pipeline execution uses internal iteration via Spliterator.
    - Parallel streams use ForkJoinPool (common pool) unless overridden.
    - Collector characteristics influence concurrency and ordering.
    - Boxing/unboxing impacts memory and CPU cost.
    - `teeing()` internally merges the two downstream collectors in one pass.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Use Collectors for grouping, partitioning, joining, mapping, summarizing.
    - Prefer primitive streams for numeric-heavy operations.
    - Short-circuiting operations dramatically reduce processing time.
    - Custom collectors enable specialized aggregation logic.
    - Performance depends heavily on pipeline design and dataset size.
    - Avoid parallel streams unless conditions favor them.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Advanced Collectors** | groupingBy, partitioningBy, joining, toMap, summarizing, teeing |
    | **Primitive Streams** | High-performance numeric processing |
    | **Short-circuiting** | Early termination for efficiency |
    | **Custom Collectors** | Tailored data reduction logic |
    | **Performance** | Depends on laziness, boxing, ordering, and parallelism |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The Stream API deep dive reveals how to harness the full expressive and performance power of streams through advanced collectors, primitive streams, custom reduction logic, and optimized pipeline design.
    
- **CompletableFuture Advanced**
    
    # **CompletableFuture Advanced**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Advanced `CompletableFuture` features enable the creation of **complex asynchronous pipelines** involving:
    
    - Chaining dependent tasks
    - Combining multiple futures
    - Recovering from errors
    - Handling timeouts
    - Completing manually or forcefully
    
    They power highly scalable, non-blocking, reactive applications.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Chaining and Composition**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. thenApply() — Transform Result (Synchronous)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Processes the result of a future and returns a new value.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> future =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10)
                         .thenApply(n -> n * 2); // result: 20
    
    ```
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    - Runs in **same thread** (current or common pool)
    - For **simple transformations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. thenCompose() — Dependent Asynchronous Composition**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Used when one async task depends on the result of another.
    
    Flattens nested futures.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<String> cf =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> "User1")
            .thenCompose(user -> getDetailsAsync(user));
    
    ```
    
    `getDetailsAsync()` returns a `CompletableFuture`.
    
    ### **Equivalent Without thenCompose**
    
    ```java
    future.thenApply(x -> asyncCall(x)); // returns CompletableFuture<CompletableFuture<R>>
    
    ```
    
    `thenCompose()` → preferred for async chaining.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Call API A
    - Then use result to call API B asynchronously
    - Avoid nested futures
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. thenCombine() — Combine Two Independent Futures**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Runs two futures independently and combines their results.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> f1 = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10);
    CompletableFuture<Integer> f2 = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 20);
    
    CompletableFuture<Integer> combined =
        f1.thenCombine(f2, (a, b) -> a + b);  // result: 30
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Parallel DB queries
    - Combine data sources
    - Process results after both tasks finish
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Exception Handling in Async Operations**
    
    CompletableFuture provides powerful error recovery mechanisms.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. exceptionally() — Fallback on Exception**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> result =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10 / 0)
            .exceptionally(ex -> {
                System.out.println(ex.getMessage());
                return -1;
            });
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. handle() — Recovery with Result + Exception**
    
    Runs for **both success or failure**.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Integer> cf =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> 10 / 0)
            .handle((val, ex) -> {
                if (ex != null) return 0;
                return val * 2;
            });
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Clean up
    - Fallback values
    - Logging errors
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. whenComplete() — Observe the Result (Do Not Change)**
    
    ```java
    future.whenComplete((value, ex) -> {
        if (ex != null) System.out.println("Error: " + ex);
    });
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Note**
    
    `whenComplete()` is for **side-effects**, not transformation.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Timeout Handling and Completion Strategies**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. orTimeout() (Java 9+)**
    
    Automatically completes exceptionally if timeout exceeded.
    
    ```java
    future.orTimeout(3, TimeUnit.SECONDS);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. completeOnTimeout() (Java 9+)**
    
    Provides default value if timeout occurs.
    
    ```java
    future.completeOnTimeout("default", 3, TimeUnit.SECONDS);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Manual Timeout Using `completeExceptionally()`**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<String> future = new CompletableFuture<>();
    
    executor.schedule(() ->
        future.completeExceptionally(new TimeoutException())
    , 3, TimeUnit.SECONDS);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Handling Timeouts in Composition**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<String> cf =
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(this::slowTask)
            .completeOnTimeout("fallback", 1, TimeUnit.SECONDS)
            .exceptionally(ex -> "error");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Completion Strategies**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Manual Completion Using complete()**
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<String> cf = new CompletableFuture<>();
    cf.complete("Done");
    
    ```
    
    Useful in bridging callbacks with CompletableFuture.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Forced Exceptional Completion**
    
    ```java
    cf.completeExceptionally(new RuntimeException("Failed"));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Combining with Any or All**
    
    ---
    
    ### **anyOf()**
    
    Completes when any future completes.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture.anyOf(f1, f2, f3).thenAccept(System.out::println);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **allOf()**
    
    Waits for all.
    
    ```java
    CompletableFuture<Void> all = CompletableFuture.allOf(f1, f2, f3);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Minimal Latency Using applyToEither()**
    
    ```java
    f1.applyToEither(f2, value -> "Fastest result: " + value);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | thenCompose() | Task B depends on Task A's output (like cooking ingredients) |
    | thenCombine() | Two independent chefs cooking dishes combined at the end |
    | exceptionally() | Insurance policy if something goes wrong |
    | orTimeout() | Countdown timer for tasks |
    | complete() | Marking a task as finished manually |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Behavior Notes**
    
    - CompletableFuture uses **ForkJoinPool.commonPool** by default.
    - `thenApplyAsync()` or `thenComposeAsync()` run in pool threads unless custom executor provided.
    - Completed futures skip thread scheduling and run inline for performance.
    - Timeouts use scheduled tasks internally.
    - Exceptions propagate through dependency chain until handled.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - `thenApply()` is synchronous; `thenCompose()` is asynchronous flattening.
    - Use `thenCombine()` for parallel independent tasks.
    - Always handle exceptions to avoid silent failures.
    - Use timeouts to prevent infinite waiting.
    - Avoid blocking calls (`join()`, `get()`) in async pipelines.
    - Custom executors improve scalability for heavy async workloads.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Chaining (thenApply)** | Transform result |
    | **Async Composition (thenCompose)** | Chain dependent futures |
    | **Parallel Composition (thenCombine)** | Combine independent tasks |
    | **Exception Handling** | Recover or observe errors |
    | **Timeout Handling** | Fail or provide fallback on delay |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Advanced `CompletableFuture` features support robust asynchronous programming with clean composition patterns, powerful error handling, and effective timeout management—essential for building scalable, reactive Java applications.
    
- **Method References Detailed**
    
    # **Method References (Detailed)**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **method reference** is a shorthand syntax used to refer to an existing method or constructor **without invoking it**.
    
    It is used wherever a **lambda expression** simply calls an existing method.
    
    Method references work only with **functional interfaces**, where the method signature matches the target functional method.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improve readability of lambda expressions
    - Reuse existing methods without rewriting them in lambda form
    - Eliminate boilerplate
    - Make functional-style code more expressive
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Four Types of Method References**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Static Method Reference**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ClassName::staticMethod
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    When the lambda body calls a static method.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Function<Integer, Double> sqrt = Math::sqrt;
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent lambda:
    
    ```java
    x -> Math.sqrt(x)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Instance Method Reference (Specific Object)**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    instance::instanceMethod
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    When calling a method on a particular object.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    PrintStream ps = System.out;
    Consumer<String> printer = ps::println;
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent lambda:
    
    ```java
    s -> ps.println(s)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Instance Method Reference (Arbitrary Object of a Type)**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ClassName::instanceMethod
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    When calling a method on each element of a stream or collection, where the instance is provided by the stream.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = List.of("a", "b", "c");
    list.stream().map(String::toUpperCase);
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent lambda:
    
    ```java
    s -> s.toUpperCase()
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Point**
    
    The target object is supplied automatically by the stream.
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Constructor Reference**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ClassName::new
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    Creating new objects through a functional interface.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Supplier<List<String>> supplier = ArrayList::new;
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent lambda:
    
    ```java
    () -> new ArrayList<>()
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Usage Patterns and Best Practices**
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Replace Simple Lambdas**
    
    Use method references when:
    
    - Lambda only calls one method
    - The lambda body matches directly to a method call
    
    **Example**
    
    ```java
    items.forEach(System.out::println);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Improve Readability in Stream Pipelines**
    
    Before:
    
    ```java
    names.stream().map(name -> name.toUpperCase());
    
    ```
    
    After:
    
    ```java
    names.stream().map(String::toUpperCase);
    
    ```
    
    Cleaner and easier to read.
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Prefer Static References for Utility Methods**
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    list.stream().map(Integer::parseInt);
    
    ```
    
    Avoid:
    
    ```java
    s -> Integer.parseInt(s)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Constructor References for Factory Patterns**
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    Stream<String> names = Stream.of("A", "B");
    Stream<Person> people = names.map(Person::new);
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent to:
    
    ```java
    name -> new Person(name)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **E. Instance Method of Arbitrary Object for Comparator**
    
    ```java
    Comparator<String> cmp = String::compareToIgnoreCase;
    
    ```
    
    Equivalent lambda:
    
    ```java
    (a, b) -> a.compareToIgnoreCase(b)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **F. Use Method References for Clean Callback Code**
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    button.setOnClickListener(this::handleClick);
    
    ```
    
    Cleaner than:
    
    ```java
    event -> this.handleClick(event)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **G. Avoid Overuse**
    
    Do **not** force a method reference if:
    
    - It makes code harder to understand
    - Method resolution becomes ambiguous
    - Lambda is clearer due to contextual naming
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    x -> doSomethingComplex(x)
    
    ```
    
    is sometimes clearer than:
    
    ```java
    ClassName::doSomethingComplex
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Method Reference Type | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Static method | Pointing directly to a tool on a wall |
    | Instance method (specific) | Giving instructions to a specific worker |
    | Instance method (arbitrary) | Assigning task to each worker in a group |
    | Constructor reference | Creating new workers on demand |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Compiles to **invokedynamic** bytecode instruction
    - Uses **lambda metafactory** to generate lightweight functional instances
    - No additional .class files generated
    - Very efficient at runtime compared to anonymous classes
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Important Points**
    
    - Method references require functional interfaces.
    - They are equivalent to simple lambda expressions.
    - Improve readability but should not harm clarity.
    - Constructor references simplify object creation in functional pipelines.
    - Instance method reference of arbitrary object relies on implicit parameter binding.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Summary**
    
    | Type | Syntax | Purpose |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static** | `Class::method` | Refer to static method |
    | **Instance (specific)** | `obj::method` | Call method on given object |
    | **Instance (arbitrary)** | `Class::method` | Call method on elements in stream |
    | **Constructor** | `Class::new` | Create new objects |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Method references provide a concise, expressive alternative to lambdas when simply calling existing methods. They enhance readability, reduce boilerplate, and integrate seamlessly with functional interfaces and the Stream API.
    

### 3.6 Utility Classes and APIs

- **Objects Utility Class** (**NEW**)
    - `requireNonNull()` for validation
    - `equals()`, `hash()` for safe comparisons
    - `checkIndex()` for bounds checking
    - `isNull()`, `nonNull()` for predicate usage
- **Math Utilities** (**NEW**)
    - **Math vs StrictMath** differences
    - **Math context** for decimal precision
    - **Random** and **ThreadLocalRandom** classes
- **System Class Utilities** (**NEW**)
    - **System properties** access
    - **Environment variables** reading
    - **System.nanoTime()** vs **System.currentTimeMillis()**
- **Pattern and Matcher** (**NEW**)
    - **Regular expressions** with Java
    - **Pattern.compile()** and **Matcher** methods
    - **Pattern.asMatchPredicate()** (Java 11+)

### 3.7 Generics

- **Introduction to Generics**
    
    # **Introduction to Generics**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Generics** in Java allow classes, interfaces, and methods to operate on **typed objects** while providing **compile-time type safety**.
    
    They enable developers to define data structures and algorithms that work with different object types without losing type correctness.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Avoid runtime `ClassCastException`
    - Enable compile-time checking of types
    - Reduce or eliminate explicit casts
    - Write reusable, type-parameterized code
    
    Generics were introduced in Java 5 to improve type safety and support strongly typed collections.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Type Safety**
    
    ### **Meaning**
    
    Type safety ensures that objects inserted into a collection (or passed into a generic class/method) are of a **consistent, expected type**.
    
    ### **Without Generics (Pre-Java 5)**
    
    Collections stored `Object`, allowing any type:
    
    ```java
    List list = new ArrayList();
    list.add("Hello");
    list.add(10);  // Allowed — unsafe!
    
    ```
    
    Runtime error occurs only when retrieving and casting.
    
    ### **With Generics**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = new ArrayList<>();
    list.add("Hello");
    list.add(10);  // Compile-time error
    
    ```
    
    The compiler guarantees type correctness.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Compile-time Type Checking**
    
    ### **How It Works**
    
    The compiler validates type compatibility during compilation, preventing invalid type insertions or operations.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    List<Integer> numbers = new ArrayList<>();
    numbers.add(10);
    numbers.add("Hello");  // Compile-time error
    
    ```
    
    The compiler blocks incorrect operations early, reducing runtime failures.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefit**
    
    - Errors are detected **before execution**
    - Eliminates unexpected `ClassCastException`
    - Encourages correct API usage
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Eliminating Casts**
    
    ### **Without Generics**
    
    ```java
    List list = new ArrayList();
    list.add("Java");
    
    String s = (String) list.get(0); // Must cast manually
    
    ```
    
    Casting is error-prone and can fail at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **With Generics**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = new ArrayList<>();
    list.add("Java");
    
    String s = list.get(0);  // No cast needed
    
    ```
    
    The compiler already knows the list contains String values.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Cleaner and more readable code
    - Safer type usage
    - Reduces bugs related to invalid casting
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-World Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Type safety | Putting items only into correctly labeled boxes |
    | Compile-time checking | Supervisor checking the packing before shipment |
    | Eliminating casts | Automatically retrieving items without guessing their type |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Generic type information is **erased** at runtime due to **type erasure**.
    - The compiler inserts implicit casts after verifying type safety.
    - Bytecode maintains raw types but keeps generics for compile-time checks.
    - Generic classes result in only **one bytecode version**, reused for all type parameters.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - Generics enforce type safety at compile time.
    - Remove the need for explicit (and unsafe) type casts.
    - Improve readability and maintainability.
    - Core part of modern Java collections and APIs.
    - Generics exist only at compile-time (erased at runtime).
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Type Safety** | Ensures consistent type usage |
    | **Compile-time Checking** | Prevents type errors before execution |
    | **Eliminate Casts** | Avoids runtime failures and manual casting |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Generics provide a powerful mechanism for writing safer, cleaner, and more expressive Java code. They enforce type safety at compile-time and eliminate the need for explicit casts, reducing runtime errors and improving code quality.
    
- **Generic Classes and Interfaces**
    
    # **Generic Classes and Interfaces**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Generic classes and interfaces allow you to define **type-parameterized templates** that operate on different data types without compromising type safety.
    
    They make code reusable, flexible, and strongly typed by introducing **type parameters** such as `<T>`, `<K, V>`, etc.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Create reusable components
    - Avoid duplication for different types
    - Maintain compile-time type safety
    - Enable flexible type constraints with bounds
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Type Parameters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **type parameter** is a placeholder for a type, written inside angle brackets (`<>`) in class or interface definitions.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Box<T> {
        private T value;
        public void set(T value) { this.value = value; }
        public T get() { return value; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    ```java
    Box<String> b1 = new Box<>();
    b1.set("Hello");
    
    Box<Integer> b2 = new Box<>();
    b2.set(100);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Common Type Parameter Names**
    
    - `T` → Type
    - `E` → Element
    - `K` → Key
    - `V` → Value
    - `R` → Result
    
    These are conventions used in Java Collections.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Multiple Type Parameters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A generic class or interface can have **more than one type parameter**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Pair<K, V> {
        private K key;
        private V value;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    ```java
    Pair<String, Integer> p = new Pair<>("Age", 30);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Represent key-value structures
    - More expressive data models
    - Avoid creating separate classes for combinations of types
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Bounded Type Parameters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Bounds restrict the types that can be used as type arguments.
    
    Useful when you need operations that only certain types support.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Upper Bounds (`extends`)**
    
    Restrict type to a class or interface and its subclasses.
    
    ```java
    class NumberBox<T extends Number> { }
    
    ```
    
    Usage:
    
    ```java
    NumberBox<Integer> n1 = new NumberBox<>();  // OK
    NumberBox<String> n2 = new NumberBox<>();   // Error
    
    ```
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - To use methods available only in upper-bound types
    - Example: numeric operations on Number subclasses
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Multiple Bounds**
    
    A type can extend **one class** and implement **multiple interfaces**:
    
    ```java
    <T extends ClassName & Interface1 & Interface2>
    
    ```
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    class MultiBound<T extends Number & Comparable<T>> { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Lower Bounds (`super`) — In Methods (Wildcards)**
    
    Lower bounds apply mostly in wildcards, not type parameters of classes.
    
    ```java
    <? super Integer>
    
    ```
    
    This is used in collections for **writing** values safely.
    
    ---
    
    ### **5. Generic Interfaces**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Interfaces can also be generic.
    
    ```java
    interface Container<T> {
        void add(T value);
        T get();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Implementation**
    
    ```java
    class StringContainer implements Container<String> {
        public void add(String value) { }
        public String get() { return "Hello"; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    Or:
    
    ```java
    class GenericContainer<T> implements Container<T> {
        public void add(T value) { }
        public T get() { return null; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Cases**
    
    - Comparable interface:
    
    ```java
    interface Comparable<T> {
        int compareTo(T o);
    }
    
    ```
    
    - Collection interface:
    
    ```java
    interface List<E> { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Examples**
    
    ### **A. Generic Stack**
    
    ```java
    class Stack<T> {
        private List<T> list = new ArrayList<>();
        void push(T value) { list.add(value); }
        T pop() { return list.remove(list.size() - 1); }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Multi-Type Generic Class**
    
    ```java
    class Tuple<A, B, C> {
        A first;
        B second;
        C third;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Bounded Type Parameter Example**
    
    ```java
    class Calculator<T extends Number> {
        double square(T num) {
            return num.doubleValue() * num.doubleValue();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-World Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Type parameter | A placeholder variable in a template |
    | Multiple parameters | A form with multiple blanks for different info |
    | Bounded types | Restricting entries: “Only students allowed” |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Generics use **type erasure**, so runtime type information is removed.
    - Compiler inserts type checks and casts automatically.
    - Only one bytecode version exists regardless of type parameter.
    - Bounds help compiler enforce method availability.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Type parameters define generic class/template behavior.
    - Multiple type parameters support richer models (e.g., Pair, Map entries).
    - Bounded types enforce constraints and enable type-specific operations.
    - Generics improve safety and expressiveness without runtime overhead.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Type Parameters** | Define placeholder types for classes/interfaces |
    | **Multiple Parameters** | Work with several related or paired types |
    | **Bounded Parameters** | Restrict acceptable types and enable type-specific methods |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Generic classes and interfaces let developers build flexible, type-safe, and reusable components. Through type parameters, multiple type slots, and bounded constraints, Java generics provide powerful compile-time guarantees while keeping runtime behavior efficient through type erasure.
    
- **Generic Methods**
    
    # **Generic Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **generic method** is a method that declares its own **type parameters**, independent of the class’s generic parameters.
    
    This allows the method to operate on **different types** while maintaining type safety.
    
    Generic methods can exist in:
    
    - Generic classes
    - Non-generic classes
    - Interfaces
    - Static utility classes (e.g., `Collections`, `Arrays`)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Increase reusability of methods
    - Enable type-safe operations across multiple types
    - Allow flexible method behavior without restricting the entire class
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Type Inference**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Type inference** is the compiler’s ability to automatically determine the type argument of a generic method based on the method's parameters and context.
    
    ### **General Syntax**
    
    ```java
    <T> T methodName(T value) { ... }
    
    ```
    
    The `<T>` before the return type introduces the method’s type parameter.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Basic Type Inference**
    
    ```java
    public static <T> void print(T value) {
        System.out.println(value);
    }
    
    print("Hello");   // T = String
    print(100);       // T = Integer
    
    ```
    
    The compiler infers `T` based on input type.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Type Inference in Return Type**
    
    ```java
    public static <T> T identity(T x) {
        return x;
    }
    
    String s = identity("Java");
    Integer n = identity(10);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Method Call Context Affects Inference**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = Collections.emptyList();
    
    ```
    
    Compiler infers:
    
    ```
    T = String
    
    ```
    
    If context is ambiguous:
    
    ```java
    var list = Collections.<String>emptyList();
    
    ```
    
    We can specify the type explicitly.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Bounded Type Parameters in Methods**
    
    Generic methods can declare **upper-bounded** or **lower-bounded** type parameters.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Upper Bounded Type Parameters**
    
    Syntax:
    
    ```java
    <T extends Number> void display(T value)
    
    ```
    
    Meaning:
    
    - `T` must be `Number` or subclass (`Integer`, `Double`, etc.)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Upper Bound**
    
    ```java
    public static <T extends Number> double square(T num) {
        return num.doubleValue() * num.doubleValue();
    }
    
    square(10);      // OK (Integer)
    square(3.14);    // OK (Double)
    square("Hi");    // Error
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Multiple Bounds**
    
    Syntax:
    
    ```java
    <T extends ClassName & Interface1 & Interface2>
    
    ```
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    public static <T extends Number & Comparable<T>> T max(T a, T b) {
        return a.compareTo(b) >= 0 ? a : b;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Lower-Bounded Type Parameters (Methods with Wildcards)**
    
    Actual methods cannot declare lower bounds in the type parameter section
    
    but can use **wildcards** in parameters:
    
    ```java
    public static void addNumber(List<? super Integer> list) {
        list.add(10);
    }
    
    ```
    
    Useful for write operations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Examples**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Generic Swap Method**
    
    ```java
    public static <T> void swap(T[] arr, int i, int j) {
        T temp = arr[i];
        arr[i] = arr[j];
        arr[j] = temp;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Generic Method with Multiple Type Parameters**
    
    ```java
    public static <K, V> void printPair(K key, V value) {
        System.out.println(key + " = " + value);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Bounded Generic Method**
    
    ```java
    public static <T extends Number> int compare(T a, T b) {
        return Double.compare(a.doubleValue(), b.doubleValue());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Using Generic Methods in Non-generic Class**
    
    ```java
    class Util {
        public static <T> T getFirst(List<T> list) {
            return list.get(0);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Generic method | A universal tool that adapts to whatever part you attach |
    | Type inference | Tool automatically adjusts size based on the bolt |
    | Bounded parameter | Tool works only for bolts within a specific size range |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Compiler applies type inference based on parameter types and return context.
    - Bounds allow compiler to insert safe implicit casts.
    - Generic information still erased at runtime (type erasure).
    - Overloaded generic methods may cause **type inference ambiguity**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - Type parameters must precede return type in a method declaration.
    - Generic methods work even in non-generic classes.
    - Type inference simplifies generic method calls.
    - Bounds restrict allowed types and enable specific operations.
    - Use multiple bounds to combine class + interfaces.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Type Inference** | Compiler deduces type argument automatically |
    | **Upper Bounds** | Restrict types to subclass hierarchy |
    | **Multiple Bounds** | Combine class and interface constraints |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Generic methods increase code flexibility by allowing type-parameterized behavior at the method level. Type inference simplifies usage, and bounded parameters enable precise control and safe operations on specific categories of types.
    
- **Wildcards**
    
    # **Wildcards**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Wildcards in generics provide **flexibility** when dealing with parameterized types.
    
    They allow unknown or partially constrained types in method parameters, making APIs more reusable.
    
    Three main wildcard forms are:
    
    - **Unbounded wildcard**: `<?>`
    - **Upper bounded wildcard**: `<? extends Type>`
    - **Lower bounded wildcard**: `<? super Type>`
    
    Wildcards are used mostly with **generic method parameters**, especially collections.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Accept broader ranges of generic types
    - Ensure type safety while allowing flexibility
    - Enable reading/writing rules for collections
    - Support PECS principle: **Producer Extends, Consumer Super**
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Unbounded Wildcards: `<?>`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `<?>` represents an **unknown type**.
    
    Useful when operation does **not depend on the element type**.
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Only reading from the structure
    - Printing or iterating elements
    - Methods that treat all types equally
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public static void printList(List<?> list) {
        for (Object obj : list) {
            System.out.println(obj);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Restrictions**
    
    - Cannot add elements (except `null`)
    
    ```java
    list.add("Hi");     // Error
    list.add(null);     // OK
    
    ```
    
    ### **Reason**
    
    Compiler cannot verify type safety since the actual type is unknown.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Upper Bounded Wildcards: `<? extends Type>`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Means “**any type that is Type or a subclass of Type**”.
    
    ```java
    List<? extends Number> numbers;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Typical Use Case**
    
    Used when the list is a **producer of data** → you intend to **read** values.
    
    ### **Example — Reading**
    
    ```java
    public static void sum(List<? extends Number> list) {
        double total = 0;
        for (Number n : list) {
            total += n.doubleValue();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Characteristics**
    
    - Allows reading elements as `Type` (e.g., `Number`)
    - **Does NOT allow adding** elements (except `null`)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why?**
    
    Compiler cannot be sure of the actual type:
    
    If list is `List<Integer>`, adding a `Double` breaks type safety.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Lower Bounded Wildcards: `<? super Type>`**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Means “**any type that is Type or a superclass of Type**”.
    
    ```java
    List<? super Integer> list;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    Used when the list is a **consumer** → you intend to **write** values.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Writing**
    
    ```java
    public static void addNumbers(List<? super Integer> list) {
        list.add(10);
        list.add(20);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Characteristics**
    
    - Can add `Integer` or its subclasses
    - Reading returns `Object` (most general type)
    
    ```java
    Object obj = list.get(0);   // Only safe type
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage Pattern**
    
    Ideal for:
    
    - Adding values
    - Sorting where comparator type must accept subtypes
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. PECS Principle**
    
    ### **PECS → Producer Extends, Consumer Super**
    
    | Wildcard | Meaning | Usage |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `<? extends T>` | Read as T | Producer |
    | `<? super T>` | Write T | Consumer |
    
    **Producer Extends**
    
    Use `extends` when reading values (they produce data for you).
    
    **Consumer Super**
    
    Use `super` when writing values (they consume data you give them).
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison Table**
    
    | Wildcard Type | Syntax | Can Read? | Can Write? | Best Use |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Unbounded** | `<?>` | Yes (as Object) | No | General operations |
    | **Upper Bounded** | `<? extends T>` | Yes (as T) | No | Producers (read) |
    | **Lower Bounded** | `<? super T>` | Yes (as Object) | Yes (T or subtypes) | Consumers (write) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Examples**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Unbounded Wildcard**
    
    ```java
    void print(List<?> list) {
        list.forEach(System.out::println);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Upper Bounded Wildcard**
    
    ```java
    double total(List<? extends Number> nums) {
        return nums.stream()
            .mapToDouble(Number::doubleValue)
            .sum();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Lower Bounded Wildcard**
    
    ```java
    void addIntegers(List<? super Integer> list) {
        list.add(1);
        list.add(2);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | `<?>` | An unlabeled container — you can look inside but not add items |
    | `<? extends T>` | A fruit basket containing any fruit subtype — safe to take fruits out |
    | `<? super T>` | A basket that accepts apples or any fruit supertype — safe to put apples in |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Wildcards exist at **compile time only**; erased at runtime due to type erasure.
    - Type bounds ensure compile-time safety for reading/writing.
    - Compiler generates bridge casts to maintain runtime consistency.
    - Wildcards assist overload resolution and avoid ambiguity.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - `<?>` is useful when only reading the structure.
    - `<? extends T>` supports reading values of type `T`.
    - `<? super T>` supports writing values of type `T`.
    - Wildcards enforce PECS rules to maintain type safety.
    - Cannot add elements to `extends` collections; cannot safely read from `super` collections as specific type.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    | Wildcard | Meaning | Usage |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `<?>` | Unknown type | Read-only, generic operations |
    | `<? extends T>` | T or subclass | Producing values |
    | `<? super T>` | T or superclass | Consuming values |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Wildcards make generics more flexible and powerful.
    
    Unbounded wildcards support general-purpose operations, upper bounds allow safe reading, and lower bounds allow safe writing—guided by the PECS principle for predictable, type-safe behavior.
    
- **Type Erasure**
    
    # **Type Erasure**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Type erasure** is the process by which the Java compiler **removes all generic type information** during compilation.
    
    Generics exist **only at compile time**, ensuring type safety, but the compiled bytecode contains **raw types** compatible with older Java versions (backward compatibility with Java 1.4 and earlier).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Maintain backward compatibility with pre-generic Java
    - Ensure generic classes generate **only one version** of bytecode
    - Avoid runtime overhead or metadata bloat
    
    After erasure:
    
    - `List<String>` becomes `List`
    - `T` is replaced with its **bound** (or `Object` if unbounded)
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. How Generics Are Implemented (Type Erasure Process)**
    
    Java uses **compile-time type checking** + **runtime type erasure**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Compile-time Phase**
    
    Compiler:
    
    - Enforces generic constraints
    - Ensures type safety
    - Inserts necessary type casts
    - Resolves overloaded methods with generic types
    - Generates **bridge methods** if required
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Erasure Phase (After Compilation)**
    
    The compiler removes type parameters:
    
    | At Compile Time | After Erasure |
    | --- | --- |
    | `List<String>` | `List` |
    | `Map<K, V>` | `Map` |
    | `T extends Number` | `Number` |
    | `T` (unbounded) | `Object` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Before Erasure**
    
    ```java
    class Box<T> {
        T value;
        T get() { return value; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **After Erasure**
    
    ```java
    class Box {
        Object value;
        Object get() { return value; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Compiler Inserts Casts Automatically**
    
    ```java
    String s = box.get();
    
    ```
    
    Becomes:
    
    ```java
    String s = (String) box.get();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Bridge Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **bridge method** is a synthetic (compiler-generated) method created to preserve **polymorphism** when generics interact with type erasure.
    
    Required when:
