    - A subclass narrows the return type
    - Implements generic interfaces with more specific types
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example — Bridge Method Scenario**
    
    ### **Generic Class**
    
    ```java
    class Parent<T> {
        T getValue() { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Child Class**
    
    ```java
    class Child extends Parent<String> {
        String getValue() { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    After erasure:
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        Object getValue() { ... }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        String getValue() { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    To maintain overriding relationship, compiler creates:
    
    ```java
    // Bridge method
    Object getValue() {
        return getValue();  // calls String getValue()
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Purpose:**
    
    Ensure method overriding works correctly after erasure.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Bridge Method Properties**
    
    - Synthetic (not written by programmer)
    - Visible in reflection
    - Crucial for polymorphism with generics
    - Result of type erasure limitations
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Limitations of Generics**
    
    Because generics use type erasure, Java generics have several inherent limitations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. No Primitive Type Generics**
    
    Cannot use primitives like `int`, `double`:
    
    ```java
    List<int>     // NOT allowed
    List<Integer> // Allowed
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. No Runtime Type Information (RTTI)**
    
    Generic type parameters are erased, so:
    
    ### **Cannot Check Generic Type at Runtime**
    
    ```java
    if (obj instanceof List<String>)   // Error
    
    ```
    
    ### **Cannot Get Class of T**
    
    ```java
    T.class   // Not allowed
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Cannot Create Generic Arrays**
    
    Runtime type information is missing, so arrays of generics are unsafe.
    
    ```java
    List<String>[] arr = new List<String>[5];   // Error
    
    ```
    
    Reason: arrays are reified (store runtime type), but generics are erased.
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Cannot Instantiate Type Parameters**
    
    ```java
    class Box<T> {
        T value = new T(); // Error
    }
    
    ```
    
    Because type information does not exist at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **E. No Overload by Generic Parameter**
    
    Method signatures cannot differ only by generic type parameter.
    
    ```java
    void m(List<String> list)
    void m(List<Integer> list)     // Error: same erasure → duplicate method
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **F. Complicated Wildcard & Bounds Constraints**
    
    “Producer Extends, Consumer Super” (PECS) required because of erasure.
    
    ---
    
    ## **G. Generics Do Not Work with Exceptions**
    
    Cannot create generic exception types:
    
    ```java
    class MyException<T> extends Exception { }  // Error
    
    ```
    
    Cannot catch by generic type:
    
    ```java
    catch (T e) { }  // Not allowed
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **H. Reflection Shows Only Raw Types**
    
    Generic type parameters (except annotations in signatures) do not exist at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Type erasure | Erasing label on a container after verifying contents |
    | Bridge methods | A translator helping two versions of a rule match each other |
    | Generics limitations | Tools that work at design time, but disappear during operation |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - JVM has **no awareness** of generics
    - All generic checks occur **before** bytecode creation
    - Compiler adds casts and bridge methods
    - At runtime, everything becomes raw types
    - Reflection sees type erasure but can see **generics metadata** (annotations) in class files
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - Generics exist only at compile time; JVM sees raw types.
    - Compiler adds implicit casts during erasure.
    - Bridge methods preserve polymorphism.
    - Several advanced features (generic arrays, runtime type checks) are prohibited due to erasure.
    - Bounds help retain compile-time safety despite erasure.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Meaning |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Type Erasure** | Compiler removes generic type info → raw types in bytecode |
    | **Bridge Methods** | Compiler-created methods to maintain overriding rules |
    | **Limitations** | No primitive generics, no generic arrays, no runtime type checks, no generic exceptions |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Type erasure allows generics to coexist with older Java versions but introduces limitations. The compiler enforces type safety at compile time, adds casts and bridge methods, and produces raw-type bytecode, making generics a powerful but compile-time-only feature in Java.
    
- **Advanced Generic Concepts**
    
    # **Advanced Generic Concepts**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Generic Type Inference Improvements (Java 7+ Diamond Operator)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Type inference is the compiler's ability to automatically detect a generic type argument without explicitly specifying it.
    
    ### **Diamond Operator `<>` (Java 7+)**
    
    Introduced to simplify generic object creation.
    
    ### **Before Java 7**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, List<Integer>> map = new HashMap<String, List<Integer>>();
    
    ```
    
    ### **After Java 7**
    
    ```java
    Map<String, List<Integer>> map = new HashMap<>();
    
    ```
    
    The compiler infers:
    
    ```
    <String, List<Integer>>
    
    ```
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Reduces redundancy
    - Enhances readability
    - Eliminates verbose generic declarations
    
    ### **Type Inference in Method Calls**
    
    ```java
    List<String> list = Collections.emptyList(); // inferred as List<String>
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Generic Constructor Type Inference**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Constructors can also declare their own type parameters, independent of the class's type parameters.
    
    ### **Example — Generic Constructor**
    
    ```java
    class Display {
        <T> Display(T value) {
            System.out.println(value);
        }
    }
    
    new Display("Hello");
    new Display(100);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Generic Constructor in Generic Class**
    
    ```java
    class Wrapper<T> {
        <U> Wrapper(U value) {
            System.out.println("Value: " + value);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    When constructor behavior depends on different type parameters than the class itself.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Recursive Generic Types (Self-Referential Generics)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A recursive type refers to itself in its type parameter.
    
    Useful for creating type-safe fluent APIs and comparisons.
    
    ### **Common Example**
    
    ```java
    interface Comparable<T> {
        int compareTo(T o);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Advanced Example**
    
    ```java
    class Node<T extends Node<T>> {
        T next;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Fluent builders
    - Enum-like class hierarchies
    - Strengthens type constraints
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Generic Array Creation: Limitations and Workarounds**
    
    ### **Limitation**
    
    Java forbids directly creating arrays of generic types:
    
    ```java
    T[] arr = new T[10];   // Error
    List<String>[] lists = new List<String>[5]; // Error
    
    ```
    
    **Reason:** arrays need runtime **reified types**, while generics use **type erasure**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Workarounds**
    
    ### **A. Create Object Array + Cast**
    
    ```java
    @SuppressWarnings("unchecked")
    T[] arr = (T[]) new Object[size];
    
    ```
    
    ### **B. Pass `Class<T>` and use reflection**
    
    ```java
    T[] arr = (T[]) Array.newInstance(clazz, size);
    
    ```
    
    ### **C. Prefer Collections Instead of Arrays**
    
    Use:
    
    - `List<T>`
    - `ArrayList<T>`
    - `LinkedList<T>`
    
    ### **D. Use `List<T>[]` only with caution**
    
    Because generics + arrays = potential heap pollution.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Intersection Types in Wildcards**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Intersection types allow specifying **multiple bounds** using `&`.
    
    Used in generics and wildcards.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    <T extends A & B & C>
    
    ```
    
    Where:
    
    - A must be a class (only one)
    - B, C, ... must be interfaces
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public <T extends Number & Comparable<T>> T max(T a, T b) {
        return a.compareTo(b) >= 0 ? a : b;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Intersection Type in Wildcard Capture**
    
    ```java
    <? extends Number & Comparable<?>>
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - Sorting
    - Numeric comparisons
    - Ensuring multiple type requirements simultaneously
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Generic Exception Handling**
    
    ### **Limitation**
    
    Java does **not** allow generic classes to extend `Throwable`.
    
    ```java
    class MyException<T> extends Exception { } // Error
    
    ```
    
    ### **Why?**
    
    Type erasure removes type parameters at runtime → exception handling becomes ambiguous.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Generic Catch Block Not Allowed**
    
    ```java
    catch (T e) { }    // Not allowed
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Allowed: Generic Methods Throwing Exceptions**
    
    You **can** declare a generic method that throws exceptions:
    
    ```java
    public <T extends Exception> void throwIt(T e) throws T {
        throw e;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    Re-throwing exceptions while preserving original type.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-World Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Diamond operator | Automatically filling form details without retyping |
    | Recursive generics | Flowcharts referencing themselves |
    | Generic constructor | Specialized behavior during object creation |
    | Generic array limits | Boxes that don’t retain labels after shipping |
    | Intersection types | Employees required to have multiple certifications |
    | Generic exceptions | Not labeling boxes of errors with dynamic types |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Type inference relies on contextual analysis by the compiler.
    - Recursive generics ensure strict type compatibility at compile-time.
    - Arrays remain reified at runtime, conflicting with type-erased generics.
    - Intersection types influence method resolution and overloads.
    - Generic exception limitations stem from erasure and catch-block semantics.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Diamond operator enhances type inference (Java 7+).
    - Constructors can declare their own generic parameters.
    - Recursive generics support fluent APIs and robust constraints.
    - Generic arrays cannot be created directly due to type erasure.
    - Intersection types allow multiple constraints.
    - Generic exceptions are restricted but generic throw methods are allowed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Concept | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Diamond Operator** | Simplifies type inference |
    | **Generic Constructors** | Independent generic behavior during object creation |
    | **Recursive Generics** | Self-referential type safety patterns |
    | **Array Limitations** | Resolves conflict between arrays + erasure |
    | **Intersection Types** | Multiple constraints on type parameters |
    | **Generic Exceptions** | Possible only via generic methods, not classes |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Advanced generics expand Java’s type system beyond basic parameterization. With type inference, recursive type constraints, bounded intersections, and careful handling of arrays and exceptions, generics provide expressive, type-safe, and powerful abstractions while operating within the limitations of type erasure.
    

### 3.8 Multithreading and Concurrency

- **Thread Fundamentals**
    - Process vs Thread
    - Main thread
    - Thread states and lifecycle
- **Creating Threads**
    - Extending Thread class
    - Implementing Runnable interface
    - Implementing Callable interface
- **Thread Synchronization**
    - synchronized keyword
    - synchronized methods and blocks
    - volatile keyword
    - Thread safety issues
- **Concurrency Utilities (java.util.concurrent)**
    - Executor framework
    - ThreadPoolExecutor
    - Future and Callable
    - Lock interface and ReentrantLock
- **Concurrent Collections**
    - ConcurrentHashMap
    - CopyOnWriteArrayList
    - BlockingQueue implementations
- **Advanced Topics**
    - Deadlock, livelock, starvation
    - Atomic variables
    - CountDownLatch, CyclicBarrier, Semaphore
    - CompletableFuture (Java 8+)
- **Modern Concurrency Features** (**NEW**)
    - **Virtual Threads (Project Loom)** (**CRITICAL**)
        - Platform threads vs virtual threads
        - `Thread.ofVirtual()`, `Thread.startVirtualThread()`
        - Structured concurrency principles
    - **Structured Task Scope** (Java 21+)
    - **ThreadLocal** and **InheritableThreadLocal**
    - **ForkJoinPool** and work-stealing algorithms
- **Reactive Programming** (**NEW**)
    - **Reactive Streams** and **Flow API** (Java 9+)
    - **Backpressure** handling strategies
    - **Publisher-Subscriber** patterns
- **Advanced CompletableFuture** (**NEW**)
    - **Exception handling** in async chains
    - **Timeout handling** patterns
    - **Combining multiple futures**
    - **Completion strategies**

### 3.9 Annotations and Reflection

- **Built-in Annotations**
    
    # **Annotations — Built-in Annotations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Annotations in Java are special metadata tags applied to classes, methods, fields, and other elements to provide **additional information** to the compiler, JVM, or frameworks.
    
    They do **not** affect program logic but serve as instructions or hints.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improve readability
    - Assist compiler in validations
    - Enable framework-level behaviors (e.g., Spring)
    - Provide metadata for tools and libraries
    
    This section focuses on **built-in Java annotations**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. @Override**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Indicates that a method is intended to **override** a superclass method.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Prevents accidental overloading instead of overriding
    - Compiler verifies correctness of method signature
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        void show() {}
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        void show() { }  // Valid override
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Throws an error if the method does not correctly override a parent method.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. @Deprecated**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Marks an element (class, method, field) as **deprecated**, meaning it should not be used anymore.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Warns developers about outdated APIs
    - Indicates upcoming removal
    - Encourages migration to newer alternatives
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    @Deprecated
    void oldMethod() { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **New Parameters (Java 9+)**
    
    ```java
    @Deprecated(since="1.8", forRemoval=true)
    
    ```
    
    - `since` → specifies version
    - `forRemoval` → indicates likely future removal
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. @SuppressWarnings**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Suppresses compiler warnings for certain code sections.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Used when developer intentionally writes code that triggers warnings.
    
    ### **Common Values**
    
    - `"unchecked"` (for generic casts)
    - `"deprecation"`
    - `"rawtypes"`
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @SuppressWarnings("unchecked")
    List<String> list = (List<String>) rawList;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Important Note**
    
    Use sparingly; do not suppress critical warnings.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. @SafeVarargs**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Indicates that a method using **varargs of generic types** is safe and will not cause heap pollution.
    
    ### **Applicable To**
    
    - Constructors
    - Final methods
    - Static methods
    
    (Not allowed on non-final instance methods due to overriding risk.)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @SafeVarargs
    public static <T> void print(T... items) {
        for (T item : items) System.out.println(item);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Prevents varargs + generics warnings:
    
    ```
    Unchecked generic array creation for varargs parameter
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Restrictions**
    
    Cannot apply to:
    
    ```java
    public void m(T... x)   // instance method not final → ERROR
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. @FunctionalInterface**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Marks an interface as a **Functional Interface**, meaning it has exactly **one abstract method**.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Enforces SAM (Single Abstract Method) constraint
    - Allows use with Lambda Expressions
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Calculator {
        int compute(int a, int b);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Compiler Behavior**
    
    - Error if more than one abstract method is present
    - Allows default and static methods
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Annotation | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **@Override** | Label saying “I’m replacing this function in the parent version.” |
    | **@Deprecated** | A road sign: “Use alternate route” |
    | **@SuppressWarnings** | Telling a reviewer to ignore specific issues |
    | **@SafeVarargs** | Certifying a container as safe for variable items |
    | **@FunctionalInterface** | A contract for “exactly one task must be implemented.” |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - These annotations are processed mainly at **compile-time**.
    - `@Deprecated` adds metadata visible via reflection.
    - `@Override` triggers compiler checks for correctness.
    - `@SafeVarargs` suppresses varargs generic warnings after ensuring safety.
    - `@FunctionalInterface` enforces SAM rules but does not affect runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - `@Override` prevents accidental method signature mistakes.
    - `@Deprecated` warns users and tools to avoid outdated APIs.
    - `@SuppressWarnings` should be used only when necessary.
    - `@SafeVarargs` ensures safe usage of varargs with generics.
    - `@FunctionalInterface` ensures lambda-compatibility and SAM compliance.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Annotation | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **@Override** | Ensures correct overriding |
    | **@Deprecated** | Marks API as outdated |
    | **@SuppressWarnings** | Suppresses specific compiler warnings |
    | **@SafeVarargs** | Declares varargs generic usage as safe |
    | **@FunctionalInterface** | Declares SAM interface for lambda use |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Built-in annotations enhance code reliability, maintainability, and clarity by guiding the compiler and developers through constraints, warnings, and metadata essential for modern Java development.
    
- **Custom Annotations**
    
    # **Custom Annotations**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Custom annotations are **user-defined metadata** that can be applied to Java program elements such as classes, methods, fields, and parameters.
    
    They behave like built-in annotations but allow developers to define their own metadata rules.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Add custom metadata for frameworks
    - Enable declarative programming (e.g., Spring, JPA)
    - Provide compile-time, class-load-time, or runtime processing
    - Improve readability and configuration flexibility
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Annotation Declaration**
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public @interface AnnotationName {
        // elements (parameters)
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public @interface Author {
        String name();
        String date();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    ```java
    @Author(name = "Ayush", date = "2025-11-24")
    public class MyClass { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Annotation declaration looks like an interface but uses `@interface`.
    - Compiler automatically extends `java.lang.annotation.Annotation`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Retention Policies**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Defines **how long** the annotation is retained.
    
    Specified using `@Retention` with `RetentionPolicy` enum.
    
    ### **Options**
    
    | Policy | Meaning | Common Use |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **SOURCE** | Available only in source code; discarded by compiler | `@Override`, `@SuppressWarnings` |
    | **CLASS** | Stored in .class file but not available at runtime | Default retention |
    | **RUNTIME** | Available at runtime via reflection | Annotations used by frameworks (Spring, JPA) |
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME)
    public @interface MyAnnotation { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    Controls annotation lifecycle based on processing needs.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Target Elements**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Specifies **where** an annotation can be applied, using `@Target`.
    
    ### **Possible Targets (ElementType enum)**
    
    | Target | Meaning |
    | --- | --- |
    | `TYPE` | Class, interface, enum |
    | `FIELD` | Field |
    | `METHOD` | Method |
    | `PARAMETER` | Method parameter |
    | `CONSTRUCTOR` | Constructor |
    | `LOCAL_VARIABLE` | Local variables |
    | `ANNOTATION_TYPE` | Another annotation |
    | `PACKAGE` | Package |
    | `TYPE_USE` | Any use of a type (Java 8+) |
    | `TYPE_PARAMETER` | Type parameter (Java 8+) |
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @Target({ElementType.TYPE, ElementType.METHOD})
    public @interface Audit { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Annotation Parameters (Elements)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Parameters inside annotations are called **elements**.
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - Elements must be:
        - Primitive types
        - String
        - Class literals (`Class<?>`)
        - Enums
        - Other annotations
        - Arrays of above types
    - Cannot have `null` as default value
    - Must specify a value unless default is defined
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Required Elements**
    
    ```java
    public @interface Info {
        String author();
        int version();
    }
    
    ```
    
    Usage:
    
    ```java
    @Info(author = "Ayush", version = 1)
    class Test {}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Default Values**
    
    ```java
    public @interface Config {
        String env() default "dev";
    }
    
    ```
    
    Usage:
    
    ```java
    @Config
    class App {}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Single Element Annotation (value)**
    
    If annotation contains only one element named `value`, it can be used shorthand.
    
    ```java
    public @interface Role {
        String value();
    }
    
    @Role("ADMIN")
    class User {}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Array Values**
    
    ```java
    public @interface Tags {
        String[] value();
    }
    
    @Tags({"java", "annotations"})
    class Post {}
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Combining Retention and Target**
    
    ### **Typical Annotation Declaration**
    
    ```java
    @Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME)
    @Target(ElementType.METHOD)
    public @interface LogExecutionTime {
        boolean enabled() default true;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Annotation declaration | Creating your own label type |
    | Retention policy | Deciding how long the label stays on the object |
    | Target elements | Deciding which objects can have the label |
    | Annotation parameters | Information written on the label |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior Notes**
    
    - Custom annotations become part of class metadata.
    - With `RUNTIME` retention, JVM makes annotations available via reflection.
    - Annotation parameters become **final members** of the generated annotation interface.
    - Annotations with `SOURCE` retention are removed during compilation.
    - No runtime overhead unless reflection-based processing is used.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Declare annotations using `@interface`.
    - Use `@Retention` to control lifecycle.
    - Use `@Target` to control where annotation applies.
    - Annotation elements must be constant expressions or defaults.
    - Avoid long field names when using `value()` element for single-value annotations.
    - Use runtime retention for framework-based custom annotations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Annotation Declaration** | Creates custom metadata tag |
    | **Retention Policy** | Controls lifespan of annotation |
    | **Target Elements** | Restricts where annotation can be used |
    | **Annotation Parameters** | Provide metadata values |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Custom annotations enable developers to embed metadata in Java programs, control retention and application locations, and define parameters for flexible configuration—forming the foundation for modern frameworks and declarative programming styles.
    
- **Reflection API**
    
    # **Reflection API**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **Reflection API** in Java allows programs to **inspect and manipulate** classes, fields, methods, and constructors **at runtime**, even if their structure was not known at compile time.
    
    Reflection is part of the `java.lang.reflect` package and enables dynamic operations such as:
    
    - Inspecting class structure
    - Creating objects dynamically
    - Accessing private members
    - Invoking methods at runtime
    - Building frameworks (Spring, Hibernate)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Enable runtime metadata inspection
    - Support dependency injection, serialization, ORMs
    - Facilitate tools such as IDEs and debuggers
    - Allow dynamic behavior configuration
    
    Reflection powers most modern Java frameworks.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Class Class**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `Class` class represents the metadata of any Java type (class, interface, array, enum).
    
    ### **Getting Class Objects**
    
    Several ways to obtain a `Class<?>` instance:
    
    ### **A. Using `.class` literal**
    
    ```java
    Class<String> c1 = String.class;
    
    ```
    
    ### **B. Using `getClass()`**
    
    ```java
    String s = "Java";
    Class<?> c2 = s.getClass();
    
    ```
    
    ### **C. Using `Class.forName()`**
    
    ```java
    Class<?> c3 = Class.forName("java.lang.String");
    
    ```
    
    Often used by frameworks for dynamic loading.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Class Metadata Access**
    
    Using a `Class<?>` object, you can retrieve:
    
    - Class name — `getName()`, `getSimpleName()`
    - Modifiers — `getModifiers()`
    - Package — `getPackage()`
    - Superclass — `getSuperclass()`
    - Interfaces — `getInterfaces()`
    - Constructors — `getConstructors()`, `getDeclaredConstructors()`
    - Methods — `getMethods()`, `getDeclaredMethods()`
    - Fields — `getFields()`, `getDeclaredFields()`
    - Annotations — `getAnnotations()`
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Accessing Fields, Methods, and Constructors**
    
    Reflection allows retrieving and modifying members even if they are **private** (with access override).
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Fields**
    
    ### **Getting Fields**
    
    ```java
    Field f = clazz.getDeclaredField("name");
    
    ```
    
    ### **Accessing Private Field**
    
    ```java
    f.setAccessible(true);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Getting and Setting Values**
    
    ```java
    Object value = f.get(obj);
    f.set(obj, "NewValue");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Methods**
    
    ### **Getting Method**
    
    ```java
    Method m = clazz.getDeclaredMethod("display", String.class);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Invoking Method**
    
    ```java
    m.setAccessible(true);
    m.invoke(obj, "Hello");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Constructors**
    
    ### **Getting Constructor**
    
    ```java
    Constructor<?> cons = clazz.getDeclaredConstructor(String.class);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Creating Instance Dynamically**
    
    ```java
    cons.setAccessible(true);
    Object instance = cons.newInstance("Value");
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Dynamic Method Invocation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Executing methods dynamically at runtime using reflection.
    
    ### **Steps**
    
    1. Get `Class<?>` object
    2. Get `Method` object
    3. Call `invoke()`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    Class<?> clazz = Class.forName("com.example.MyClass");
    Object obj = clazz.getDeclaredConstructor().newInstance();
    
    Method m = clazz.getMethod("sayHello", String.class);
    m.invoke(obj, "Ayush");
    
    ```
    
    ### **Return Values**
    
    `invoke()` returns:
    
    - Method result
    - Wrapped primitive types
    - `null` for void methods
    
    ---
    
    ### **Handling Exceptions**
    
    Reflection throws several checked exceptions like:
    
    - `ClassNotFoundException`
    - `NoSuchMethodException`
    - `IllegalAccessException`
    - `InvocationTargetException`
    - `InstantiationException`
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Limitations and Performance Considerations**
    
    Reflection is powerful but has notable drawbacks.
    
    ---
    
    ## **A. Performance Overhead**
    
    Reflection is **slower** than direct method calls because:
    
    - JVM cannot optimize reflective calls well
    - Access checks and metadata lookups occur at runtime
    - Boxing/unboxing often required
    
    Reflective invocation can be **5–20x slower**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **B. Security Restrictions**
    
    Reflection can break encapsulation:
    
    - Access private fields
    - Modify final fields
    - Invoke sensitive methods
    
    JVM security manager can restrict reflection in secure environments.
    
    ---
    
    ## **C. Compile-Time Type Safety Lost**
    
    Reflection bypasses compile-time checks:
    
    - Wrong method signature → runtime failure
    - Incorrect parameter type → `IllegalArgumentException`
    - Runtime errors instead of compile-time errors
    
    ---
    
    ## **D. Code Complexity and Maintainability Issues**
    
    Reflection makes code:
    
    - Harder to read and debug
    - More error-prone
    - Dependent on string-based class names
    
    ---
    
    ## **E. No Support for Generics at Runtime**
    
    Due to **type erasure**, reflection cannot retrieve generic type arguments of fields like:
    
    ```java
    List<String> list;
    
    ```
    
    Reflection sees only `List`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **F. Access Restrictions (Java 9+ Module System)**
    
    Modules can restrict reflective access unless explicitly opened.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Reflection | Inspecting and modifying a machine while it is running |
    | Dynamic invocation | Calling a function on-the-fly using a control panel |
    | Access override | Breaking open a sealed component |
    | Limitations | Slower operation and safety hazards |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Internal Behavior Notes**
    
    - Reflection relies on metadata inside `.class` files.
    - `Method.invoke()` uses native calls → slower.
    - JVM cannot inline reflective calls → no JIT optimization.
    - Illegal access is checked unless overridden with `setAccessible(true)`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Reflection enables powerful dynamic capabilities.
    - Accessing private members requires `setAccessible(true)`.
    - Slower performance and higher overhead than normal calls.
    - Use sparingly in business logic; ideal in frameworks/tools.
    - Risky due to broken encapsulation and runtime errors.
    - Restricted by Java Module System unless opened.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Class class** | Entry point to metadata |
    | **Field/Method/Constructor access** | Read/modify members dynamically |
    | **Dynamic invocation** | Invoke methods at runtime |
    | **Limitations** | Slower, unsafe, complex, restricted |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Reflection provides dynamic inspection and manipulation of classes at runtime but comes with performance, safety, and maintainability challenges. It should be used sparingly and typically only in frameworks, tools, or advanced utilities.
    
- **Advanced Annotation Features**
    
    # **Advanced Annotation Features**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Annotation Processors — Compile-time Processing**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Annotation processing is a mechanism where tools analyze and process annotations **during compilation** to generate code, configuration files, or perform validations.
    
    It uses the **Java Annotation Processing Tool (APT)** or `javax.annotation.processing` API.
    
    ### **Key Interfaces and Classes**
    
    - `Processor` (interface)
    - `AbstractProcessor` (common base class)
    - `RoundEnvironment`
    - `ProcessingEnvironment`
    - `@SupportedAnnotationTypes`
    - `@SupportedSourceVersion`
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Generate boilerplate code (e.g., Dagger, Lombok)
    - Validate annotations on classes/methods
    - Build meta-models
    - Enforce rules statically at compile time
    
    ### **Example — Simple Processor**
    
    ```java
    @SupportedAnnotationTypes("com.example.MyAnnotation")
    @SupportedSourceVersion(SourceVersion.RELEASE_17)
    public class MyProcessor extends AbstractProcessor {
        @Override
        public boolean process(Set<? extends TypeElement> annotations, RoundEnvironment env) {
            // Inspect elements annotated with @MyAnnotation
            return true;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Workflow**
    
    1. Compiler discovers annotation processor
    2. Processor receives annotated elements
    3. Processor generates files or reports errors
    4. Compiler continues with next round
    
    ### **Practical Uses**
    
    - Auto-generating builders (`@Builder` in Lombok)
    - Generating database mappers (MapStruct)
    - Dependency injection (Dagger/Hilt)
    - Serialization classes
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Repeatable Annotations (Java 8+)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Allows applying the **same annotation multiple times** on a single element.
    
    ### **Requirement**
    
    - A wrapper (container) annotation
    - Annotate main annotation with `@Repeatable(Container.class)`
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ### **Step 1 — Define Container**
    
    ```java
    @interface Roles {
        Role[] value();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Step 2 — Make Annotation Repeatable**
    
    ```java
    @Repeatable(Roles.class)
    @interface Role {
        String value();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    ```java
    @Role("ADMIN")
    @Role("USER")
    class Account { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - More expressive metadata
    - Avoid array-style annotation usage
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Type Annotations (Java 8+) and JSR 308**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Type annotations allow applying annotations **anywhere a type is used**, instead of only on declarations.
    
    Enabled by **JSR 308 (Type Annotations)**.
    
    ### **Where They Can Be Used**
    
    - Generic type
    - Constructor call
    - Cast
    - Implements clause
    - Throws clause
    - Type use in variables
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    ### **Annotating Generic Types**
    
    ```java
    List<@NotNull String> list;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Annotating Cast**
    
    ```java
    String s = (@NonEmpty String) value;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Annotating new Object**
    
    ```java
    new @Secured Service();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Practical Use**
    
    - Static analysis tools (Checker Framework)
    - Nullness checking (`@NonNull`, `@Nullable`)
    - Security frameworks
    - Concurrency analysis
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Annotation Inheritance Behavior**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `@Inherited` controls whether annotations on a superclass are inherited by subclasses.
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - Works **only on class-level annotations**
    - Works **only when annotation is annotated with `@Inherited`**
    - Does NOT work for methods, fields, parameters, constructors
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ### **Annotation**
    
    ```java
    @Inherited
    @interface Audit { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Usage**
    
    ```java
    @Audit
    class Parent { }
    
    class Child extends Parent { }
    
    ```
    
    Now:
    
    ```java
    Child.class.isAnnotationPresent(Audit.class); // true
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Limitations**
    
    - Does not apply to methods:
    
    ```java
    @Override annotations are NOT inherited
    
    ```
    
    - Subclasses must be actual classes; interfaces do not inherit annotations through `extends`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Runtime Annotation Retention — Practical Uses**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Annotations with `@Retention(RetentionPolicy.RUNTIME)` remain available to JVM via **reflection** during program execution.
    
    ### **Why Runtime Retention Is Useful**
    
    - Frameworks inspect classes dynamically
    - Dependency injection
    - Mapping libraries
    - Validation libraries
    - ORM (Hibernate JPA)
    - Custom processing at runtime
    
    ### **Examples in Practice**
    
    ### **A. Dependency Injection (Spring)**
    
    ```java
    @Component
    @Autowired
    @Service
    
    ```
    
    Spring scans these at runtime to build the application context.
    
    ### **B. ORMs (Hibernate/JPA)**
    
    ```java
    @Entity
    @Table(name = "users")
    @Column
    
    ```
    
    Processed at runtime to map Java classes to database tables.
    
    ### **C. JSON Serialization (Jackson)**
    
    ```java
    @JsonProperty("name")
    @JsonIgnore
    
    ```
    
    Used at runtime to convert objects to/from JSON.
    
    ### **D. Validation (Jakarta Validation)**
    
    ```java
    @NotNull
    @Min(1)
    @Pattern(...)
    
    ```
    
    Evaluated dynamically during validation.
    
    ### **Summary of Practical Uses**
    
    - Runtime scanning
    - Reflection-based mapping
    - Dynamic proxy generation
    - Serialization/deserialization
    - AOP (Aspect-Oriented Programming)
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | Annotation processor | A compiler-time inspector that builds tools or blueprints |
    | Repeatable annotation | Putting several labels of the same type on a package |
    | Type annotation | Labeling not just the box but the contents inside |
    | Annotation inheritance | A child inheriting tags from a parent class |
    | Runtime retention | Labels that stay on the product through shipping |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Notes**
    
    - Compile-time processors use the javac plug-in mechanism.
    - Repeatable annotations convert annotated elements into a container annotation behind the scenes.
    - Type annotations are stored with new `TYPE_USE` metadata in class files.
    - Runtime retention annotations are visible via reflection APIs.
    - Inherited annotations require explicit `@Inherited` and only work at the class level.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Compile-time processors enable code generation and static validation.
    - Repeatable annotations require a container annotation.
    - Type annotations enhance analysis tools and static checking.
    - Annotation inheritance is limited and needs `@Inherited`.
    - Runtime retention enables reflection-based frameworks.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Annotation Processors** | Compile-time validation & code generation |
    | **Repeatable Annotations** | Apply same annotation multiple times |
    | **Type Annotations (JSR 308)** | Annotate all type usages |
    | **Annotation Inheritance** | Only inherited on classes with `@Inherited` |
    | **Runtime Retention** | Supports reflection-based frameworks |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Advanced annotation features expand Java’s metadata model beyond simple tagging. With compile-time processors, type annotations, repeatability, inheritance rules, and runtime reflections, annotations become a central part of modern Java frameworks and toolchains.
    
- **Modern Reflection API**
    
    # **Modern Reflection API**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Overview**
    
    Modern Java introduced advanced reflection mechanisms that address the limitations of traditional reflection, improve performance, and provide safer, low-level programmatic access.
    
    Key technologies include:
    
    - **Method Handles** (`java.lang.invoke.MethodHandle`)
    - **VarHandles** (`java.lang.invoke.VarHandle`)
    - **Dynamic Proxies** (`java.lang.reflect.Proxy`)
    - **Module System Reflection Restrictions** (Java 9+)
    - **Reflection Enhancements for Records** (Java 16+)
    
    These provide faster, more expressive, and more secure reflective operations suitable for modern applications.
    
    ---
    
    # **2. Method Handles (Java 7+)**
    
    ## **2.1 Definition and Purpose**
    
    A **MethodHandle** is a typed, direct reference to a method, constructor, or field.
    
    It provides faster invocation than traditional reflection’s `Method.invoke()`, with performance comparable to regular method calls after JVM optimizations.
    
    Part of the `java.lang.invoke` package.
    
    ### **Advantages over Reflection**
    
    - Type-safe
    - Faster — JVM optimizes method handles (JIT inlining)
    - Enables dynamic language support on JVM (Invokedynamic)
    
    ---
    
    ## **2.2 Obtaining Method Handles**
    
    ### **A. Create Lookup**
    
    ```java
    MethodHandles.Lookup lookup = MethodHandles.lookup();
    
    ```
    
    ### **B. Access an Instance Method**
    
    ```java
    MethodHandle mh = lookup.findVirtual(String.class, "toUpperCase",
        MethodType.methodType(String.class));
    
    ```
    
    ### **C. Access a Static Method**
    
    ```java
    MethodHandle mh = lookup.findStatic(Math.class, "max",
        MethodType.methodType(int.class, int.class, int.class));
    
    ```
    
    ### **D. Access a Constructor**
    
    ```java
    MethodHandle mh = lookup.findConstructor(String.class,
        MethodType.methodType(void.class, byte[].class));
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2.3 Invoking Method Handles**
    
    ```java
    String result = (String) mh.invokeExact("hello");
    
    ```
    
    ### **Invocation Modes**
    
    - `invoke()` → flexible, slower
    - `invokeExact()` → strict types, faster
    
    ---
    
    ## **2.4 Use Cases**
    
    - High-performance dependency injection
    - Dynamic languages (JRuby, Nashorn)
    - Bytecode frameworks
    - Building flexible APIs
    
    ---
    
    # **3. VarHandle (Java 9+)**
    
    ## **3.1 Definition**
    
    A **VarHandle** represents a typed reference to a variable (field, array element, buffer element) and provides:
    
    - Safe, modern alternative to `sun.misc.Unsafe`
    - Atomic and volatile operations
    - Precise memory ordering
    
    Introduced to support Java’s new memory model and remove unsafe APIs.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3.2 Obtaining VarHandles**
    
    ### **Access a Field**
    
    ```java
    VarHandle vh = MethodHandles.lookup()
        .in(Point.class)
        .findVarHandle(Point.class, "x", int.class);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Access Array Elements**
    
    ```java
    VarHandle arrHandle = MethodHandles.arrayElementVarHandle(int[].class);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3.3 Operations**
    
    ### **A. Get and Set**
    
    ```java
    vh.set(pointObj, 10);
    int value = (int) vh.get(pointObj);
    
    ```
    
    ### **B. Atomic Operations**
    
    ```java
    vh.compareAndSet(pointObj, 0, 20);
    vh.getAndAdd(pointObj, 5);
    
    ```
    
    ### **C. Memory Ordering Variants**
    
    - `getAcquire()`
    - `setRelease()`
    - `compareAndExchange()`
    - `getOpaque()`
    
    ---
    
    ## **3.4 Use Cases**
    
    - Concurrency frameworks
    - High-performance data structures
    - Replacing Unsafe-based atomic operations
    
    ---
    
    # **4. Dynamic Proxy Creation and Usage**
    
    ## **4.1 Definition**
    
    Dynamic proxy allows creating classes **at runtime** that implement interfaces and route method calls through an `InvocationHandler`.
    
    Useful for:
    
    - AOP (aspect-oriented programming)
    - Logging
    - Security checks
    - Remote invocation
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.2 Creating a Dynamic Proxy**
    
    ### **Step 1 — Create InvocationHandler**
    
    ```java
    class MyHandler implements InvocationHandler {
        private Object target;
    
        MyHandler(Object target) { this.target = target; }
    
        @Override
        public Object invoke(Object proxy, Method m, Object[] args) throws Throwable {
            System.out.println("Before call");
            Object result = m.invoke(target, args);
            System.out.println("After call");
            return result;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Step 2 — Create Proxy Instance**
    
    ```java
    MyInterface proxy = (MyInterface) Proxy.newProxyInstance(
        MyInterface.class.getClassLoader(),
        new Class<?>[]{ MyInterface.class },
        new MyHandler(new MyClass())
    );
    
    ```
    
    ### **Calling Methods**
    
    ```java
    proxy.perform();  // Methods intercepted
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4.3 Limitations**
    
    - Works only with **interfaces**.
    - For classes, use libraries like CGLIB or ByteBuddy.
    
    ---
    
    # **5. Module System Reflection (Java 9+)**
    
    ## **5.1 Definition**
    
    Java 9’s Module System introduced **strong encapsulation**, which restricts reflection unless explicitly allowed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5.2 Access Rules**
    
    ### **A. Exporting Packages**
    
    Module must declare:
    
    ```java
    exports com.example.package;
    
    ```
    
    ### **B. Opening Packages for Reflection**
    
    ```java
    opens com.example.package;
    
    ```
    
    `opens` is required for frameworks like Spring and Hibernate.
    
    ### **C. Open Entire Module**
    
    ```java
    open module my.module { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5.3 Impact on Reflection**
    
    - `setAccessible(true)` is no longer enough
    - Illegal reflective access warnings occur
    - Frameworks must use `-add-opens` during runtime if module isn’t opened
    
    ---
    
    ## **5.4 Example of Restricted Access**
    
    Trying to reflectively access a private field without `opens` results in:
    
    ```
    java.lang.reflect.InaccessibleObjectException
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    # **6. Records Reflection Support**
    
    Java 16+ adds special reflection APIs for **records**.
    
    ### **6.1 Record-Specific Reflection Methods**
    
    Using `Class<?>`:
    
    ### **Check if Class Is a Record**
    
    ```java
    clazz.isRecord();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Retrieve Record Components**
    
    ```java
    RecordComponent[] components = clazz.getRecordComponents();
    
    ```
    
    ### **6.2 RecordComponent Information**
    
    Each component provides:
    
    - Name — `getName()`
    - Type — `getType()`
    - Annotations — `getAnnotations()`
    - Accessor method — `getAccessor()`
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    record Person(String name, int age) {}
    
    Class<?> c = Person.class;
    for (RecordComponent rc : c.getRecordComponents()) {
        System.out.println(rc.getName() + " : " + rc.getType());
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **6.3 Use Cases**
    
    - Serialization libraries
    - Framework metadata processing
    - Building DTO mappers
    
    ---
    
    # **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Feature | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | MethodHandle | A direct function pointer with strong type guarantees |
    | VarHandle | A safe tool to manipulate variables with memory ordering |
    | Dynamic proxy | Creating an actor who impersonates another role |
    | Module reflection | Locked rooms requiring explicit permission |
    | Record reflection | Reading structured labels on immutable data packets |
    
    ---
    
    # **8. JVM / Compiler Notes**
    
    - MethodHandles are optimized by JVM using *invokedynamic* and inlining.
    - VarHandles implement new memory semantics introduced in Java 9.
    - Dynamic proxies generate bytecode at runtime using ProxyGenerator.
    - Module system enforces access through `opens`, restricting deep reflection.
    - Record metadata is encoded in class file attributes (Record attribute).
    
    ---
    
    # **9. Important Points**
    
    - Method Handles are faster and type-safe compared to reflection.
    - VarHandles replace `Unsafe` for field and array operations.
    - Dynamic proxies implement interfaces dynamically at runtime.
    - Java 9+ module system restricts reflective access.
    - Records have enhanced reflective capabilities for structured data.
    
    ---
    
    # **10. Summary**
    
    | Feature | Purpose |
    | --- | --- |
    | **MethodHandle** | High-performance method and constructor access |
    | **VarHandle** | Safe and modern variable access with atomic ops |
    | **Dynamic Proxy** | Runtime proxy creation for interface-based AOP |
    | **Module Reflection Rules** | Strong encapsulation requiring explicit opens |
    | **Record Reflection** | Accessing structured metadata of record components |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Modern reflection APIs in Java provide fast, safe, and expressive access to methods, variables, records, and runtime-generated proxies, while the module system adds strong encapsulation and controlled reflective access.
    
- **Reflection Performance**
    
    # **Reflection Performance**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Performance Optimization Techniques**
    
    Reflection is powerful but slower than direct method calls because of:
    
    - Dynamic type resolution
    - Security checks
    - Indirection via `Method.invoke()`
    - Lack of JVM optimizations (no inlining)
    
    To minimize performance overhead, several optimization techniques can be used.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Avoid Repeated Lookups**
    
    Do NOT repeatedly call:
    
    ```java
    clazz.getMethod()
    clazz.getDeclaredField()
    clazz.getConstructors()
    
    ```
    
    Lookup reflection objects **once** and reuse them.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    private static final Method PRINT_METHOD;
    
    static {
        try {
            PRINT_METHOD = MyClass.class.getMethod("print");
        } catch (Exception e) {
            throw new RuntimeException(e);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Set Accessible Once**
    
    Calling:
    
    ```java
    method.setAccessible(true);
    
    ```
    
    removes further access checks and improves speed.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Minimize Boxing/Unboxing**
    
    Using reflection with primitives triggers boxing:
    
    ```java
    method.invoke(obj, 10); // int → Integer
    
    ```
    
    Use specialized APIs like **MethodHandle** or **VarHandle** to avoid this overhead.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Use Reflection Only When Necessary**
    
    Prefer:
    
    - Direct calls
    - Lambdas
    - Method references
    - Service loaders
    
    Reflection should not be used in tight loops.
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Use Method Handles for Hot Code Paths**
    
    `MethodHandle` is far faster than reflection after warm-up because JVM can inline them.
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Batch Metadata Retrieval**
    
    If processing many fields/methods, extract metadata (fields, constructors, methods) once and reuse them.
    
    ---
    
    ### **G. Prefer RUNTIME Retention Only When Needed**
    
    Annotations with runtime retention caused additional reflective scanning overhead; avoid when unnecessary.
    
    ---
    
    ### **H. Use Switches or Lookup Tables Instead of Reflective Routing**
    
    If reflective invocation is frequent, consider:
    
    - Enum-based dispatch
    - Lookup maps
    - Pre-registered callbacks
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Reflection vs Method Handle Benchmarks**
    
    Benchmarks vary based on JVM, CPU, and JIT warm-up, but patterns remain consistent.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Relative Performance**
    
    Typical ratios after warm-up (approximate):
    
    | Operation | Relative Cost |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Direct method call** | 1× (baseline) |
    | **MethodHandle.invokeExact()** | 1.1× – 2× |
    | **MethodHandle.invoke()** | 3× – 6× |
    | **Reflection (`Method.invoke()`)** | 10× – 30× |
    | **Reflection without `setAccessible(true)`** | 20× – 40× |
    
    ### **Key Observations**
    
    - **MethodHandle.invokeExact()** becomes nearly as fast as direct calls due to JIT inlining.
    - Regular `MethodHandle.invoke()` still faster than reflection.
    - Reflection is slowest because of:
        - Argument boxing/unboxing
        - Access checks
        - Varargs array creation
        - No JVM inlining
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Why Method Handles Are Faster**
    
    - Statically typed (especially `invokeExact`)
    - Can be inlined by JIT
    - Optimized via invokedynamic
    - No reflective security overhead after lookup
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Sample Benchmark Summary**
    
    (Representative pattern using JMH-style tests)
    
    | Technique | Avg Time per Call (ns) |
    | --- | --- |
    | Direct Call | ~5 ns |
    | MethodHandle.invokeExact() | ~7–15 ns |
    | MethodHandle.invoke() | ~20–50 ns |
    | Reflection invoke() | ~100–300 ns |
    
    **Conclusion:**
    
    For high-frequency invocation, prefer **MethodHandles** instead of reflection.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Caching Strategies for Reflection**
    
    Caching reflective objects greatly reduces overhead.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Cache Method, Field, Constructor Objects**
    
    Since reflection lookups are expensive, cache them in:
    
    - Static fields
    - HashMaps keyed by name + params
    - Concurrent HashMaps for thread-safety
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    private static final Map<String, Method> METHOD_CACHE = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    
    Method getCachedMethod(Class<?> cls, String name, Class<?>... params) {
        return METHOD_CACHE.computeIfAbsent(
            cls.getName() + "#" + name,
            k -> cls.getDeclaredMethod(name, params)
        );
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Cache Metadata for Frameworks**
    
    Frameworks like Spring, Jackson, and Hibernate heavily use caching for:
    
    - Field lists
    - Getter/setter maps
    - Annotation metadata
    - Constructor references
    
    This makes repeated runtime operations efficient.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Use MethodHandle Lookup Once and Reuse Handle**
    
    MethodHandle creation is expensive. Cache it:
    
    ```java
    private static final MethodHandle MH_PRINT;
    
    static {
        MH_PRINT = MethodHandles.lookup().findVirtual(MyClass.class, "print",
                     MethodType.methodType(void.class));
    }
    
    ```
    
    Invocation becomes much faster after caching.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Use VarHandles Caching for Repeated Field Access**
    
    VarHandles provide atomic and volatile-safe access with high performance.
    
    Cache them similarly:
    
    ```java
    static final VarHandle X_HANDLE =
        MethodHandles.lookup().findVarHandle(Point.class, "x", int.class);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **E. Avoid Creating New Proxy Instances Repeatedly**
    
    Dynamic proxies involve costly class generation. Reuse when possible.
    
    ---
    
    ### **F. Preprocess Annotation Metadata**
    
    Many frameworks compute annotation maps once during startup, then reuse.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Real-World Use Cases for Reflection Optimization**
    
    - **Web frameworks** (Spring, Quarkus)
    - **Serialization libraries** (Jackson, Gson)
    - **Dependency injection** containers
    - **ORMs** (Hibernate, JPA)
    - **RPC frameworks** (gRPC, RMI tools)
    - **Testing tools** (JUnit, Mockito)
    
    These systems rely on efficient reflection and heavy caching/MethodHandle usage.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Internals Behavior Notes**
    
    - Reflection bypasses JVM optimizations → slower.
    - `MethodHandle` can be inlined → faster.
    - VarHandle follows new memory model → safe and efficient.
    - Frequent reflective access without caching triggers GC pressure.
    - Access checks (`setAccessible(true)`) cost time unless disabled.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Important Points**
    
    - Always cache reflective lookups.
    - Use MethodHandles when performance matters.
    - Reflection is fine for occasional use but poor for tight loops.
    - VarHandles provide fast, safe field access.
    - Dynamic proxies should be reused to avoid repeated class generation.
    - Frameworks heavily optimize reflection in initialization phases.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Summary**
    
    | Topic | Key Point |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Performance optimizations** | Cache lookup results, avoid repeated reflection, precompute metadata |
    | **Reflection vs MethodHandle** | MethodHandles are significantly faster; `invokeExact()` approaches direct-call speed |
    | **Caching strategies** | Store Method/Field/Constructor handles; static caching reduces overhead drastically |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Reflection is inherently slower, but with caching, `setAccessible(true)`, and switching to MethodHandles or VarHandles for hot paths, Java reflection can become efficient enough for large-scale, modern frameworks.
